THE RED PAGE.
A Psalm of Life and Death .
11.
Surely before pale gleaimngs from the Dove
Eternal fell upon the elder place
Uf floods, beyond our fleeting time and space,
M3 r kingdom is ; and there in silent love
1 walk with quiet face.
There can I make my home, perchance for aye,
.cor in our sorrow and our festival
a j time ; yea. we are masons all,
And dealing with pure spirit do our day
.Before the judgment fall.
There nothing loud can enter; and the lands
Are hushed beneath a grave and mellow light;
And winds blow soft, forgetful of their might,
V oiceful of rest; and slow the hourly sands
.Lapse from reluctant height.
Faint, wistful, few, the things stored there have
braved
The chancings of the heart: there leaves unfold
J3y bronze and marble, by an abbey old,
And perfect words of human wisdom graved
In melancholy gold.
Mournful and fated man goes to his end
By vale and mound—so dreamed the ancient
Greek
Brooding upon the dark —torn by the beak
Of vulture, yet with soul that will not bend,
Lamenting, but not weak.
O deep the stillness of the quiet heart,
Purged by these solemn passions, set on high,
Antique in strength, and not unloth to die
So faith be kept, and asking but to part
With human tear and sigh.
Immortal forms of sun, and wind,*tnd dew,
Of purer spring and finer vintage flame,
Move in these legends, giving death and fame
And seats to heroes in the tranquil blue,
And calling each by name.
Neighboured with this deep life a forest land
Yields breath and murmur full of ancient days
To him, the forester, within the ways
Of man’s din; heart, with claims from that huge
band
Of kin in distant Mays.
O brother tree, redeem us yet again,
Remember old salvations, save us now
From dust and noise, O bathe the aching brow
In thy green quiet, let us lose our pain
Beneath thy long low bough.
Yea, let thy spell, thy spell of forest peace,
Be on our spirit: altars we will raise
Of unhewn stones, to mark the sunny days
With stealing shadow ; nor will ever cease
To hymn thee with our praise.
O let us sleep upon thy summer grass
With sweetest summer buds in leafy tent,
Or rushy ark with branches gnarly bent
For rafters-—sweet it were to dream and pass
The hours in fair content!
The gently chiding brook shall be our bell
In heart of greenest woodlands, far and deep ;
And we will dream in pauses of our sleep
Of fallen leaves afloat where waters well
And into streamlets weep.
The long sweet day is over: leaves are dead
And falling through the melancholy air
Of late and waning Autumn ; brown and bare
Are all thy branches in the golden-red
Of sunset faint and rare . . .
Looking like crag from swarded mountain side,
Or ancient cromlech, see my abbey loom :
Time’s golden stains are lost in silver gloom
Save where above the creeping twilight tide
Lingers a rosy bloom.
So old the stones they seem once more to be
Unlaboured, lost in moss, and flower, and blade,
And Nature s own ; but still though man may fade
And pass away, where he has bent the knee
An absence he has made.
Lost in this world of crumbling stone I love
To trace its sheafy pillars to the height
Where molten rubies kindle, or the light,
Yellow like autumn leaves, sleeps far above
A dimly glorious night.
The shadows gather as we softly go
On hearts unmindful now of love and hate,
And pore on emblems of their mouldered state,
Sepulchral—tears for beauty lying low
With all too brief a date !
Here on a marble leafage laid in gold
Unfaded, sleep in bronze the ancient brave,
From their dear land or from the alien wave
Returning home to earth in silent cold
Of uninvaded grave.
On this waste tomb I love to read the verge
Of Alianor reposing, jadis Reyne
De Augleterre, with blazon and rich stain
Armorial of old Castile, and dirge
Arid tears forlorn and vain.
Sorrow resigned and deep hangs over all
These regions of cave-darkness, vast and drear,
Worm-eaten crumbling holds of very fear
And dungeon light remote, dim, tragical
With life’s untimely bier.
So read the labels: ‘ ‘ killed in flower of age ”;
Bringing a breath of brine and smoke from far;
And crimson, golden glories crown their star,
Rich, puissant, superb ; with graven page—
Memorials of war.
But well I know the splendour of their day,
Embattled ancientry, is closing fast
To silence and forgetfulness : the past
Is for the slayers ; but the future’s way
With life and hope is vast.
The faces that shall look down from my wall
Are meditative, full of still repose,
And eloquent of conquest over foes
Within the soul, and brotherhood to all
Who feel for mortal woes.
And those that loiter in niy abhey aisles
Bv sun or taper-light shall never rake
In old oblivion and them awake
To glory, but their dues of tears and smiles
Shall to Love’s altar take ...
O mystery of love ! the happy time
As sweet with youth as is the spring-time flower,
And sweeter than the buds of summer bower,
Is in the very word ; beneath the lime
I dream the lated hour.
Her cheek pure rose, her eyes like violets
A-brirn with dew, her hair blown wild and free,
Remembering, my dead love comes to me,
Unsullied in the golden sun that sets
Behind our trysting tree.
How sad and sweet the smile; how faint the tone;
How beautiful the lips that breathe again
Of love and laughter, fragrant as this rain
Of scented blossom ; ah, that now alone
I seek my rest in pain !
When yon departed to return no more,
Save as from sleepy shades of twilight tomb,
A shade yourself, though very dear with bloom
Of love and death, I knelt without your door,
Then went into your room.
Your face was changed beneath Death’s awful
hand;
Haughty and pure it was like those of old
The Greek cut in his marble —yea, as cold ;
But as I gazed we were in love’s sweet land
Of immemorial gold.
That woodland day when first I told my love,
I said—you must remember yet; the stream
We drank from pure and bright as morning beam ;
The hazel nuts I shook from boughs above
Our heads; the quiet dream ;
The shy, sweet parting in the scented dusk;
Touch of the lips and brief embrace and sigh
For curfew bell from village church tower nigh,
Reminding us that Time leaves but the husk,
And even lovers die.
With your still face before me deeper grew
My passioning : I thought of those old plays
V\ here when Love cannot dream of love’s decays,
oteps in grim, envious Death —and saddest rue
Is all of Spring’s fond days ;
Of lips that rolled in dust because a queen
Had stooped and lost her way all for a kiss ;
Of those great hearts who in the moment’s bliss
Went proudly earthward, evermore unseen,
And lost in rude abyss.
O shadow of my love, go with me now,
Revisit all our walks where as we stray
For all unvalued kindness I will pay
With bitter tears ! Ah, pale the cheek, the brow—
The debt must be for aye !
Thou art returning to the unpathed deep ;
Thy look is on me ; O the yearning sting
Of that last look of farewell ! See, I bring
These blood-red tears from broken heart: I weep
Thee, and thy beauty’s spring.
In vain—my love can never come again ;
Then I must to her whither she has fled,
Explore the sullen regions of the dead, '
Wrest their dim secrets, learn in doubt and pain
The way that she did tread.
And, O my love, the day when we shall spring
From the pure cold of death to silent air
Of faintest, freshest sweetness, everywhere
To bathe in light unorbed on angel wing
With spirits debonair !
J.H.
Recently Published.
In The Story of Notation C. F. A Williams
gives an interesting summary of the development of
methods of visualising musical sounds. There are
numerous illustrations. “ The representation of musical
sounds in writing, called musical notation or simply
notation, from nota , a mark or sign, is a thin" so
commonplace, so universal, and apparently so simple
that we are apt to overlook the fact that our stave with
its variously shaped ‘ notes’ and all that goes to convey
a composer’s thoughts to the world, are the outcome of
centuries of experiments and gradual improvements.”
The development of “measured music” from plain-
song took some three centuries to accomplish “The
new notation and music were objected to by the Church
A hull was issued at Avignon about 1322 to suppress the
innovations under severe penalties. ‘ Some disciples of
the new school,’ it was said, ‘ while they apply them-
selves to measured times, introduce new notes prefer
their own to the ancient chant; the Church music is
sung in semibreves and minims, and is killed with little
notes They intersect the melodies with hoquets, slide
about in discant, and sometimes even load and crowd
the chants with triplet, and common motets.’ ”
' *
Horace Lorimer’s Amer. Letters from a Self-
Made Merchant to his Son deserve a good deal of the
vogue they have gained. The book phrases briskly the
shrewd, vulgar wisdom for which America has had a re-
putation since Benjamin Franklin, and which has not
essentially changed in 150 years. Indeed, Lorimer may
be said to play a modern fantasia on Poor llichard’s
Almanac. “ If you would be wealthy, think of saving as
well as getting The eye of a master will do more
work than both his hands’’—“Not to oversee
workmen is to leave them your purse open”
“Experience keeps a dear school, but fools will
learn in no other Pride that dines on
vanity, sups on contempt”—and so on : these are pre-
cisely the staple of the successful pork-packer’s advice
dressed and diluted. He adds a pithy commentary •— ’
“Ibelieve women weren’t cut out for business—at
least for the pork-packing business. I’ve had dealings
with a good many of them, first and last, and it’s been
my experience that when they’ve got a weak case they
&>Q(i their sex to it nnd win, <md that when they *ve £rot sl
strong case they subtract their sex from it and deal with
you harder than a man.”
There is nothing in this talk that two can live cheaper
than one. A good wife doubles a man’s expenses and
doubles his happiness, and that’s a pretty good invest-
ment if a fellow’s got the money to invest."
“Of course, clothes don’t make the man, but they
make all of him except his hands and face during business
hours, and that’s a pretty considerable area of the
human animal.”
“ While you are at it, there’s nothing like picking out
a good-looking wife, because even the handsomest
woman looks homely sometimes, and so you get a little
variety ; but a homely one can only look worse than
usual. Beauty’s only skin-deep, but that’s deep enough
to satisfy any reasonable man.”
“The easiest way in the world to make enemies is to
hire friends.”
“Remember that when you’re in the right you can
afford to keep your temper, and when you’re in the
wrong you can’t afford to lose it.”
“ Whenever any one offers to let you in on the ground
floor it’s a pretty safe rule to take the elevator to the
roof garden.”
“ Give most men a good listener and most women
enough note-paper and they ’ll tell all they know.”
“There are two things you never want to pay any
attention to—abuse and flattery. The first can’t harm
you, and the second can’t help you. Some men are like
yellow dogs—when you’re. coming toward them they’ll
jump up and try’ to lick your hands ; and when you ’re
walking away from them they’ll sneak up behind and
snap at your heels.”
That is the way Lorimer has built.
My Relations with Carlyle , the pamphlet left by
Froude for his posthumous'vindication, is interesting.
The reference to Carlyles alleged constitutional inca-
pacity for marriage is more clearly put. “Carlyle did
not know when he married what h s constitution was.
The morning after his wedding-day he tore to pieces the
flower-garden at Comely Hank in a fit of ungovernable
fury. The London life was a protracted tragedy.” Mrs.
Carlyle was jealous of Lady Ashburton. “Carlyle had
sat at the feet of the fine lady adoring and worshipping,
had made himself the plaything of her caprices, had
made Lady Ashburton the object of the same idolatrous
homage which he had once paid to herself.. . There are
in existence, or there were, masses of extravagant letters
of Carlyle’s to the great lady as ecstatic as Don Quixote’s
to Dulcinea.” “On the lady’s side.. . there was nothing
else but the imperious mistress, to whom Carlyle was a
passing amusement.”
Fronde’s story brings conviction of his good faith. Of
his good judgment one is less sure. He tried to shield
Carlyle rather than to blame him ; but he was too inti-
mate with C. and too sympathetic with Mrs. C. to be the
ideal biographer. The alleged garbling of MS. is probably
explained sufficiently by the illegible character of
Carlyles memoranda. It is clear that Froude was, if
anything, a too-devoted friend. Sir James Stephen, his
Co-executor, sided entirely with him. Carlyle “ did not
use you well,” writes Stephen to Froude. “He threw
upon you the responsibility of a decision which he ought
to have taken himself in a plain, umnistakeable way. . .
In order to present to the world a true picture of him as
he really was, you, well knowing what you were about,
stepped into a pillory in which you were charged with
treachery, violation of confidence, and every imaginable
base motive, when you were in fact guilty of no other
fault _ than that of practising Mr Carlyle’s great
doctrine that men ought to tell the truth.” Generally,
says Stephen of Froude in his life-relationship with
Carlyle, “ the most affectionate son could not have acted
better to a venerated father.”
Stephen’s opinion is final. Mrs. Alexander Carlyle,
to whom Froude voluntarily gave above jfilOOO—nearlv the
whole of the profits made on the MS. Reminiscences
bequeathed to him by Carlyle—appears in the light of
Stephen’s statement as a mean and greedy woman.

The French duel is fair game for English
“ humourists and a descriptive article in June Idler
is unusually piquant. It is alleged that Parisians of
savoir faire carry two card-cases. One contains cards
printed with a false name and address. When the
offending or offended party seems not to be worthy of
one’s steel, one pulls out this second card-case.
“Monsieur, behold my card I." one says, without tha
slightest risk.
“ And mine, monsieur !” replies the other.
Now, if the second card contains a false name and
address, each party can claim to his friends that the
other party feared to meet him ; and no harm is done.
Note—it is highly improper to foist the card of a friend
or acquaintance on the irate stranger.
A friend of mine tells me that this manoanvre has saved
his life fi fl y times at least. I saw it save him once—no,
twice. This youth, of mild and pleasing manners, has
the great misfortune—in a place like Paris—to he cross-
eyed. They look outward. So in caf6s, theatres and
restaurants, ladies think that he is making sheep’s eyes
at them. On this occasion there were two very pretty
ones, to his right and his left, and each accompanied. I
saw them flush up and begin to squirm and pout. My
cross-eyed friend observed it too. He moved his unluckv
head about and talked with me intently. All in vaiD.
The offended ladies whispered to their'escorts, and in
less time than three minutes they ro9e and struck
attitudes,
“ Messieurs, it is an error— ” I began.
My cross-eyed friend, however, gently waved me to sit
down and keep silence.
“If you refuse explanations, messieurs,” he said, “I
am at your orders. Here is my card ’’—giving one to each.
“Thanks!” accepting one from each. “I shall expect
to hear from you ! ”
With this he let me pay the drinks, and we walked off,
mild y and worthily, possessed of the respect and admira-
tion of our table-neighbours.
“ Now you’ve got two duels upon your hands !” I said
when we got out.
“No,” he replied, with a wide smile, “not I. Two
other men, perhaps, who wanted to fight duels with me
last week may get into a controversy with these two of
to-night. But I will not be in it.”
“How?”
“ I keep the cards of those who challenged me to-day
to give to those who challenge me to-morrow. Sol let
them fight it out among themselves.”
With this he packed away the two cards he had just
received, and we began to speak of other things.
In 1897, according to an Eng. estimate, Austra-
lasia imported from Great Britain £424,000 worth of books
and magazines ;in 1901, worth. Canada took
only £?67,000 in 1901 : because it is more convenient to
deal with the U.S.A., and because the Canadian Copy-
right Act strengthens the hands of the local author,
printer, and publisher.

.1 udging by the verses in another column, our
absent poet is getting closer to the Ideal of his quest—he
is “ burning,” as the children say at forfeits. Apparently
he has put himself imaginatively in the place of his
heroine, in order to utter the patsion he attributes to

Pictures can still bring money in England. On
Mav 23, ninety lots from the Vai'le collection and others
were sold at Christie’s for i?105,8i5—about A1170 per lot.
Four of Boucher’s panels were sold for 22,300gs. Diana
Reposing , by the same pain ter, measuring about 3ft. by 2ft.
6in., brought SOOOgs, Other prices were Rossetti’s Veronica
Veronese, 330ogs. ; Giorgio Cornaro, by Titian, 4500gs.;
Venus and Mart, 'by Veronese, 6000gs.; a Reynolds por-
trait for 2ipi)gs., and a Raeburn for 2600gs. On May 21
another Raeburn portrait, Sir John Sinclair, fetched
14,000gs. (Friends of local potterers had better keep this
paragraph from their sight, in case of bitter reflections.)
Another picture sold on May 21 was Gains-
borough’s portrait of Elizabeth Foster, Duchess of Devon-
shire, second wife of William, fifth Duke of Devonshire.
The price was 900gs. Printed that “ the Duchess gare
the portrait to a Mr. Foster, with instructions that he
was not to part with it during his lifetime. Foster left
for Australia in 1850, and some years after his death John
Foster, his son, brought it to England. For convenience
in moving, it was cut out from its frame and rolled up,
and thus it remained for many years. In 1808 John
Foster returned to Sydney, and, having by this time some
idea of the great value of the painting, he kept the canvas
roll in a long box, labelled ‘ Fishing rods.’ In spite ol
these precautions, the picture was stolen from Foster’s
sitting-room at his Sydney residence, and was only
recovered by great good fortune, as it had been deposited
in a shipping office by the thief, who meditated bringing
his booty to England. After the commotion caused by
the theft of Messrs. Agnew’s ‘ Duchess,’ Mr. Poster
brought the picture to London, and in England it has
since remained.”
The Lamp, a monthly literary maga., published
by Scribner’s, New York, prints this under heading “ The
Literary Querist” in its June number: — “751.—( 1) I
have heard that the largest book store in the world is in
Melbourne, Australia. Is this true?” “(1) We believe
it is true. The proprietor’s name is Cole, and he is said
to have a million volumes in stock. Not more than half
a dozen of the world’s greatest libraries exceed this,
though, of course, the book store has many copies of one
book.”

WHISTLER.
James Abbott McNeill Whistler, the eminent artist, died
20th July, 1903, aged 69.
Voices breathe upon the air
(Whistler’s dead)
Silence silence everywhere
' (Whistler’s dead)
No more quip, or jest, or scorn,
Epigram is left unsaid,
Lo ! the world is poorer grown : —
(Whistler’s dead.)
n.
Art is long and life is Short
Whistler’s dead!
Sure of hand as great in thought
Whistler’s dead 11
“ Harmonies of white and gold ”
“ Symphonies in green and red ”
Affinities with masters old
’Whistler’s dead 1!!
hi.
Let us bow our heads and mourn
f .Whistler’s dead -
Great, where many great are born
t Whistler’s dead

SONG OF BRUNELYS.
My lover is coming to-night !
0 ecstasy ! 0 bliss for me !
And someone has told the breeze, for it lingers
Soothing with delicate, tremulous fingers
So gently, so caressingly,
My throbbing heart, my poor hot head.
And the stars have a mellower, softer light,
Shining, “ Poor little human, we know what we know;
But be happy, since you will have it so...”
Ah, the stars have no lover, their youth is dead !
My lover will bring for betrothal a basket
And offer it to me, for I must not ask it,
With armlets and cuscus and teeth. . .
0 girls ! are you ready to beckon the boat on ?
Have you gathered dt-accenas and coleus and croton ?
Are you sure it’s becoming, my wreath ?
You fire-fiy sprites, hang out your lights
To deck his path to me !
You demons who dwell in the great conch shell,
Tell my lover I love him well,
Welcome my victor joyously !
My ear is close to the pulse of things,
And I hear the flutter of angels’ wings ;
He is my lover, I am his,
And the world is so beautiful!
BRUNELYS.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Camille.
I am sitting in her chamber, and the delicate
perfume
From her hair and person wafted, sweetly per-
meates the room.
And I gaze upon her features, and she lovelier
seems to me
Than proud Cleopatra sighing in the arms of
Antony.
Then think of how the tricks of Fate, that work
us woe and weal,
Place me here within the chamber of the beautiful
Camille.
And I know if I had met her ere our lives had
ruined been
I’d have loved her to Perdition —she is fairer than
a queen !
Very many lovers has she, but they do not love
her long:
Never one of all shall linger with her till Life’s
evensong.
For her sweetness and her beauties when all these
have passed away,
None shall know, and none shall care, who knew
her in her former day.
Sweet it is when Life grows dreary and is drawing
to a close—
Sweet it is to have another who the same dark
journey goes:
One who loved us in our glory, one who loved us
in our woe,
Hand in hand thro’Life to pass; and so unto the
grave to go.
But old age will never crown her, for she lives
this life too fast;
’Tis to her a burning fever, and it burns too fierce
to last.
Still, when Life’s grim game is over, and her sins
are sinned, she must
Just like to the purest maiden, only crumble into
dust!
And who knows of the Hereafter ? Men have only
blindly guessed:
And her being when it ends, shall end in nothing,
like the rest.
Thus, within her chamber sitting, were the
thoughts that came to me ;
But I banished the intruders when she kissed me
passionately ;
And she said, complaining, pouting with red lips
so beautiful:
“ All the time I’ve known you, darling, you have
never been so dull!”
S.A.
W. M‘DONALD.
The City of Sunset.
In a region faint and far
Over sapphire sea and bar, .
In a realm that lies between
Day’s unrest and nights serene—
There a mighty wizard dwells
Weaving wondrous magic spells.
At the waving of his wand,
In that shoreless dim beyond
You may see a city rise
Canopied by sunset skies,
Turret, tower, and battlement
In a maze of color blent.
High o’er keep and bastion
Float the banners of the sun ;
Moorish mosque and minaret
In a dream of azure set;
Fairer all than things which be
Seen in dream of fantasy.
And o’er gleaming roof and dome
Sun-waves break in crimson foam,
Surging ever upward, higher,
Over flashing fane and spire,
Decked with guerdon of the sea
Where all wondrous gem-stones be.
When through archway wide and vast
Day with all his hosts has passed,
On their cloud-ships sailing far
Over sea and under star,
Where the old immortal Gods
Nightly hold their high synods,
Then from off his high watch-tower
Swift descends the Wizard Hour,
And the leaguer’d hosts of Night,
All in shroud of sable dight,
Rise and gather to the fray
Like the ravens o’er their prey.
Shadowy, impalpable
As the fogs o’er fer.s that dwell,
Scaling keep and crumbling wall,
Wide beneath their banners’ pall:
Fallen fane and yielding tower—
Built and unbuilt in an hour !
TAS.
ALMA S.
Foreboding.
I dreamed I saw a figure, bowed, pass heedless
thro’ a careless throng
Of happy faces, youthful, bright, whose merry
laugh and joyous song
He chorused with a sigh.
For those he loved rejoiced no more, had changed
with years, or crossed the surge,
And stranger faces mocked his gaze with songs
that knelled like wailing dirge—
I dreamt that man was I.
The friends, I dreamed, that decked the fields of
Life like flowers in glowing May,
Had withered and a stranger bloom their loved
places filled to-day—
Filled—aye—but could not hold.
Alone — hopes shattered, lave,* a dream, past
buried with glad years long fled,
I wandered through a stranger world, unknown,
uncared for, worse than dead—
I dreamed that I was old.
vie.
J. McCLELLAND.

Low, Languid, and Dispirited.
The Vagaries of a Torpid Liver.
A Useful Lesson for Sufferers*
The Case of tyr. J. DECNAN.
(FOR THE “ REDFKRN TIMES.”)
Among the most important missions of
inquiry placed on record was that recently
made by our reporter to the home of Mr.
John Degnan, Garden-street, North Botany.
This is due as much to the magnitude of his
discoveries as to the interest of the incident
he elicited on inquiring.
“ Will you favour me with a complete
outline of the vicissitudes you successfully
combated ? ”
“ Right willingly,” answered Mr. Deg-
littlli as he offered the pressman a seat. Five
yeat's went by from the time my illness
started till it was finally conquered, and if
I did not have a bad time it was a caution.
Bear in mind, it was all through the torpid
state of my liver, and, believe me, I got
into such a very wretched condition that I
did not care whether I lived or not. Before
I was really aware w’hat disaster was be-
falling me I used to feel as though I had no
life in me, and was always low-spirited and
sleepy. There was only one thing that I
was fit for, and that was to lie down, but
when I did so 1 could not sleep, my mind
being actively engaged in going through a
multitude of unhappy thoughts which
seemed to be born of the melancholy condi-
tion I was in. What caused me considerable
uneasiness of mind was that my appetite
became poorer every day, and still more
anxietj' was engendered by the knowledge
that what food I did eat was of no benefit
to me, my ability to digest any kind of
eatables having vanished. In this way it
transpired that my strength wasted away
till it was often compulsory for me to knock
off work, although my employment at that
time was not of a heavy description.”
' *‘How did you feel after your meals ? ”
“Just as if I had swallowed so much
lead. Something like a heavy ball seemed
to form in my stomach, the pains arising
from which were simply horrible, aud from
ths stomach the heavy sensations travelled
up to my chest, thereby giving me extra-
ordinary difficulty in breathing. My head
ached to a degree which proved almost dis-
tracting, for I could not settle down to
read or do anything, and there was even a
swollen feeling in my eyes to annoy me.
Owing to a pricking sensation under my
shoulder-blades I used to think I had
caught a cold, but experience lias taught
me that that was a deranged liver symptom,
while another one, although more torment-
ing in its persistency, was a nasty aching
pain across the loins, w hich I felt very dis-
tinctly wheu .lioving about. The bitter
taste in my mouth was enough to turn any
man off his food, and the dirty coating on
my tongue used to make me feel sick. At
times, when exhausted naturo claimed a
few hours’rest, I fell asleep, but there was
no comfort in doing so, as the ugly dreams
I had simply beggar description. The flesh
gradually fell away from my frame till my
bones were quite visible, and my prospects
were anything but rosy, especially as my
head had long since been troublesome.
Without any ostensible reason it used to
start beating violently against my ribs, the j
sound it made being quite loud enough to
be beard several yards away. It seemed
more than likely that my heartwas diseased,
particularly so as those fits of palpitation
were usually accompanied by feelings of
faintness. The worry of mind and pain of
body at last succeeded in wearing out my
patience, for it took but little to make me
irritable and excited, after which I sank
into the old gloomy groove of misery till
something else occurred to upset me. Ner-
vousness was not the least of my ailments,
as my nerves twitched involuntarily when-
ever anything happened to disturb me, and
in spite of the medicines I had taken, they
still trembled at the least provocation.”
“ Where did you get your medicine from,
Mr. Degnan ?”
“ I refer to the medicines which my
i doctors prescribed for me, but which I
might as well have left untouched for all the
good they were. I was lways paying
money away to doctors, and never got any
value back for it; but now if I should
have the ill-luck to fall into bad health
again I would go straight for Clements
Tonic, as I know it to be the bist medicine
ever brought within the reach of suffering
humanity.”
“ That being your opinion, why did you
not take some earlier than you did ? ” -
“ Recause I had been suffering five years
before I was told about Clements Tonic,
and another reason was because I had de-
cided against taking any more medicine, as
I thought it was useless. But a relative
came upon the scene and told me how
Clements Tonic had cured his complaints,
which were like mine in nearly every detail;
so I promised to try it, and when I did so
it was the best day’s work I ever did, for
Clements Tonic piloted me to health. I
used to dread the very idea of taking any
food, but when I had been on Clements
Tonic a few days it was quite different, as I
was always the first to sit down to meals
and the last one to finish eating, and before
a week was over there was a great reduc-
tion in the severity of my pains in the
stomach. My food did not ferment and
turn to wind as in former years, but it
digested quite readily, and of course I got
the benefit of its nourishment. The weary
feelings which had darkened my days were
wafted away by the invigorating qualities
of Clements Tonic, which likewise buckled
together my shattered nerves and male
them firm and true. The greatest surprise
I ever got in my life was when I discovered
what a splendid medicine Clements Tonic
was for promoting healthful sleep, and I
say ‘healthful’ advisedly, for there was no
languidness or depression on awaking, such
as is experienced after taking sleeping
draughts or other such means for producing
slumber. The anxious misgivings which I
had borne in silence when my heart was
given to palpitating were not long in being
dispelled, for Clements Tonic made that
organ beat with the regularity of a clock,
and I can make the same statement in
regard to my internal functions generally,
for I never suffered from constipation after
taking a few bottles of Clements Tonic,
which relieved my chest pains and head-
aches very easily. It was pleasant to get
up and notice that my tongue was clean and
that the bitter taste had left my mouth,
but what was still more to be appreciated
was the fact that every ache and pain had
deserted my body, which was regaining its
weight from day to day.”
“ Are you sure about the completeness of
your cure ? ”
“lam quite positive that Clements Tonic
made a new man of me by giving me better
hoalth and more strength than I ever had
before. Not long ago I was helping to cut a
road through the mountains, about ten miles
from Nowra, and I found that I could do
the heaviest manual labour going without
being unduly fatigued, and, as Clements
Tonic gave me the health to do it, you can
publish my story in any way you want.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
i, John Dionan, ol Garden-street, North Botany,
in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare
that I have carefully read the annexed document,
consisting of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and is a
true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic; and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any wav my statements—
which I give voluntarily, without receiving any
payment; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Darlinghurst this twenty-
eighth day of February, one thousand nine hundred
and two, before me,
THOMAS MOORE. J.P.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 6, 1903.
Our Great Loss—as Shown in the
Arbitration Bill.
The loss of Kingston’s voice in the Federal
Cabinet had been scarcely announced to the Aus-
tralian people before they were made to feel how
serious that loss was. The various members of
the Australian Government had, from the begin-
ning, an almost equal chance of showing their
worth. The portfolios were nearly all of about
equal importance; there was no great, over-
shadowing position. In that equal competition
Kingston came easily first; indeed, with the
exception of George Turner, there was ho one
else “in the race.” William Lyne, carrying the
traditions of hisN.S.W. career into the Federal
sphere, promptly earned a reputation for seeking
support by small, squalid concessions, and figures
now as the politician who has most conspicu-
ously failed to rise to the Federal ideal. John
Forrest, in another way, has earned an equally
poor opinion ; his ideas are still of the Crown
Colony and convict-prison order. Postmaster
General Drake has been, on the whole, colorless ;
a worthy enough Minister, not rising to any very
marked extent above the State traditions of such an
office, but still doing good, respectable work. R. E.
O'Connor and Alfred Deakin have proved well
fitted for their positions ; the former in particular
lias shown himself a very master of politics, and
the latter has, in the piloting of the Judicature
Bill, relieved, with a gleam of true courage, a
dangerous tendency to over-suavity. Premier
Barton has been, almost in every respect, dis-
appointing, undecided, inert, feeble. George
Turner, without a very ample opportunity—the
Federation not being in the financial bog which
in Victoria gave his strong ability and splendid
honesty their scope—has added something to his
already great reputation; if he had been con-
fronted with the species of difficulty he loves he
would have added more. But Charles Kingston
has made a new reputation.
* * * *
The Customs portfolio entrusted to him was
not in itself the most promising from the stand-
point of public note. Foreign Affairs and
Home Affairs really gave better opportunities.
But Kingston made it the portfolio of the
Cabinet and himself the commanding personality.
And this without any tricks of vulgar advertise-
ment or any attempt to steal marches on his col-
leagues, but simply by showing in his every
speech that he was honestly Australian and
honestly Radical, that he neither made conces-
sions to Imperial favor nor to plutocratic influ- •
ences. It was made plain that, whoever else was
timorous to do right or inclined to bargain with
wrong, Kingston was firm for the people’s cause
all the time. He thus came to be looked upon as
the backbone of the Ministry’s democracy.
That that estimate was correct is now shown.
As soon as he retired, the difference in the
feeling of the Cabinet was made apparent. The
Industrial Arbitration Bill, asdrafted by Kingston,
provided for a court of justice to try any industrial
cause affecting the Commonwealth which the
Constitution allowed it to judge. He made no
pxemption in favor of English shipowners trading
on our coasts; for he had no fear of Joseph
Chamberlain. He made uq exception in favor
of State Governments; for lie had no fear of
Irvine or Philp or Bent. The Industrial Arbi-
tration Bill, as brought forward now by Alfred
Deakin, specially exempts seamen and State em-
ployees and employees of any public authority
constituted under the Commonwealth or State
(such as a Harbor Trust, a Water Board, or a
Municipal Council) from its jurisdiction. It has
been so emasculated in deference to Chamberlain
and Irvine, that it is hardly an Industrial Arbi-
tration Bill at all. A N.S.W. measure of the
same character, specifically excluding from its
benefits all miners and all shearers, would be
omewhat analogous.
* * * *
. As to the exemption of seamen from the measure,
it is not yet possible to judge clearly. Deakin
seems to fear that to include seamen in the Bill
would cause it to be reserved for the Royal assent
and thereby give rise to delay ; and there is a more
or less distinct understanding that in some other
way the protection, due to the Australian sailor
and shipowner, will be conferred. If the Attorney-
General is sincere in his belief, an easy way out of
the difficulty presents itself—to bring down at the
same time as the main Arbitration Bill a short
one-clause Bill extending all its benefits to sailors.
If this second Bill is reserved for the Royal
assent, no delay will be caused to the main
measure. The exclusion of State employees from
the scope of the measure is a much more serious
matter, for it is a deliberate decision, in a matter
of policy, to pander to the anti-Federal “State-
rights ” cranks, and to the Bent-Irvine incen-
diaries in Victoria, and, in deference to their
notions, _ to make the whole scheme of Federal
arbitration practically a nullity.
• * * *
It is to settle possible disputes between State
Governments and their employees that a Federal
Arbitration Act is most urgently needed. No
other disputes are as dangerous to the peace of
the Commonwealth, no other could be so disas-
trous to its interests and its revenues. A railway
strike, such as that recently brought to a lame
conclusion in Victoria, would, if persisted in for
any length of time, paralyse inter-State trade and
disorganise the whole mail service of the Common-
wealth, besides causing unimaginable public and
private loss. The Victorian men, after being
goaded into a strike which was, on their part,
absolutely justifiable, were bamboozled into a
surrender which was a complete sacrifice of their
interests. Developments might easily have been
otherwise. The men might have stood out with
as much resolution as they went out, and a situa-
tion would then have been created in which
even the laziest Australian Premier imagin-
able would have found himself forced to inter-
ference. _ In a future trouble of that sort
—and, given a corrupt press, and men of
the Bent-Irvine stamp, future troubles are
always possible—the course of events might lead
to bitter happenings. The Commonwealth—
consisting of States, three of which have already
affirmed that industrial peace must be kept as
well as civil peace, and the power taken from any
brutal ruffian of an employer or criminally feather-
headed agitator to stop industries and bring about
misery and starvation and loss of capital—being
itself about to affirm the same sensible prin-
ciple, puts itself in a position worthy only
of the contemptible Yes-No Reid when
it says that as regards industrial differences
in private employment it will substitute
justice for violence, but as regards State employ-
ment it will allow the old anarchical conditions to
continue. The State employer may be a brutal
bully and a sweating, swindling tyrant; the State
employee may be a hot-headed fool or a cold-
blooded conspirator to “corner” his country at a
crisis—without interference. It is an utterly
absurd and illogical position, and all the wreaths
of oratory that Alfred Deakin may weave
around it cannot conceal its folly and its wicked-
ness. Victorian experience has shown that a
Government may act towards its employees as a
brutal Degree, and, practically disfranchising
them, bar any hope of a State remedy.
N.S.W. experience has shown—at Fitzroy
Dock, for instance—that workmen may rob
the State in various ways with official con-
currence, and it is quite easy to imagine
that, if deprived of wrongful privileges, they
or others might, with a strike-threat, squeeze
the Government into renewing them. The States
are the largest employers of labor in the Com-
monwealth ; their employees have the working of
the most vitally necessary public services. A
system of arbitration is thus most of all needed
to prevent the possibility of their striking. The
N.S.W. Legislature admits that principle. The
Australian Parliament can only omit it by con-
fessing itself craven.
# * *
It does not make the position any better
that the Australian Government has avowedly
taken its present paralytic attitude in response to
a threat from Irvine. No other State politician
hinted at a protest against Kingston’s Bill. It is
the man who, if the Australian Government had
done its duty, would by now have been branded
as a law-breaker and an enemy of the Constitu-
tion, the man who most of all fears the intruding
arm of Justice in the relations between him and
the State servants so unhappy as to be in his
power, to whom Alfred Deakin bows down in
grovelling submission. A State Government, if it
intends to be fair and reasonable in the treatment
of its employees, can have no more reason to
fear a court of industrial justice than a fair
private employer. It is only an administration
which has the wish to wrong that fears justice.
Irvine’s desire to settle any disputes he may pro-
mote with Victorian State employees by Bent
bluster, by newspaper lying, and by callous
tyranny, is the worst of all reasons which an Aus-
tralian Minister could advance for refusing to
apply the same rules of free and fair trial of
industrial disputes to State as to private
employers. It is a poor sort of justice which
trembles before the powerful and resolves to limit
its decisions to the weak. The Australian Govern-
ment should not be at the beck and call of any
State. To its justice a State should be in
exactly the same position as a private litigant,
and entitled to as much consideration—no more
and no less.. That is really the main principle of
the Australian Constitution, and any concession
made by the. Australian Government to a State as
against a citizen is contrary to the spirit of that
Constitution. To exercise complete control over
aiT matters of Australian interest—without refer-
ence to the views of the State Governments, and
with reference only to the will of the Aus-
tralian people; to hold even the scales of
justice, in all matters on which it has
authority, between State and State, and State and
individual; that is the lofty purpose of the Com-
monwealth deed of government. That purpose
Alfred Deakin departs from at the dictation of
a small subordinate potentate. A railway strike
in South Australia might wreck the Broken Hill
field and the Barrier people would be left helpless
to protest. A Victorian railway strike might cut
the Commonwealth in two and give it over to
armed disorder. The stoppage by an industrial
dispute of the water and sewerage services in one
of the great cities might bring a disastrous epi-
demictothewholecontinent. Againstsuch dangers
common-sense demands protection, and the Aus-
tralian Constitution—designed to impose and
maintain equal law throughout the Common-
wealth in all matters of national importance—
authorises the granting of protection. On
the other side are Irvine and Bent. The
Australian Ministry prefers to listen to them.
At their orders it deprives an immense
body of citizens of the rights, which the
Constitution professes to grant to all Aus-
tralians, and it makes of the Commonwealth’s
dignity a cloth for Irvine’s hands and a mat for
Bent’s feet. Truly Kingston’s voice is no longer
in the counsels of the country.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
N.S.W.’s Influx of Population.
The fashion in which a section of the Australian
press invents a great national calamity when a
great national calamity is required to uphold its
particular views or to grind its particular axe,
is so magnificent in its impudence as almost
to command admiration. The daily press
travels on the popular ignorance and on the
fact that 99 per cent, of the people never think
much nor investigate at all for themselves, but
take for granted the thing they have read some-
where, if only they have read it somewhere with
sufficient frequency and heard it somewhere
with sufficient loudness. Lately, it occurred
to the Sydney Foreign Importers' press that it
would be a good thing to invent a great and
disastrous exodus of population from New South
Wales, as the natural and inevitable result of
Federation and of even the small measure of
Protection that the State now enjoys, and to show
that no such disaster was ever heard of in the
good old days of Separatism and Shoddy. So it
just invented the exodus (there was a real enough
outflow of people as regards some States, but it
was necessary to invent one for N.S.W. to show
the grief that fell on the land as soon as
M‘Millan’s calico was discouraged). Straight-
way there appeared announcements—so confident
and circumstantial that they took in even intelli-
gent people—about the general and unanimous
streaking away of N.S.W.’s population to better
lands where the blare and curse of State Socialism
and Protection were not. The Sydney Telegraph
struck the top note with the following announce-
ment, which only a very unscrupulous rag or a
miserably mismanaged and misconducted one
could be guilty of:
Never before in the history of New South Wales has
the emigration exceeded the influx of people.
That is the imaginary fact, mentioned to lend
weight to the imaginary calamity that was men-
tioned to show how disastrous it was to dis-
courage M‘Millan’s imported calico. Yet the
real facts as regards past years are accessible
to everybody who cares to consult Coghlan’s
official statistics, and Coghlan’s works are cheap
enough to be within the reach of most citizens :
N.S.W.’s N.S.W.’s
Loss by Excess Gain by Excess
of Departures. of Arrivals.
1893 1560 ..
1894 .. 919
1895 840 ..
1898 8967 ..
1897 173 ..
1898 .. 1789
1899 390
1900 1518 ..
1901 6744 ..
1902 .. 4561
1903 (£ year to June).. . . 3170
A great deal might be said about the possible
share that the loan disease has in causing the pre-
sent influx of population into N.S.W., but it isn’t
the cause, only the fact itself, that is at present
under consideration. The fact is that in six out
of the last nine years of the parochial system
N.S.W. lost population ; that in four out of the
six years which constituted the Reid Importers’
tariff period N.S.W. was regarded as a good place
to get out of; and that it was only with the advent
of Federation and at least partial Protection that
the flight was stayed. And the Sydney Tele-
graph joyfully invents an exodus —allegedly the
first on the State’s record—just when the exodus
which had been going on for years under the
Telegraph’s pet policy had been stopped; and
the Evening Noose has a frantic leading article
about the flight and sets it down to “socialistic
legislation ”; and Chambers of Commerce and
Buch-like buzzing aggregations have wept bitterly
about the imaginary calamity which they set down
to the Labor party, and have utterly destroyed each
other’s shirt-fronts with their tears. And all the
time, so far as N.S.W. is concerned, it was all a
lie —just a common tin lie at two a penny, with a
reduction on taking a quantity.
♦
The Chamber of Horrors.
The President of the Sydney Chamber of
Horrors, or Commerce, or something, recency
addressed that august body on what should be
done in order that New South Wales might be
saved. His great central idea was unique in its
imbecility; the Chamber of Horrors person
reckons that what is wanted is Assisted Immi-
gration. Just at present New South Wales
fails badly to find work for the people it has got
already, and the Chamber of Horrors proposes to
repair this by bringing in more people —heaps
more people, and these in the mam poor people
who must promptly find work in a workless land,
or else starve. As a general thing, thousands of
persons are leaving New South Wales annually be-
cause they are glad to get away; the only thing
that prevents a loss of population is the influx of
persons who are still more glad to get away from
some other place. If N.S.W. were made a
place worth living in, a lot of the people who
are going away would remain, and a lot more
people would come, and the State would have all
the immigration it wants. It is merely a question
of having the work to offer the people and they
will do the rest; but the Chamber of Horrors idea
is, apparently, that we should get the people, and
never mind about providing the work—that we
should buy population with loan money, and then
find it relief work at stonebreaking or something
of the sort when it arrives. This sort of imbecility
gives one a low opinion of the Chamber of
Horrors. The work problem is the only one to be
solved ; once that is done the other problems will
solve themselves. Seeing that the bottom has
been persistently falling, for ten years or more,
out of the great “ natural ” industry of rearing the
foreign sheep amid unsuitable surroundings, and
that the great “natural” industry of rearing the
foreign wheat plant on an arid soil isn’t going well,
and that the mineral industry is fading somewhat,
the only line in which there is much prospect of em-
ployment for a great influx of population, or even
for the population we have, is in manufactures.
And we can’t manufacture a great deal more than
we do without importing a great deal less; and
that involves dealing a deadly blow at the para-
sitical old importers of the Chamber of Horrors.
They live on imports; it is because N.S.W. im-
ports so ridiculous an amount that there is so much
commerce in N.S.W. And because it imports
so much it manufactures little. Because it manu-
factures little its people are largely out of work,
and living on relief jobs propped up by loans,
and it is with the wages they receive out of these
loans that they buy the imported goods;
therefore, the importing business, to a great
extent, rests on loans. And because there is so
little genuine work there is but little increase of
population. The Chamber of Commerce, or
whatever it calls itself, can do a little to repair
the evil if it likes —it can go and hang itself. But
it shouldn’t pretend to seek for means to alleviate
the trouble. It is itself the outward sign and
symbol of the system that causes the trouble ; it
is the emblem of the rottenness that has overtaken
the State ; it lives on the country’s sufferings and
misfortunes, like a parasite growing on dead men’s
bones.. If N.S.W. will throw all its weight, as
the principal member of the Commonwealth, in
favor of getting such a heavy tariff (a tariff such
as Australia has never yet possessed) that the
State’s imports from outside the Commonwealth
will be diminished one-half, then there will be
nearly £9,000,000 worth more goods to be manufac-
tured annually in N.S.W. alone; there will be that
amount more employment and more wages, and
that amount more inducement for immigrants.
Incidently, also, a good many members of the
Chamber of Horrors will probably go to the
Benevolent Asylum, but that can’t be helped.
The essential beginnings of reform consist in
doing great damage to that august institution.
The Absurdity of Being' Partially
Honest.
Queensland Labor party has been advised by one
of its. prominent State members to “sink the
fiscal issue at the forthcoming Federal elections,”
and make the question at the polls a straight one
of “ Labor v. anti-Labor.” The Bulletin sin-
cerely hopes that the Queensland workers, who
constitute one of the strongest squadrons of Aus-
tralian Radicalism, will follow no such foolish
advice. It would be akin to the old, old
folly of seeking the Kingdom of Heaven
by self-mutilation. The Labor parties in
Australia, beginning with a somewhat nar-
row programme, and a more or less worthy
class purpose, have, in some cases consciously,
in others unconsciously, developed towards being
sound democratic organisations. In different
States the development has progressed to different
stages. In none is it complete, but taking
them in the aggregate, the various bodies which
make up the Australian Labor party represent
what is most hopeful in Australian democracy. It
is the party of a White Australia, of Federal
economy without Federal Kyabramming, of a
national outlook in methods of defence and of pro-
gressive industrial legislation. But it still lacks a
definite financial programme. In the matter of
the tariff it was divided, when by being united it
might have put the Australian Government in the
position of being able to found an old-age-pensions
scheme and the Australian worker of having more
avenues of employment open to him before he is
qualified for a pension. Now, just when the
resignation of Kingston from the Cabinet
gives an unequalled opportunity to the Labor
part}' to attract to itself a man of the
type of Ballanch, who could lend to it
the administrative experience which it as
yet lacks, just when the possibility of a
Kingston-Watson Ministry is not at all remote,
the Queensland workers are asked to adopt an
attitude which would make their contribution to
the Labor party practically useless, for no party
which hasn’t a financial policy can make a
Government, or be of much assistance in making
one. The Labor party will be a traitor to
itself and unjust to the Commonwealth if
it shackles itself with conditions which bar
it from undertaking the government of the
country. Let it be as positive and as progressive
on the financial question as on other matters,
The Naval Tribute.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The lands of Earth are far and wide,
Tho men of Earth are black and pied,
And white and brown and various-eyed
And filled with greed unholy;
But most can raise a kind of gun
And fight awhile before they run,
But We —we take th’ immortal bun
For mortals meek and lowly.
We may not fight—we only groan,
We are a worm that weeps alone;
Oh, blame us not because we own
No sturdy race of yeomen ;
We’ll send along to Ikey Mo —
Who’ll send along to Bunt and Co.—
“ Collect your vessels, John, and go
To save them from their foemen ! ”
Let not your righteous anger burn,
Oh, do not spit on us and spurn—
We are a thing that will not turn
Or otherwise torment you ;
Suppose you smite our dexter cheek
We turn the left in manner meek,
We are the folk that eat the leek—
Oh, will not that content you ?
When we were full of sap and young,
Our Jingo journals always sung, *
With throat of brass and leather lung,
“ Oh ! Trust unto your Mother, dear;
You do not need to build a ship;
She’ll smite your foes upon the hip,
She hath a noble upper lip,
She is above all other dear.”
And so, while other peoples fought,
And kicked as healthy peoples ought,
We learnt the lesson we were taught,
And ran a race reptilian ;
And when they spoke of danger near
We cried, “ Look out! our mother dear
Has guns and boats and British beer
And Tommies by the million !”
We may not fight—we only groan—
, We are a worm that weeps alone;
Oh, blame us not because we own
No sturdy race of yeomen;
We’ll send along to Ikey Mo —
Who’ll send along to Bull and Co.—
“ Collect your vessels, John, and go
To save ’em from their foemen ! ”
PHILANDER FLAM.


declaring for a White Australia, a white-wage
Australia, and a debt-free Australia, and the next
elections will put it on the threshold of power. It
cannot sink the fiscal question unless it proposes
to be all its life a wobblesome hanger-on of other
parties. It must take the responsibility of saying
how it will raise, as well as how it will spend,
revenue. It must, if it wishes to enforce a
minimum wage, factories legislation, and fair
hours of labor, also decide to take such steps as
will effectively protect the manufacturers, forced
to grant those conditions, against rivals
who are not. It must also declare for a
release of the land from its bondage to the foreign
money-lender, since the reckless pledging of the
country’s assets to the Hebrews of afar land is
not only an economic evil in the present but in-
volves a grave danger that political bondage
will follow on financial slavery. In short, the
Australian Labor party must be a party of
stringent Protection and of Financial Honesty.
If it attempts to shirk a decision on those two
points it must either be content to always take
its meals in the kitchen, as a menial party not fit
to be in the dining-room; or it must frankly join
in with the Dry Dog crowd and seek power as a
party which can swindle and fake and lie,
and let things slide just as well as any
body of old - established politicians. There
are no possibilities in a Reform Party which
sinks the fiscal issue. A wrong decision on that
fiscal issue will bar it from attempting almost all
genuine reforms, and the fiscal issue is the vital
matter on which reform is needed. There is no
sort of use in a bank teller going into the lofty
virtue line of business with a declaration of strict
honesty on all points except the mishandling of
his bank’s funds, and on that point keeping an
“open mind.” There is no use in a Treasurer
having all the virtues except a knowledge of
finance; or a Government having every good
quality and no money.

The Flames of Smithfield.
The Bulletin receives from “G.N.” another
wild letter on the above subject:
A few reasons why the sectarian cry, from a Protestant
point of view, is not at present an unnatural one :
(1) On the most valuable sites in every town of any size
in N.S.W. you see, flaunting their heads above all
others, magnificent buildings devoted solely to Roman
Catholicism—convents, training colleges for priests, or
schools, where the principal thing taught is how to
become a R.C.
(2) N.S.W. statistics as to the spread of R.C.-ism may
turn out quite differently to Australasian ones.
(3) It is the most intolerant religion in the world, and
divides families, friends, and communities as no other
denomination does ; even innocent little children attend-
ing the convent schools soon learn to look askance at
Protestant children. An intolerant religion having
ascendancy in a country is worse than civil war. No
country has ever prospered under such conditions.
(4) Roman Catholics accept help and donations towards
the furtherance of their objects from other denomina-
tions, but never assist in return.
(5) Protestants have not forgotten a certain reign dur-
ing which R.C.-ism dominated England, and, naturally
do not want to run any risks.
(6) Banish the R.C. religion, and all others might meet
on some common ground ; while the ages last the Roman
Catholics will not budge an inch.
(7) It is the realising (as they are beginning to do) of
the magnificent organisation of this church that never
slumbers nor sleeps, its vast resources and its innumer-
able ramifications, and having in view several of the
imperious demands made lately by its head in N.S.W.,
that has made the matter an important one—even from a
strictly temporal point of view—to every Protestant.
I am not an Orangeman, and never will be, but (desiring
the welfare of my adopted country) for the reasons given
above, my vote goes every time to a Protestant or none
at all.
The reply, especially as the subject is a rather
weary one, may be compressed into very few
words:
(1) The Roman Catholics don’t hold “ the most valuable
sites in every town of any size ” in N.S.W. Take the 100
most valuable sites in Sydney (value estimated by selling
price per foot), and Catholic institutions probably don’t
occupy one of them The two most valuable church sites
in Sydney are held by Anglicans.
(2) In 1871 Roman Catholics numbered 293 out of every
1000 persons in N.S.W. In 1900 they numbered only 260
out of every 1000. Therefore, the yarn that old Tory
Simon Fraser and his species should be elected to Parlia-
ment on account of the “ alarming spread of Romanism "
is an attempt to get office under false pretences.
(3) As to intolerance, the Greek Church is the great
“ Christian ” persecutor nowadays. “The most intolerant
religion in the world ” is often rather hard to define.
(4) The story of Queen Mart’s reign in England is
pretty old now. Since then, there has been the Anglican
domination in England when Dissenters were branded
on the forehead, and their noses slit, and their ears
cut off ; also the Presbyterian domination in Scotland
when the “cashielaws” were still in vogue (described as
an iron receptacle in which the feet and legs of the
prisoner were confined, and a fire built round them); like-
wise the dreadful years of torture and other horrors
during the Anglican persecutions in Scotland. It was
only under the Protestant regime that torture was
legalised in Ireland, and the scalping of Catholics with
the pitch cap was practised till not much over 100 years
ago. Since then most religions have become civilised,
by compulsion of the civil power. The flames
of Smithfield are as extinct in Catholic as in Pro-
testant countries (except that people are occasionally
burned in the United States), and Dill Macky is no more
likely’ to go back to the ways of his evil and horrible an-
cestors thanany body else is. Even Bishop Smith isn’t likely
to thumbscrew any Presbyterian nowadays, and the
flogging of Baptists by the English Church is adjourned.
(5) The political question has nothing to do with religion.
It is simply this : A handful of disastrous Tories, whose
political supremacy would set back democratic progress
for many years, and who have no hope on
their political merits, are trying to get into power by
babbling about the burning of Protestants over 300 years
ago. And they' represent churches whose record of
bloodshed, tyranny, persecution, torture and intolerance
is about as bad as anyone could ask for. The 28 years’
persecution carried but by the Anglican Church in
Scotland broke all records of Catholicism in that country
anyhow. The moral is that the churches, when they get
control of the State, are all pretty much alike, and the
politician who travels on any religious ticket—Roman
Catholic, Anglican, Presbyterian or any other—should be
avoided as if he were a sepent. He generally is a
serpent.

Australia’s Nobbled Sou—Once More.
Premier Barton’s explanation to Joseph Cham-
berlain that the Australian Postal Act does not
prevent Australian mails being sent by nigger-
manned ships —that though no postal contract can
be made with nigger-labor companies, yet the
mails can be sent without contract at so much per
pound—is put in such a way as to be extremely
disquieting. Barton, shuddering before the
basilisk eye of Chamberlain, assures him that he
has taken special advice from the law officers of
the Crown, and that there is nothing in the Postal
Act to prevent the Commonwealth paying mail
Spundage to a vessel “manned by Japanese.” And
larton says this in such a way as to give the
impression that his real message is: “It is all
right, old man; the Act will be a dead letter.”
Tne Postal Act, as passed by Parliament, vetoed
the payment of mail subsidy by the Australian
Government to any vessel not employing white
labor exclusively. But it did not precisely say
that the Government should not pay poundage to
colored labor vessels for the carriage of mails.
Perhaps it did not contemplate the possibility of
an Administration taking the police-court attor-
ney point that, whilst barred from paying a
colored-labor employer for maxi carnage by means
of a subsidy, it was free to pay him just as much
by means of a poundage rate—that, in short, it
could pay him 205., but must on no account pay
him a pound. Perhaps (which is the more likely)
it considered it a fair thing to allow in
special cases an occasional Australian mail to be
carried with Australian money by a colored or
partly colored crew It is a common thing, and a
wise enough thing usually, to give, an Adminis-
tration some permissive power in regard to
details, trusting to it to carry out as far as pos-
sible the spirit of an Act. But if Premier
Barton —as he seems to hint to J. Chamberlain
—intends to make the anti-colored-seaman clause
of the Postal Act practically inoperative, it will
be a serious question whether he is fit to be left
in charge of any permissive legislation—or,
indeed, of any legislation at all.
The Judiciary Bill.
There are several good reasons why the Senate
should make an effort to get the Federal Judiciary
Bill back to something like the shape in which it
was brought down to the House of Reps In the
first place there is the reason, which was not suf-
ficient for the House of Reps., but which may
have more strength with the Senate, that Aus-
tralia needs a strong dignified judiciary and can’t
make any sort of pretence of being in the business
of nation-making seriously unless it has the first
essential of good government—an incorruptible
Supreme Court with adequate powers, adequate
membership and adequate pay. Other reasons, of
the several, are less worthy, or perhaps not worthy
at all, since a member of the Australian Parliament
ought to be an Australian first and last, and not a
Tasmanian or a Victorian; but they may be
mentioned just as reasons which will influence to
the good some weaker vessels, and. which may
lead some Senators to do the best thing from the
worst motives. The Senator of a “small” State
ought to be particularly anxious to see a strong
judiciary, for the small States have been called
upon to trust a good deal of what they value to
the Federation. The fact that what they value is
a worthless thing intrinsically,. and that they
would be far better off and happier if they forgot
all about the tinsel dignities of their “State
independence” and merged themselves abso-
lutely into the Commonwealth, hasn’t any-
thing to do with this argument. The fact
is that the small States do value their
State existence from absurdly sentimental
motives which they will grow out of
one day ; and the only bulwark it has is the Con-
stitution, and. the Constitution without a strong
High Court is a very poor bulwark. The law is
in itself no protection if it has not a policeman
and a court to punish offenders ; and a Constitu-
tion will only prove as strong as the Judiciary
entrusted with its administration. If there is
any danger of a big strong State over-riding a
weak little State (The Bulletin doesn’t at all
believe that there is, but still the possibility may
exist), then a strong High Court, which is so well
paid that it can afford to be incorruptible, is the
best protection against such a danger. It is also
the best protection against Irvine and Philp and
Robert Reid and all the various sins against Aus-
tralia which they typify; and that’s really why
The Bulletin is so anxious to see it established,
but if that issue doesn’t appeal to the Senate
others may. The High Court is to be the inter-
preter of the Constitution. If one State should
seek to take advantage of another by a false read-
ing of some dubious clause in the Constitution,
the High Court is the only authority that can
intervene. It is supposed to be strong enough to
sit down hard on the Federal Government itself
if that body exceeds its powers. It is supposed
to belong to no State but to do equal justice to
all (if Tasmania ever thinks itself wronged by
Victoria, and finds its case, for want of- a High
Court, brought before a Victorian Judge acting
in a temporary Federal capacity, it will under-
stand the importance of this point). It is to be a
body with such extraordinary powers that it re-
quires for its Judges the strongest, wisest, and
most incorruptible men in Australia —and such
men can’t be got without adequate payment.
There are many institutions which may be jerry-
built and yet no serious harm arise, but the High
Court is certainly not one of them.

The Low-down Australian of the Daily
Press.
It would be difficult to over-estimate the damage
done to the Australian people by its bitterly
unpatriotic daily press. The press preaches,
almost without exception, and almost without
ceasing, the doctrines of dependence and servility
and inept helplessness ; represents the Australian
as a poor creature incapable of any intelligent
organised effort, fit only to do the harsh, menial
work of some higher race, and to live on- the
money he can borrow by pledging his soul to
Cohen. Sydney Evening News had a charac-
teristic picture sermon on this text the'other
day a cartoon suggesting that if an
Australian navy were created it would neces-
sarily be run on Owe’Sullivan lines, without
discipline and without efficiency, and would go out
to meet the enemy with its Admiral horn-piping
with an Irish sailor, and its crew at cards and
whisky. The lesson sought to be impressed was
that the Australian is such a low-down-in-the-
animal-scale creature that he must necessarily rely
on the better type of the Englishman to defend
his coasts —that if he were to attempt naval defence
on his own the effort would certainly be a-farce
culminating in a disaster. It is because such a
very large section of the Australian press holds and
promulgates these views—all the Freetrade papers
and some of the allegedly Protectionist ones-;that
the Australian is, to some extent, falling away
from his old standards of sturdiness and courage.
The persistent preaching of lying pessimism has
some effect, as the people don’t fully recognise
yet that their freetrade daily papers after all
represent only the draper spirit of the community,
that they live on the drapers’ advertisements, thjtt
their gods are the wholesale ragmen of Flinders-
lane and Clarence-street, and that their ideas on
national matters are the ideas of the shop-walker.
If it were a fact that the average Australian is
such a miserable creature that he hasn’t the enter-
prise to till his own land, and carry on his own
manufactures, and defend his own home, and pay;
his way out of his own revenue, the call would be;
clear to every man unfortunate enough • to
live among such a mob to pack his trunk
and seek some land with a destiny aiid
a prospect of national existence. But emphati-
cally it is not a fact. It is a misapprehension of
the cowardly draper’s soul, a wrong conclusion of
the man with the cheating.yard-wand (which is
the sceptre of the Freetrade newspaper world).
Australia, as was shown in the past, before the
days of the borrowing disease and the centralising
disease and the importers’ newspaper disease,
showed spirit enough and promise enough of
greatness. It has been handicapped seriously by
the piling up of a huge debt—as part of the in-
terest on whioh the country is expected to sacri-
fice to some extent its national ideals; and by the
helpless, hopeless maunderings of draper news-
papers and K.C.M.G. politicians. Probably
Australia will not be able to make any real pro-
gress on the path to nationhood until the habit of
mortgaging everything is cured, and the growth
of manufactures makes the nation self-supporting
and self-contained, and the process of de-central-
isation kills the political power of the drapery
organs. But all these things are possible. The
Australian Government has borrowed nothing as
yet; Australia has actually one Government
that has existed a year or two without
floating a loan. The Australian tariff —in-
adequate though it is—is doing something to
stimulate manufactures. The Australian Par-
liament is more free from the domination of the
cheating yard-wand press than any legislative
body has been on this continent for a quarter-of-a-
century. Further, the early records of Australia
forbid the idea that we are doomed to become a
people of shop-walkers and poor-spirited agri-
cultural serfs. It was not for that end that the
goldfields attracted to these shores, undefiled by
any traditions of human slavery, some of the
freest and most enterprising spirits of Europe and
America. Australia can be a nation, and will be,
in spite of its newspapers.

Xyabram has Suspicions.
Last January Victorian Premier Irvine made
some severe remarks about the locked-up land in
the Western district, and said that, unless the
squatters broke up their big estates, something
might happen to them. A little later Minister
Taverner was reported by the Argus as saying
that a measure somewhat on the lines of the Maori-
land Compulsory Land Purchase scheme might
be necessary to secure the breaking up of the said
estates. It really looks as if there were something
in these remarks, and the Argus of July 22nd
announced that “ A feeling exists . . . that there
is a great deal behind the recent statements of the
Premier . . . that 1 water should be brought to
the waterless, and the land to the landless men.’”
“ Politicians declare,’’ concludes the Argus, “that
they would not be surprised to find the Govern-
ment proposing a compulsory land purchase law.”
Kyabram and Co. are beginning to be suspicious
of Irvine, and to recall that it is not so long ago
since he was a declared single-taxer. If the
frozen leader of the Government goes to the
country with a clear-cut policy of breaking up the
big estates, a lot of his past misdeeds will be for-
given him. The locking up of the land in
great neglected areas is a huge dead-weight
on the chest of Victorian agriculture. No
one knows this better than Minister Taverner,
who is trying to coax selectors _ on to
poor, miserable, scrub-covered, vermin-infested
land in the back of Gippsland and in the_ Otway
forests. Even for this awful land, where it takes
three generations of selectors to clear the scrub
and timber, and where it is an axiom that the first
selector retires ruined or dies of broken heart,
there are hundreds of applicants. And all the
while there are scores of magnificent estates, scat-
tered all over the State, which are ideally situated
and composed of excellent land, but which are
looked up in the hands of wealthy squatters and
not used to one-tenth their capacity. These vast
estates support half-a-dozen boundary riders, etc.,
now, while they are capable of providing for hun-
dreds of prosperous families. In the single county
of Hampden, for example, there are about 40 huge
estates, ranging up to 50,000 acres in area, nob
counting properties that are only fit for sheep-
farming. While this enormous expanse of valuable
land is lying practically idle, farmers are pouring
out of the State to Maoriland, Tasmania, South
Africa, Canada—anywhere, in the search for
farms. They are being crowded out—crowded
out of a country where the most fertile
counties have about five persons to the square
mile. There are actually fertile and well-watered
counties where the population decreased largely
between the last two censuses—man being elbowed
out to make room for sheep. If Irvine attacks
this land question in dead earnest, Victoria may
yet see the spectacle of the democratic vote keep-
ing him in office, and Kyabram raving in the
distance.

Another specimen of the short-skirted damsel
with the long tongue and the improper story
transpired at Flemington (Melb.) Police Court,
the other day. Her simple little tale of violent
assault was complete in all details, even to some'
very visible bruises and scratches on her cheeks,
and was so convincing that the police immediately
arrested the accused—her employer—who was'
just about to sail for India with horses, and locked
him up. The chief reason why she fell in was
that accused was rich enough to employ a clever
lawyer who speedily knocked the lady’s fairy-tale'
out in one round. The doctor who attended her
swore that there were no bruises, scratches or
abrasions on her body, but that there were deep'
and apparently self-inflicted scratches on each
side of her face; that, though the neck-band of
her under-garment was torn, that of the dres3 she'
was wearing at the time of the assault was intact
that there were no signs of recent assault, and that
the plaintiff was a girl whose loose character was of:
longstanding. In cross-examination shefirst denied,,
then admitted, that she had been intimate with,
other men—she realty couldn’t tell how many ; also
that she had been paid for her indiscretions —they
were at least professional. Then the case collapsed,
and accused left the court to catch his boat if he'
could, and make up for the time and money lost.
The girl also left the court—that sort of girl'
always does. She had committed gross perjury ;;
and she had laid a trap—stupid, perhaps, but
none the less malicious —to ruin her employer
or to blackmail him. But the Law has a regard
for the evil-minded girl-perjurer and never dreams
of prosecuting her. The bench and the alleged
Crown are both in the conspiracy to annul the law
as regards oneof the worst and most prevalent crimes i
in the community. The Crown doesn’t prosecute
the Judge before whom the perjury took place'
doesn’t commit the perjurer. Yet Australian.
Judges are much-given to complaining about then
prevalence of perjury. How can it be otherwise'’
than prevalent when such Judges exist ?

“ J.F.” writes :
You could strike matches on the faces of the congrega-
tion of crusted old white-labor-haters that gathered 1 ,
under the ajgis of the Royal Geographical Society in.
Melbourne the other day to whine again at the “ White.
Australia” sentiment. H. Gyles Turner presided,
whilst J. A. Panton made fine large statements about
the immense area of land in Australia that is suitable for
cotton growing “if the Government would so amend,
their policy as to enable ‘ suitable labor ’ to be
imported to develop the industry.” Other talkers
as well as Panton harped continually on the
“suitable labor" foolishness; and manifestly knew
so little about the details of cotton culture, that it
was plainly evident they had only seized cotton as a peg
whereon to hang their whines for cheap labor. Mr. Le ;
Soeuf tickled them immensely with some information
that somebody else had given him, to the effect that
somebody up at Gairns was growing cotton, from which
he expected to reap £264 6s. sd. per acre per annum.
At mention of this “expected” result, the Tory crusts
could bp heard rattling as they came together in hurried!
communion. Then they assured one another that a yield
of £264 an acre was not sufficient to enable a man to pay
white laborers £1 a week, “ and a sufficiency of ‘ suitable
labor ’ was imperative.” Finally some M’sL.O. fatuously
remarked that they had “great hopes that the ‘note
struck’ at this meeting will lead to,” etc. And in all
this fuss not one dreary wretch in the crowd attempted
to explain why not a soul of those present had attempted
to grow cotton, or showed the least interest in cotton,
during all the years and years in which niggers were
freely admitted. It is over 40 years since the N.S.W
Government offered land gratis to intending cotton-
growers, and they didn’t turn up. Nobody cares for
cotton, except as something to make a row about in the
interests of black labor.

Very shortly 8,000,000 acres of leases in the
Victorian Mallee expire, and Lands Minister
Taverner is making haste, or is going shortly to
begin to make as much haste as so slow a man is
capable of, to cut them up for settlement. The
Mallee is Victoria’s drought-land and great agri-
cultural tragedy. It has about two decent
seasons in seven years. So far it has ruined and
broken the heart of nearly every settler who went
that way. But the Chirnsides and the Clarkes
and the Manifolds and the rest who own the
great fertile area of south-western Victoria must
not be disturbed. So the Mallee is the only place
available. There have been a few showers there
of late, and there is a temporary scurf of green
over the Mallee, and a flicker of hair on the skull
of Victoria’s great North-Western horror, so
Taverner will doubtless find victims enough.
Therefore, he is going to make haste—gradual
haste, after the manner of a tortoise with an
appointment to keep —and cut up this flattened
Slieol for the promotion of settlement. The pro-
motion of settlement is a great idea.
