A LETTER from Yarra’s smellful shore
A really choice suggestion was sent to Melbourne Agk
lately by a “ Farmer”—a sturdy, self-reliant toiler in our
great natural industries. He hailed from Traralgon,
where the farmers mostly plant store-bullocks, poddy
calves, and other vegetables in the long grass, and sit
down to wait till the season comes to reap “ fats.” What
he wanted was that the Government should straightaway
borrow £2,500,000—n0 less—to make roads all over the
country, from the farmer’s back-door to the nearest rail-
way station. “Farmer’s” scheme was simplicity itself.
The Government was to borrow 2j millions, and instead
of giving the present municipal subsidy of £lOO,OOO was
to use the subsidy to pay the interest on the loan ; also
the shires were to form a sinking fund of 1| per cent.
What was to happen to the shires that didn’t form such a
fund—if anything—he didn’t say ; probably they would
repeat the Irrigation Trust dodge and pass the debt on
to the State. Seeing: that most of the shires refuse to
pay anything like reasonable taxes and keep their existing
roads in repair by means of the present subsidy, these
“ permanent and reproductive works ” would probably go
to ruin altogether if the subsidy was suddenly withdrawn ;
“Farmer” didn’t say what was to happen then. But the
most beautiful thing is the “ Farmer's ” calm assumption
that the municipal subsidy is to continue for ever and
ever ; that the city toiler—who mostly dies worth about
a week’s wages —is to pay taxes for ever to build the far-
mer’s roads and the roads of the rich country landowner.
I begin to wonder that the Victorian farmer doesn’t
demand from the Government an assured, settled income,
free of taxes, to him, his heirs and assigns for ever.
From Tasmania:
After all the blow and bluster of. new Tasmanian
Government, the great scheme of Parliamentary reform
is to make the two Houses sit together in the same
chamber; but the Upper House is still to be elected on
the old franchise, and the Houses are to vote separately.
The benefit promised is the saving of £4OOO in printing
and salaries in the distant future, and a reduction
of eight members in the two Houses. The Ministry hopes
that the antiques which make Tasmania's Upper House
hideous will have their Conservative feelings democratised
by sitting in the same room as the Lower House members.
Isn’t that a great idea ? Experience points all the other
way; that the “aristocratic" influence will destroy the
democratic sympathies of the “lower order ’ member,
and Heaven knows he has so few of them in Tasmania
that he can’t spare any. It took Attorney-General
Nicholls four columns of Launceston Examiner to ex-
plain the beauties of the scheme. He challenged and
defied criticism; he omitted everything which might
•offend the susceptibilities of the Gods of Things-as-They-
Are ; but he omitted to face the real issue that the whole
plan is a sham and put-off. Ministry cries : “ Wait till
Parliament meets and we ll show you what we’re going
to do.” But meanwhile the electors are getting im-
patient ; and if the alleged strong Ministry deals with
Ueform as it did with the smallpox, Teddy Mulcaiiy will
find his island wishing it hadn’t fired him out with quite
so loud a bang.
In its half-yearly report, lately issued, the Vic-
torian Miners’ Association describes the condi-
tions under which some of the deep ground is
worked —the ground below 3000 ft. At one Ben-
digo mine, visited by the officers of the union, a
pool of water is kept near the shaft at 3175 ft. for
the miners to cool-off in. The men were working
in a level driven 403 ft.; in this hell the tempera-
ture varied from 95 to 98 degrees, and the alleged
atmosphere was charged with dust (made by the
boring machines) and ’fracteur fumes. Says the
report: —
We could scarcely breathe, and our sensations were
horrible. The poor fellows working there were quite
resigned to their fate, and simply remarked, “ Well, you
knew, somebody has to do it.”
And yet, a couple of hundred feet up the same
shaft, where there was a connection with another
deep mine, the ventilation was good ; there was,
in fact, air going to waste. But it costs money to
make connections between shafts at these depths,
and miners are cheap —horribly cheap at Bendigo.
•So the connections go unmade, and men work
under conditions beside which those of a Queens-
land canefield are pleasant and, beyond all com-
parison, more healthy. And Victoria talks proudly
•of its great natural industry of gold-mining, a.nd
the marvellous enterprise of the deep-sinking
•companies and mine-owners.

One encounters truth even in the witness : box,
•and a soldier may write sensibly on a political
•question. In proof, Lieut.-Col. A. W. Pollock
in Macmillan’s Magazine on Imperial Defence :
If the British Empire were to fall to-morrow, the sub-
jugation of, ifor example, Australia would not necessarily
That Great Britain, shorn of her colonies, would
sink to the level of Holland is clear, but that her colonies
in general wtmld become the spoil of the victor is by no
means a foregone conclusion.. The conquest of Australia
would be a,colossal enterprise, beside which our recent
•expedition tq South Africa would sink into insignificance.
. Thus—even if we were justified in adopting such a tone
I —threats to cast off Australia as the penalty of failing to
•embrace the entire doctrine of “one navy, one sea,”
Would, if carried out or anticipated, result in certain
mischief to the Mother Country, but in only moderate
risks to her discarded daughter. In such circumstances,
quite apart from all considerations of good taste and
brotherhood, it seems better for us to accept any avail-
able compromise than to insist vainly upon the full
pound of flesh.

i , Maybe, when August Ist (last Saturday)
was waning ;to midnight, and the lustrous and
illustrious audience that had watched out the in-
auguration programme at the resurrected Her
Majesty s was departing to its carriage and auto-i
mob’le, J. C. Williamson said unto himself,
-ly® bj® this date in wine upon a rose-leaf
scroll Maybe, instead of quoting the Persian
poet (and being used to success), he merely
yawned and asked if the £7OO odd proceeds of the
night was safely Ghubbed. Sixteen months ago,
on a Palm Sunday, during the first week of “ Ben
Hur, the old theatre was burned to the ground.'
Last Saturday night the new theatre, shining as
the_ new Jerusalem, opened its doors for the first,
performance, and raised its curtain upon a
oapanese tragedy. A Jewish play closed an epoch,
a Jappish play began another. “ Madame But-
,a^ of Japan,” is a perfect instaiice
ot the truth about distance lending enchantment.
If its central figure and environment had been of,
London or Sydney it would be a sordid story,:
worn threadbare in the oft-telling. In its alien
setting it takes on a poetic grace, a quaint pathos;
an idyllic charm that give it a very far aloofness!
from our Philistia But the philosophy at thd
back of the play is very near and very real, and its
lesson is that it is not well to contract a time-
limited marriage with an alien lady of color. The
sweeter and more virtuous she is the more it is
not welL-for her ; nor for you if a humane heart
if ?S e P ossess ions. The story : BiefltenaMij
B. x. Pinkerton, a U.S.A. naval officer, marries
a Japanese orphan maiden, Cho-Oho San, Butter-
fly in translation, who, as the daughter of an
officer, is a lady of quality. He marries lief 1
jus foh a lil’while,” then; after some months,’
rejoins his ship and sails away, promising'to re-
turn “ when the robins nest again.” The play
ppejis in a typical Japanese home, with a view of
a floWerJaden, sun-lit garden; a cherry-tree
massed with pink blossoms sheds its petals 6n to'
the balcony. Cho-Cho-San and her maid, Sukiki,
and a two-year child of whose advent Pinkerton
knows nothing, form the household, and both
women are in a charming state of excitement, for
tfiey have discovered a robin beginning to nest.'
Presently gunfire is heard, and they know the
baby s father is in port on his war-ship. They
make preparations to receive him; the child is
placed on a cushion, given a U.S.A. flag,
and told to wave “like hell,” “foh that
iss what Le’ten’t B F. Pink’ton use’ to
say.” The two women bring out lamps; and
put the room into a flowery disorder. Bios,
soma are strewn everywhere, and the floor is
deluged with cherry petals. It is an exquisite
way of welcoming the old man, so much more
refined than our “feed the brute.” However)
the sailor does, not come, although Butterfly
stands watching and waiting and longing at the
window the whole night long whilst maid and
ohild slumber. On the morrow Butterfly’s heart-
break is complete when she learns from Pinker-
ton’s friend, the" U.S.A. ’Consul, that her
marriage is not binding, and that Pinkerton’s
American wife, whom he has recently married
has joined him’ in the port. ’
* * * *
Butterfly all along has a most eligible
suitor in the person of a young Jap. gentleman,
a citizen of New York, who dresses as if he were
paying a chronic visit to White House, and who
appeals “to this statesman of integrity” (meaning
the Consul) to back his suit. But the deserted
wife rejects him, and when the American
wife comes in with offers to adopt the
child the gentle Butterfly entreats her to
come again on the morrow. Then with her
father’s sword, on which is inscribed “to die with
honor when one can no longer live with honor,’’
she kills herself, dying just as her sailor lover
bursts into the room. In her role as Madame
Butterfly, Ethel Mollison, in an orange kirnona
embroidered with lotus flowers, plays the delight-
ful Jap girl as well as any delightful native-born
might live it ©Her broken English, of which not
a word is unintelligible, her salaams, fan-play and
little staccato trot, her obeisance before her
shrined god, her tea-making, and her innocent
recital to the hard-eyed young Consul, of Pinker-
ton s “ lof-makin’ ” —all these are as seemly to her
as wings to the pretty fly that explains her name.
Susie Vaughan’s Sukiki is a tender bit of Jap serv-
ing-damselism. Harry Plimmer might mix some
sympathy with the officialism of his portrayal of
the Consul. Philip Lytton, as Pinkerton, the
remorseful, tune-limited husband, makes his
entrance as if he were going to stop a row in the
gun-room or chain up a cannon.
* * * *
But, after all, the chief interest of that
first night lay in the re-appearance of Mr.
Williamson himself. As Kerry, an old butler in
the service of an Irish family, he had his audience
alternately in laughter and tears. His make-up,
with legs and'arms akimbo, his brogue, his humor
and pathos, and the sudden alternations of his
V® lGe f rom deep tpnes to piping, whistling notes
old age, proved him still tne master of his art.
Cousin Joe,” in which Mr. Williamson appeared
as a wide-mouthed, smiling English yokel, made
the audience rock with laughter. In both little
plays, Rose Musgrove filled her roles with
such merry or tearful excellence as the parts
demanded;

.The writer had fully intended to begin
opening of the “Midsummer
T’M" ® 8 Cream ” season at Sydney Royal with
Marry something orother in the trueShakspear-
ean manner. Somehow, though, no suitable open-
ing showed itself. Nelertheless, it was a brilliant
occurrence. Only the weather might have been
better; the night “put one in remembrance of a
' Inside, however, a bright audience
filled every part—a bright audience streaked with
a lew clerical coats and countenances. The acting
was_ from good to better; the effects and musio
varied from excellent to tremendous.
* • • •
Marry (that remark is now disposed of)
—Marry, then, what would Shakspeare have said
ol it 1 When he created, Bottom and the rest of
that company of players he most likely wasn’t
thinking of Athens at all. Just as Musgrove, if
h f u^ rote come( Jy,,might show up the magnificence
productions by portraying the shifts to
which the poor players who “do” the shearing-
sheds out in the mulga country are put, so
Shakspeare, part owner of “the Gloabe on the
Banckside, was willing to show the superiority of
his own methods by picturing those of the poor
players of his own time or of a time not so very
remote. Yet the chances are that the hungry
backblooks battlers, who try to knock a living out
of ‘East Lynne” and “Ealsely Accused,” do
almost as well in the way of stage accessories,
at any rate, as Shakspeare did when he appeared'
m 8 °wn roofless Globe. Still, the patron of the
txlobe had this advantage over the modern Man
from Cracked Mountain—the Globe man was at
least getting something worth listening to even if
there was nothing to look at, and the other man'
rarely gets either. The modern manager (in the
city, of course) knows so many mechanical and'
other tricks that the matter is not of supreme im-
portance ; so, as likely as not, if Shakspeare, with
his keen eye to business, were still in the play-
writing line, he would decide that it was
labor lost to sweat out his best stuff. Certainly
he would alter his style. The limitations of
mechanical stagecraft as he knew it compelled
him to adopt devices which are no longer necessary.
Moreover, his audiences, aware of those limitations,
were content with suggestions. They were not
used to stage realism, and did not think of de-
mandmg it. And that is really the difficulty in
connection with the staging of Shakspeare. The
modern playgoer demands realism, and Shaks-
peare invented situations and actions with which
even modern stagecraft cannot cope. Personally
he didn’t try to cope with them at all. He
belonged to an age when you could stage Jonah
and the whale, the public imagining the whale.
But modern stagecraft is progressing fast—-how
fast one does not realise until one witnesses such
a triumph as George Musgrove’s production. In
the Dream ”we get tolerably close to the
realism we demand. True, Puck doesn’t yet give
the impression that he could “ put a girdle round
the earth in 40 minutes.” Twenty years hence
we may smile good-humoredly when we remember
that Dorothy Marsden didn’t really fly. But, for
all that, this represents marvellous mechanical
and (in a measure) artistic progress—these magni-
ficent stage pictures by Hawes Craven, the elec-
tric lighting, the invisible wires on which the
fairies swing down from space, and all the rest of
that sort.
* • • •
The “book” is followed closely. Tastes
have changed in 300 years, and though we stand
plays with grosser plots than Shakspeare ven-
tiired on, a few of his expressions have had to go.
For the rest, the “cuts” are mostly to save time
—the extra time, perhaps, that is consumed by
the farcical business and by the slow dawning of
day in the forest—an incomparable piece of stage
management—at the end of the third act. As to
that farcical business, it was said just now that
when Shakspeare conceived Nick Bottom and his
company he probably was not thinking of Athens
at all. A very similar license is taken in this
presentation. Shakspeare brought the travesty
up to 1600 or thereabouts; W. H. Denny and the
others bring it up to 1900 or thereabouts. When
Denny sticks to Shakspeare his work is tho-
rough ; when he gives us Denny it is, at
any rate, most enjoyable fooling. The humans
are, on the whole, more satisfactory than the
fairies. Miss Maude Milton’s rendering of the
love-sick Helena is extremely fine, and an almost
equal success is gained by Miss Cynthia Brooke
as Henma—she of the more shrewish tongue.
Apart from their acting, their performance is an
elocutionary treat. The fairy king (Miss Dora
Rignold) and queen (Miss Nora Kerin) are charm-
ing to look upon, and charming to listen to in
song; but, unhappily, it seems that the “upper
circles ’ in fairyland have developed the quite
human frailty of calling an herb an “ahrb,” and
here “ he-ah-h-h.” Miss Marsden is a highly suc-
cessful Puck, with a merry laugh, a good voice
and sprightly limbs; and Mr. Croker-King’s brief
(especially in the first act), as the angry old
father of Hermia, is exceedingly well done. There
are others—but there will be time to talk of them
later on.

In another part of this issue appears a
portrait of Miss Florence Menk-Meyer, an Aus-
tralian pianist of note, who returns to her native
land to have another temporary smell of the
wattle and a look at the kangaroo of her infancy,
and, incidentally, to play a short season, com-
mencing at Sydney Centenary Hall on the 15th.
Miss Menk-Meyer has been absent from Australia
for some years, and during that time she has
gathered a bookful of enthusiastic notices in every
language of any account that is spoken in Europe,
and has made a pleasant and favorable dazzle in
all the capitals of that scrappy little continent
which stioks out like a collection of legs and
wings from the western side of Asia.

“Madame Sans Gene,” the Napoleonic
play with its suggestions of the fierce spirit of
French Republicanism, surviving even in the days
of the Imperial harlequinading to which Napoleon
descended in his decadence, is full of life and
color and humor. When presented before in
Sydney by the Broughs it suffered rather from
over-refined and tame treatment; .as staged by
the Frawley Co. at Sydney Palace last Saturday it
was more. rugged, in some respects more
effective, in others rathe* too much remini-
scent of the cowboy spirit. The Napoleonic
eagles scream occasionally in the lingo of Texas.
Miss Van Buren, in the title-part, is the most
successful of the east. Her rendering of the
thankless r6le of Mrs. Bonham in “Arizona”
suggested that the lady kept the stage on the
strength of her figure and her face only. But
“Sans Gene” shows that she has art. Both in
the hoydenish scenes of humor and the more
serious situations, Miss Van Buren plays with
skill and force. Frawley’s Napoleon is tuned to a
far too melodramatic key. Admitted that the
period of the play is that of the Empire—when a
great revolutionary and a marvellous soldier
had sunk to the position of an Emperor,
to the mental condition in whioh the,
symbols and trappings of magnificence are
of mote importance than the realities of power—
the Frawley make-up and reading of the charac-
ter must still, be regarded as faulty. The actor
almost rants in places. Mr. Reynolds makes of
Lefebvre a notable figure, and two others stand-
ing out from the ruck are Mr. Shaw as Fouche,
and Mr. Amory as Savary. Of 16 or so other
characters all are played evenly, and the piece
throughout moves briskly and well. A farcical
comedy “ In Paradise’’—adapted from the French
—is promised for next Saturday.
At Sydney Lyceum J. H. Nunn continues
to lead the gallant Gordons up the canvas heights
of Dargai and the Silvenis to tie themselves into a
Ladder of Life and rescue Miss Guildford from
the steam hammer. Next Saturday night “The
Greed of Gold ” will be revived—the play in which
Willard is (or used to be) struck dumb early in
the evening, with the result that he finds great
difficulty in convincing the world that he didn’t
commit murder or some other trifle.

The frantic play founded on the vulture
freak will finish its course at the Criterion on Fri-
day, and next night Charles Holloway will stage
one of the most popular of melodramas, “ The
Shadows of a Great City.” The fact that Hollo-
way has to borrow it from J. C. Williamson is
circumstantial evidence that this stage-story of.
New York life is ahead of the ordinary shocker
if it wasn’t, J.C.W. wouldn’t own it. So far
Holloway has mounted his productions excellently,
and he will have plenty of scope for jthat branch
of effort here.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, W. C. Fields has now
ousted Rudinoff from the star place. Fields is a
comic juggler—with a good deal of the skill of Cin-
que valli, though with none of his panther-strength.
He is best. in the comedy part of his business, but
some of his balancing is up to the standard of
Salerno, if not of Cinquevalli. Rudinoff has
changed his programme slightly again ; has even
introduced some new jokes. But he would be
well advised to drop his lightning sketches of
Sydney Harbor scenes ; they are not at all con-
vincing in color or in outline.
*# * *
“ The Serenade,” having failed to strike
Melbourne very hard, will quit the Princess’s on
Friday this week. It seems bad luck for the
management when a handsome and melodious
opera bOuffe misses the mark because it isn’t
funny, considering that the same defect in weaker
musical shows is often received with roars of
merriment. But perhaps iir these other cases the
people merely don’t know what they are
laughing at, whereas in “The Serenade” case
they have a keen perception of what they are not
laughing at. People, as a rule, can see
no joke in the labored frivolities of Madame
olapoffski, or the insincere friskiness of
Miss May Beatty, who appears to be out of
health and spirits. To enjoy this performance
one must take the opera seriously, and cast a for-
giving eye upon the bouffe. Saturday will bring
another opera of a somewhat different sort, only
more so. Donizetti’s “LaFille du Regiment” is
to be revived, with Madame Slapoffski and
Lempriere Pringle in the very principal parts,
and a good deal of chorus scattered around. The
Musgroveian advt. describes “La Fille” as a
conuc opera, making no allowance for the fact
that Donizetti died nearly 60 years ago. It was
co .mic enough then, no doubt. Next Saturday, it
will have ilie charm of an antique romantic grand
musical comedy in two acts, and the revelation
begins at 8.

At Melb. Her Majesty’s they are repeating
“The Messenger Boy” for a second week ; so
that child of uncertain age is still getting pursued
from London to the Paris Exhibition (via Brindisi
and Cairo) by the full strength of the company.
Opinions are divided as to whether the co. chases
the boy, or vice versd, but the first statement holds
good m default of coherent evidence to the con-
trary. Little Miss Olive Lenton plays the part
which Miss Musgrove filled on the first night, and
does it very well in an amateurish way ; also the
character of the Messenger Boy’s mother has
passed from the care of Miss Rose Hamilton into
the keeping of Miss Aggie Kelton. Present
scribe entertains a dim suspicion that Miss Kelton
fills the bill as a low-comedy mother, but this
is her first reappearance in Melbourne since a
way-back period, and, r ‘ like the odor of brine
from the ocean comes the thought of other
years” when she was a dashing, up-to-date
impersonation of Love’s Young Dream in “Doro-
thy ” and “ Princess Ida,” and subsequently a
delectable Chopinette in “Paul Jones.” All
things considered, it is consoling to hear Hooker
Pasha’s private secretary refer to Tommy Bang’s
mother as “rather tasty.” Next Saturday a
revival of “ The Runaway Girl ” comes along and
lasts three nights, after which “San Toy’’and
“ The French Maid.”
m* * *
At Melbourne Royal, the new Blandolt
pradrama (Bulletin word, signifying drama com-
posed mainly of prad, like a beef sausage) gives
every promise of being a remunerative circus for
weeks to come. It satisfies the sesthetic few who
want fairly brisk dialogue with their spectacular
shocks, whilst it delights the vast demonstrative
majority that wants nothing more intellectual
than ’orses, and stoushes and bona-fide vittles
on the. table when the needy hero comes to
dine with the low comedian. The vittles scene
in the pradrama aforesaid is a huge success,
although it makes no variation from twenty
previous banquets of the kind, except in the
startling fact that a Jew money-lender arrives
with, a ppcket full of fried fish, which he presents
to Baker as a tribute of esteem for that
poor, busted Gentile. This inhuman act of
Nathanio generosity seems to have no ulterior
motive whatever. The affluent usurer casts
his fish upon the waters without a chance of
getting it back after many days. He has no
interest in the plot of the play because his
assistance is not required. Owing to the pre-
valence of horse there is hardly enough plot to go
round among four people, and the dramatic duties
of clever Miss Brightie Smith are confined to a
cab yard, where she hops around on a game leg,
rejoicing in the prospect of a “scrap” between
hero Baker and a brother cab-driver.
*# # *
At the Opera House, a well-appointed
French lady is giving the prettiest bird show this
country has yet seen. Once upon a time, a charm-
ing Queen of the Feathered Tribe, or some such
potentate, appeared at the old Opera House with
a number of hungry pigeons, and made a good
impression, but the pigeon lady was a small
dramatic item compared with Madame Marzella,
the latest Rickards engagement. Madame’s
performing birds are mostly cockatoos, white
or. gaudy, supported by a ruminative
fair of black crows and a gifted eagle.
n the absence of the eagle, whose “turn’’was
not yet ready for production, the cockatoos and
crows evoked special enthusiasm on Saturday
night, and the entertainment, which is strong in
decorative fittings and limelight effects, went
straight to the audience’s Australian heart.
Madame Marzella adds a lot of importance to the
Rickards’ programme. Frederick Hallen and
Miss Fuller, with the assistance of a silent third
person, are still giving a crudely humorous comedy
on the lines of the Drew comedies, and Dan
Crawley has changed some of his chants. >’
** * *
“ O. Kapple,” about a disappointing aris-
ocrat:
A most awful public entertainment was Count Vav de
Vaya’s lecture, entitled “ How I came to Australia,”
given at Melbourne Town Hall. His Nibs didn’t provide
all the agonies of that entertainment. He was preceded
by musical items, including two organ solos, for which
the organ, perhaps, deserves more blame than the man
who unkindly consented to perform upon it. Between
two dismal periods of organ came three lady vocalists and
two Misses Pinschof. The first songstress was a smart
and pretty girl with a tuneful little soprano—hardly a
full-sized drawing-room voice ; just a satisfactory parlor
voice. She sang two numbers (bracketed), and evidently
enjoyed her long stay on the platform. The other singers
were not decorative items, but the sorrowful-looking one
who sang a long aria from Weber’s “ Frieschutz ” had a
fairly large voice, foggy, yet sweet; also her vocalism
was artistic. The third lady seemed rather too
grown-up for her skittish Christian name of “ Lovie,”
and when she was uttering two Brahms songs(bracketed)
the name of Moanie or Groanie would have fitted her
much better. Then the two Misses Pinschof ! One at a
time they come forward as though Papa had bought the
Town Hall, and hired the vice-regal presence for the
occasion. Each Miss Pinschof starting to play the piano
adjusted herself leisurely on the music stool and went
through a series of “ side ” exercises. The elder Miss
Pinschof, after striking a note severely wdth her forefinger,
would pause such a long time that her next note came as a
surprise. The arrogant self-sufficiency of these damsels !
The musical portion of the show (prices 35., 2s. and Is.)
was followed by Mayor Gillott’s introduction of Mon-
signor the Count, and the Major cut his introduction
very long. He had taken the precaution to assume his
robe, of office—a heavy winter garment trimmed with fur;
and whilst the unfortunate Town Hall listeners were
doing the usual perish Mayor Gillott maundered on. He
had nothing to say, but he said it repeatedly. His few
remarks stammered round and round the point until they
grew too dizzy to stammer further. Then Count Vay
de Vaya began his “ lecture.” A handsome-faced
count in a red cassock with elbow-sleeves, blue
and white silk band, and a gold chain round
the neck,. and numerous jewelled decorations glitter-
ing on his chest. A cross of crystals, or diamonds,
looked very pretty. An ornamental Count in all respects,
but otherwise an out-and-out fraud as an entertainer.
His foreign accent made him unintelligibly to people at
the back of the hall, whilst those who could understand
what he was saying marvelled at the feebleness of his
discourse. One doesn’t expect too much from, titled
entertainers. I had sampled the stage work of the Earls
of Yarmouth and Shaftesbury in days of yore, and was
prepared to think charitably of the Vatican Count, but
after hearing him lecture I look back with gratitude at
the comparatively brilliant performances of the other
exalted amateurs. Count V. de V.’s red robe and glitter-
ing cross protected him from any open demonstration of
discontent, nevertheless a large percentage of the audi-
ence retired noisily from the premises long before the
infliction was ended.

RABBITS!
ARE THEY TO BE
A PROFIT OR PEST?
It rests entirely with our settlers whether it shall cost them and the State a large amount
continue the war which has been waged against “ bunny ” for years past','
whether they will take the trouble to turn him into an asset which will bring a steady
revenue to the country and provide employment for THOUSANDS of its people. The
Kosewarne Packing Company, Ltd., is now receiving rabbits at its works in Young,
JXarandera and Sydney. The highest market price is given in return for freshly-killed
rabbits free of any commission or charges. r , , ~ v
For further particulars, address —
THE ROSEWARNE PACKING COMPANY, Limited,
HEAD OFFICE— NARANDERA
70 PITT ST., SYDNEY or YOUNG.


his direction, but the music for the most part hardly
suggested the meaning of the poem printed on the pro-
gramme. The same orchestra had given a comparatively
lucid statement of the “ Tannhauser ” Overture. But
•* Tannhauser ” offers the instruments fair chances of rea-
listic effect, as far as inarticulate sounds can go in pictur-
esque narrative and spiritual suggestion. Strauss Spoem
lias no light variety or movement in it. The orchestra
i> asked to portray a noble-minded man dying in a garret
with one tallow candle to light his agonies.- At first he
is asleep, and then the music is supposed to show a
melancholy smile flitting o’er his pallid features. He
recovers consciousness to agonise a while, after which he
dreams of how he grew from a happy child into the ill-
paid poet who is now about to be a dismal subject for a
pauper funeral. In due course the lonely dreamer ex-
pires. The poverty-stricken, ill-ventilated garret has a,
corpse for a tenant. Up to that point the orchestras
task is dull and colorless. Then the late unlamented
body releases the soul, and it soars upward to “ heavenly
plains,” where its desire is fulfilled ; and then, of course,
the orchestra grows impressive—but only in an orthodox
way. Strauss’s Heaven shows no novelty of imagination
to compensate the audience for his dull pretence of de-
picting a sordid scene of silence by a suitable arrange-
ment of sounds. ■

At Adelaide Tivoli, the Boissetts have
bashed each other with bricks and with eatables
of various kinds to the apparent satisfaction of
large audiences ; but they show a regrettable
American tendency to end up with. an explosion
and some red fire. (By a grim coincidence, the
very day they opened in their bricklayer sketch,
the fun of which largely consists in falling off
ladders, a mason fell off his ladder in an Adelaide
church and was killed among the pews.) The
Boissetts now appear in a new sketch, wherein
they wear evening dress instead of moleskins;
but they bash each other just as before. By
the way, the small humorist of the Go. does not
look nearly as much like Johnny Briggs in a
tail-coat as he did in a corduroy. The Royal has
been empty for a few nights, the bard and the
fairies having moved on. To the great regret of
Adelaide, Nora Kerin of the Ada Ferrar voice,
and Cynthia Brooke of the Lillah M’Carthy voice,
and Bucklaw of the tired voice, and the Croker-
King who plays two dukes, and all the rest of
them, are-gone. The takings for the season are
officially stated to be the best ever known, except
for Sara Bernhardt, who charged increased prices,
and so doesn’t fairly compare. George Lauri and
the Comic Opera Co., with a quiok-ohange season
of four old plays, are imminent.

“The Right Hon. the Treasurer has merely given
us a good book-keeper’s view of the state of the finances.
I am bound to express my profound disappointment
with it.”— George Reid, in the Reps.
Australian Treasurer Turner’s.. Budget is a
model of straightforwardness. The only charge that
even G. H. Reid could make in connection with it was
that Turner had stuck hard and fast to his figures—
which amounts to about the best testimonial a Treasurer
ever got from the leader opposite. The totals are :

The total actual expenditure for 1902-3 was £3,901,759, or
£350,147 less than had been estimated ; the estimated ex-
penditure now goes up by £418,690—a large sum, yet
only £68,543 more than had been estimated for 1902-3.
The Treasurer expects that the post office will cost
£2,525,758, as against £2,406,193, an increase of £119,565 ;
the elections (charged to the vote for Parliament) are re-
spons ble for an extra £45,000 ; the High Court is down
for £4500. No provision, however, seems to have been
made for the Arbitration Court. Last year an absolute
saving of £78,145 was made in the Defence department as
compared with the previous year of blood-drunkenness ;
a further saving is promised here this year of over
£60,000. This, however, is independent of the naval
tribute.
As to the three departments that were taken
over by the Commonwealth, the expenditure in the last
year that they were under State control was, as nearly as
can be estimated, £3,067,000; for the current year it is
estimated at £3,152,000, or £85,000 more. The largest
increase has been in N.S.W. That there has been any
increase at all, under the almost drastic economy of
Federal control, is due to the fact that the revenue is
now charged with much of what was formerly charged to
loans, while on the eve of Federation several of the
States allowed their stores of ammunition to run down
almost to nothing: also they rushed up the classification
of some of their servants in the departments to be trans-
ferred, and left the Commonwealth to pay the bill. As
to the position of the States during the year just com-
menced, the position is that they will, it is estimated,
receive £958,393 less than they received last year. The
amount is made up in this way :

In view of the heavy debenture burden which the co.
carries (£848,000 of debentures have been issued), and of
the fact that these debentures begin to fall due in 1908,
the increase of dividend did not look justified when
it took place, and it looks even less justified now that the
profits are so much less. After payments have been
made out of revenue for new works, there remains this
year only £629 to add to reserves; while the reserves
have been drawn upon to the extent of £5665 for the
renewal of meters, leaving a net reduction of reserves
amounting to £5036. The reserves now consist of pre-
mium on shares, £466,536 ; reserve fund, £87,292 (£50,000
of which is represented by debentures, probably the co’s.
own); meter renewal fund, £11,836 ; and undivided
profit, £4720.

* * * *
The N.S.W. Government is fooling in an extra-
ordinary fashion with the question of Broken Hill water
supply. Apparently more with the object of keeping
local people quiet than for any other reason, a rabbit-
brained scheme has been put in hand at Umberumberka
Creek without the sanction of either Parliament or Public
Works Committee. The Owe’Sullivan’s brilliant idea is
to build a dam at this place, and a tramway to connect
the dam with the Adelaide-Broken Hill railway, sq that,
when the company’s reservoir runs dry, water may be
carted to Broken Hill in trucks. When there is any
water in the company’s reservoir, of course, nothing
will be drawn from Owe’Sullivan’s wild-cat dam,
and it will be earning nothing, though it will be
costing the country from £3OOO to £3500 a year in inte-
rest and upkeep. If the company’s reservoir doesn’t
fail for another ten years (up to now it hadn’t failed for
15 years), then Owe’Sullivan’s wild-cat will cost £35,000
before it has a chance to do anything. Even then the
water will have to be carted in trucks 20 or 30 miles ;
and, though that might be some sort of a makeshift so
far as a domestic supply is concerned, if the Owe’Sullivan
reckons upon trucking water 25 miles at a rate cheap
enough to allow the concentrating mills and other
works to use it, then he is a painfully ignorant
Owe’Sullivan. Meantime, in spite of an outlay
of £45,000, Broken Hill will not, as an ordinary
thing, have any more water, or any cheaper water, or
any purer water. All it will have for the £45,000 will •
be a dam out in the wilderness, 25 miles or thereabouts,
and the memory of a Minister for Works who had an idea
that the proper way to supply a dry city with water is to
cart it 25 miles in buckets. Moreover, as before
stated,’ Owe’Sullivan has ■ put this ridiculous scheme
in hand without a shred of Parliamentary authority, in
spite of the fact that it is estimated to cost £45,000, and
in cheerful defiance of the law which says that every pro-
posed work estimated to cost £20,000 or more shall be
referred to the Public Works Committee.
• • • • •
Probably the Umberumberka Creek ought to be
dammed ; that, anyhow, is one possible scheriie. But it
is no use making a dam and leaving it there ; it must be.
connected with the town and the mines, not by a futile
tramway, but by a rising main. In that case the Govern-
ment would have to supply the town, and get power to
levy a water-rate; as for the supply of the mines, it
would have to compete with the present company for
that. The other scheme involves taking over the whole
of the present company’s works, and extending the catch-
ment area. Which of the two it is wiser to carry out
depends wholly upon the price that the co. is prepared to
accept. The matter is one for the careful inquiry of the
Works Committee. The company ought to be invited to
say what is its rock-bottom price "; then the Works Com-
mittee should go ahead and decide - between the two
schemes. What the Owe’Sullivan is doing now is simply
prejudicing that simple and effective settlement of the
trouble, because the more money that is spent at Umber-
umberka,- even though it be without authority, the less
inclined will, the Works Committee be to consider the
other scheme on its merits. The apology for a scheme
which Owe’Sullivan has putin hand, without having this
inquiry made, certainly involves a less Outlay than either
of the others ; but in nine years out of ten it will be of no
more benefit to Broken Hill and its mines than astatue of
Owe’Sullivan on the South Head would be, and in the
10th year the benefits will probably be too small to be
worth bothering about.

The North Coast Steam Navigation Co.

The reserves consist of insurance fund, £33,260, and a
“fund for the equalisation of dividends” (practically
undivided profits), £8333. From the former £l9O has
been taken during the half-year ; and the dividend
has reduced the latter by £1355. The co. has lost,
one of its steamers, the Oakland, but.has added two
others to its fleet; and its ship property is valued
at £137,261, as against £133,879 six months ago.
This property is covered by insurance to the extent of
£66,250, or, approximately, half the full value. The
serious reduction of profits has-been mainly due to the
recent drought on the North Coast ; but as drought is,
comparatively speaking, a stranger in those parts, what-
ever change occurs in the co.’s business ought to be for
the better.

“ P.” writes : “ The art union which was started
some time back with such a flourish of trumpets to raise
funds for the relief of the Mount Kembla sufferers seems
to have panned out badly. ‘ Advertised value of prizes
was £3050. The drawing took place in March last, when
it was announced that the receipts from the sale of
tickets was about £2391, and expenses of advertising,
printing, postage, salaries, etc., about £1620. Alleged,
that the expenses, including cost of four motor cars, will
practically absorb the whole of the proceeds. At all
events, so far, no money has been handed over to the
Mount Kembla Relief Fund.

„ 1902 .. 45 45 9524
Profits.
ii 1903 mm 708 — — 8816
The position, though still poor enough', is steadily
improving. In-the 12 months ended June, 1901, the gross
return on the paper value of the co.’s investments was
£3 17s. lOd. per cent. ; to June, 1902, £4 os. 7d per cent.;
in the past year, £4 ss. 2d. per cent. After working
expenses are paid the return is, however, only £2 12s. 9d.
per cent, (it was £2 7s. 2d. per cent, in the previous
term); and this is not large enough to give a fair margin
of profit after paying interest on the disproportionate
amount of borrowed money. The position is that the co.
is working on about £293,000, of which £145,000 is its own
and £148,000 is borrowed. But while the whole £293,000
returns only £2 12s. 9d. net, the £148,000 costs about
£4 7s. 6d. The improvement in the business is hopeful,
but daylight still looks a long way off. A good deal of
the improvement this year 13 due to the reduction of
expenses ; but even if there were no expenses at all—no
salaries, no taxes, no repairs, no anything—it would still
be impossible to pay even 4 per cent.

The S.M. Herald is vigorous and venomous on
one point only—it loathes that great and magnificently
successful example of State enterprise, the Maoriland
Government Life Insurance Department; and, when it
gets on that subject, the old, dead paper shins out of its
grave in real earnest, and takes its tombstone and its
coffin under its arm, and goes forth like a raging lion.
Last week it published a bitter curse on the Maoriland
Department for having so much of its money invested in
safe Government securities, and tells of some alleged
person at Wellington who, as a consequence, is surren-
dering his policies in disgust and “ leaving the colony.”
The Herald actually said that this individual is leaving
Maoriland because the Maoriland Government Insur-
ance Department has invested a lot of money in
3 per cent. State securities. And yet when the Herald .
come 3 across a privately managed office at its own
door, where the whole income for a recent year didn’t
cover working expenses, and though it has, in its own
city, a life office whose funds are mostly represented by
the funds added, on pap'qr, to the alleged value of its
building, these things don’t worry it to any extent worth
mentioning. The only life assurance Office -which rouses'
the Herald to fury is the one that charges the lowest
premiums, gives large bonuses, shows the lowest expense
rate, save one, among Australasian life offices, and the
solvency of which has been consistently free from all
breath of suspicion. But that office represents the de-
tested policy of State Enterprise, which is anathema.

The Broken Hill mines are getting along
somehow—working when they can, and knocking off
when they can’t. There isn’t enough water available to
keep them all going; so some of them take turn and
turn about. Every few days the papers announce that
some mine or other has “resumed work”; this simply
means that it is up to that particular mine to breast the
bar and get a drink, while some other mine falls back
into idleness. Only the B.H. Prop, is working much as
usual; of the others the British keeps going best. No
rain has fallen there—-there.was a .temporary dampness in
the air and that was all.

Messrs. Gillespie Bros, and Co., of the Anchor
Roller Flour Mills, Sydney, have placed an order with
The Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne, for the construction of a modern semi tandem
horizontally coupled triple expansion surface condensing
engine. The engine will be capable of indicating 450
horse-power ; the cylinders being 12", 19" and 30" diam. ’
respectively, with a stroke of 48 inches, and will be fitted ;
with specially designed inlet and exhaust steam valves on ■
the Sultzer principle. This engine will form another
excellent example of modern high class engine construc-
tion. to the credit of the builders, who have already
attained a well deserved reputation throughout the
Commonwealth for design and construction of this class
of work.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
Started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOJin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

To empty old abandoned shafts, or to cope with
inrushes of water at a minimum cost, may be effected by
the use of the Vertical Plunger Sinking Pumps, manufac-
tured by The A. S. Cameron Steam Pump Works of'New
York ; and represented by Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, who hold stocks of these and various other
sizes and styles of steam or air pumps. These pumps are
distinct from any other make of so-called “Cameron’
Pump. They work automatically, and have no dead
centre, neither have they springs nor outside valve gear
to become deranged. They occupy less space in a shaft
than any other Sinking Pump of same capacity.*

After six or seven years’battling with adverse 1
elements, Mount David (N.S.W.) has at last got into
smooth financial water. Co. has now a credit balance of
over £lOOO, clear of all liabilities, and, according to news-
paper reports, there is sufficient payable stone in sight to
keep the battery running for nine months. How sorely
tempted the directors must be to declare a dividend—
even though it be only 3d. per lt is only in this
way that the few patient shareholders will ever get any-
thing out of the concern. The sharp market obstinately
refuses to be guided by, favorable pars., and the value of
shares remains stationary. Mount David is one of the
worst things N.S.W. speculators have experienced in
recent years; certainly it ranks next to Turon River
Dredging Co.

Poor Turner, on his office stool,
Insists that one and two make three ;
I laugh at such a stupid rule,
And scorn such drab monotony.
(I sometimes make the answer four,
And sometimes just a trifle more.)
Dull Turner, on his office stool,
Says one from two leaves always one.
Such nonsense let us ridicule,
Let’s make it more—it’s better fun.
(Or if you’d rather have it less
That can be managed, too, I guess.)
Grey Turner, on his office stool,
Says three times two are surely six.
That’s good enough perhaps for school,
But not for Dry-Dog politics.
(The answer’s either five or eight—
’Twould take too long to calculate.)
Drab Turner sits upon his stool,
And puts his figures through the mill;
I travel as the fakir who’ll
Make figures answer at his will.
(Heigh, Presto 1 Surpluses are here 1
I make a pass—they disappear 1)
I pity Turner on his stool,
He has no soul, poor honest chap ;
While I—I am the merry Fool
Who travels with the'bells and cap.
(Oh yes I Oh yes ! roll up, walk in—
Our Moral Show will now begin.)
S.H.P.

In a certain State a public official has
been suspended, on account of an ingenious
arrangement of mirrors in his office which reflected
the stockings Of girl clerks going up and down the
adjacent stair. Whether he was suspended on
moral grounds, or because the reflection of the
stockings made him neglect his work, hasn’t
transpired yet.
The obituary column in the daily paper is
now a recognised training ground for poets. In
the Age of a few weeks back a tribute to the lost
one concluded as follows :
And while I hope his spirit rests in peace,
I know my love for him shall never cease ;
Endeared to me by his kind, loving ways,
No word of mine is too good in his praise.
(All rights reserved.)
Actions for infringement of obituary verse copy-
right are looming in the distance.
• * * *
A burglar with a sense of courtesy has
turned up at Auckland (M.L.). Mr. L. D.
Nathan, merchant, whose house had been broken
into, advertised next day, asking for the return
of a bunch of keys which had been taken along
with the booty. In response he received the keys
and a number of small articles which the burglar
found of no use, together with the following note:
Sir.—Am exceedingly sorry to have created such dis-
order, but ’tis fated thus. U will find keys and sundries
in hedge near main gate. .. . Entered 12 p.m. Exit
I p.m. — I remain, sir, William Sykes, a small actor in this
drama of life, though not, I must say, a favorite with the
audience.
A Redfern (Sydney) poet displays in his
hairdresser’s window a “poem”on the execution
of Grand and Jones, Printed copies are one penny
each. The opening verse:
On the I7fch of July nineteen hundred and three,
Digby Grand and Jones met their death by Nosey Bob’s
dexterity
He greased the rope and tried it, and tied it round their
craw
And sent them off to Kingdom Come to justify the law.
The barber considers the effusion splendid, and
makes vigorous efforts to push sales.
• * * *
“John Carew ” writes :
Re the agitation for the release of Ethel Herringe : She
was a young woman of rapid susceptibilities. While
wearing mourning for a dead cohentingenter, she
accepted the clandestine attentions of a live cohentin-
genter, in whose public-house she was a domestic.
Without any apparent conventional reason she sprang a
marriage ceremony, accompanied by a holy man of the
wrong sort, on him, in the dead of night, at the point of a
revolver. When he declined to acquiesce in the unusual
arrangement, Ethel perforated him with three bullets.
Although, as a medico observed, “ he had the constitu-
tion of a horse,” the victim died in three days. Many a
horse would have died in less time than that. Ethel,
favored by the pleading of George Reid and the summing
up of the most sympathetic Judge on circuit, got a
verdict of manslaughter, and a sentence of two and
a half years. The subsequent Grenfell petition for her
immediate release was coolly received in Oowra, general
opinion being that the dead man hardly had a fair Show.
Even the tardy infant failed to evoke enthusiasm, babies
having acquired a knack of being born in such alarmingly
undesirable places that the circumstance it devoid of
novelty.

’ The howl that goes up, whenever a de-
frauding importer is fined, about “men of
acknowledged probity and reputation being
turned into criminals by a nefarious Act ” makes
the man in the street tired. If the reputation of
the average importer was to meet his true
character in the street they wouldn’t recognise
one another.

Hiphen: The Bulletin asks (18/7/’O3)
“Have we no soul above beer?” —this apropos
of the Tocsin publishing a sketch of the Eight
Hours memorial at Melbourne
Trades Hall with an advertise-
ment for beer writ across its
base. But that is not even
a circumstance! Last
Saturday I saw floating
in the breeze above a
St. Kilda pub, opposite
the football ground, the
flag of old England, that
has braved a thousand
fights on sea and land—
the flag on which Kruger
tried to wipe his whis-
kers, and failed; with
the same familiar legend
in big white letters across
it, “Drink Ale”!
My blood boiled, and I
would have pulled down
the flag, and cast the
pub into Hobson’s Bay,
and dealt severely with
that disloyal pub-monger
—only I had paid my
tram-fare, and besides I
know he is a large size in
publicans. Also, I still
owed him for the last
beer, and it didn’t seem
any of my business, and
ray religious principles
forbid the use of violence
to an erring brother—es-
pecially a violent brother.

“David”: At the
N. S. W. Public School
Teaohers’ Conference I
noticed that the prevailing
type among the school
ma’ams was a long upper lip.
Among the female sex the normal
to long upper lip predominates; the remainder are
short and shorter. Why should not the female
school teacher show the same variety. Do the
short-lipped ones lack the teacher’s ability, or do
their lips grow with their salaries, or are the short
lips married young ?

"PUT NOT A PERIOD TO PLEASURE!”
DAUGHTER: “ I’ve been engaged, to him nearly
two years; don’t you think it’s time I married
him?"
DADDY: "Oh, I don’t know; if you really care
for the fellow let him be happy as long as
you can.”
A Salvarmy man has been writing for the
War Howl a description of his voyage to Eng-
land. An interesting extract re Port Said—that
Sodom and Gomorrah of Egypt :
The deck of the vessel is changed into a kind of busi
ness street. Passengers gather round the salesmen ready
to make bargains. . , . jNorisitthe least interesting to
watch the sleight-of-hand men go through their tricks.
They are atjle men in this particular line. Live chickens
are made to do all kinds of amazing things. One chick
becomes (wo before one’s eyes ; and we gaze in wonder-
ment. The chick then lays an egg ; then out of its
mouth comes a gold ring. The next move is—we see it
no more.... Like the flight of a spirit, it is gone. The col-
lection box is passed round, and before fresh groups of
admirers the tricks are repeated. Oh, that t hese men
were under the influence of the Christian faith !
That is the only bit of snuffle in the whole narra-
tive. The sight of tho collection box being passed
round evidently woke the Salvationist from a
trance, and he promptly snuffled. There isn’t a
word about any desire to gather in the hard-work-
ing and probably honest Arab coal-lumpers, but
the desire to convert the man who could make one
chicken into two and get valuable jewellery out
of a fowl was overwhelming.
* * * *
As repulsive a “ charitable ” institution as
there is in Australia is the Old Man’s Depot, at
Mt. Eliza, outside Perth. It reeks with
memories and suggestions of lagdom.
The building itself, or most of it, was
built for convicts by convicts; only
lately the bars were removed from the
windows and the heavy bolts stripped
from the doors. But, though the bolts
and bars have gone, the old atmosphere
is there —it couldn’t be anything but a
prison. Of the 300 or so inmates, more
than 50 per cent, are “expirees,” and
most of them are men who didn’t make
an effort to shake off'old associations
and habits. Their principal recreation
is talking of early exploits, real and
imaginary; and when the stock of
home-made lies runs out, the few who
oan read rejoice the rest with extracts
from the goriest of Deadwood Dick
yarns. Inside the premises a contiuual
warfare is carried on with vermin, and
the vermin more than hold their own ;
for pretty well every time that one of
the old hands breaks away, and stays
out for a week or two—and it often
happens—the army is replenished. The
old ramshackle building has to serve as
a hospital, too ; among others, there are
cases of cancer there. Altogether, the
depot fairly well represents the last
dregs of Swan River convictism. Per-
haps the worst feature of the whole
business is that, compelled to mix with
the sinful old ancients, are a few who
deserve better surroundings to die in.
* * * *
“D. Harum” ; Woman is slowly
discovering that she is not such a timid
soft little thing as she has in the past
imagined herself to be. Igo home from
the city _ (Adelaide) by a special horse-
car, which stops neither to pick up
passengers nor to drop them, every day at 1 o’clock.
We always have on board three lady clerks who
do the “ drop off,” and very often the “jump on.”
We business men look forward with pleasure to
either performance. There was a fourth lady
clerk once, but she got tangled up in her skirts
one day, fell, and barked her knee-cap. She hasn’t
ridden on the “ special ” since.

Melbourne has got a name as a musical city princi-

Clermont (Q.) lately developed
a lunatio who possessed a mania for
climbing trees. When arrested by the
police he owned a sum of £6 3s.
lid., of which £2 12s. was deducted
by the authorities for doctor’s fees,
travelling expenses, rations, and wear
and tear of tree. The time will yet
come in Queensland when the drunk
will be turned upside down and
shaken, and all property that falls out
of him will go to reduce Cribb’s deficit.
“Trevvy” : Re Salvarmy par in Bul-
letin (27/6/’O3). At “night of prayer” once
in Broken Hill one soldier seemed unable
to attain the hallelujah stage of perfect
bliss that characterises the “saved.” He
appeared unable to remember any great
sin he had committed. At lastj with a
great effort, he gave his experience,
and stated that his wife, who had
five children, was not his wife—he had
never married her. He said he felt
better after he had told them. But
when he went home and told the lady,
she gave her experience, and her elo-
quence was so glowing that the neigh-
bors threatened to send for the police.
There is no encouragement nowadays
for a man to confess his sins. I believe,
by the way, that this individual was
legally married, but the most successful
Salvarmy man is the one who confesses
huge iniquities done in his unregenerate
days, and for a man with a bald imagina-
tion who can’t think of many sins it is a
difficult position.
* * * *
In Pahiatua (M.L.) there has been
formed a Bald-Headed Club, an anti-
labor, or more strictly an employers’,
organisation. The avowed object of
its members is to get their hair cut at
reduced rates on the ground that they
have less than the average amount of hair
to cut.
The people who study astronomy, in the
Government House Domain, Melbourne, had a bad
time of it the other week. The Field Engineers,
who have their depot on the Yarra bank, invited
all their best girls and relations to inspect their
war apparatus, and exhibited among other things
the wonderful powers of the searchlight. The
stream of light was switched from point to point
with great rapidity, and as the operator directed
its rays on the most likely spots many an inter-
esting hug was interrupted. Visitors who followed
the searchlight saw other men’s wives and other
women’s husbands flying in great disorder and
endeavoring to establish an alibi behind trees.
But for the neglect of the engineers, who forgot to
send a force round to cut off the retreat, there
would have been many interesting identifications.
The tale of a mean street is generally told
in a police-court. As an instance two rather
draggled people named Tidy wandered into a
Melbourne court last week to get morally dis-
entangled. They had been married two months,
and the lady complained of being left without
nourishment. The lady had been married three
times before, but it was the gentleman’s first
attempt. She said there was no furniture and
that he brought the horse into the house. He
said that she had first proposed to him, that she
drank, and that she struck him in the eye with
•beep’s entrails. A distracted bench told them to
go back and squall at each other for another
month, and then, if things were no better, to
bring up their untidy wrangle for further con-
sideration.
AN AUTUMN DAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The glory of God in the morning is over it all—
The blue of the sky, and the pile of the fleecy white
cloud,
From the sentinel mountains, so beautiful, stately, and
tall,
To the song of the magpie, now trilling and calling aloud.
The glory of God in the morning is over it all
Ere the dew is yet dry, and the emerald slopes are a-blaze
With a million white gems, when the orchard leaves
flutter and fall,
And the Earth is at peace in the Autumn’s soft, wonder-
ful days.
From the haze of the ranges, the stretch of the far forest
trees,
The murmuring sound of the river’s soft echoing fall.
The Earth is at peace in the laugh of the soft, sighing
breeze—
The glory of God in the morning is over it all.
vio.
KATHLEEN WALKER

“ALL MEN ARE FREE!"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ All men are free and equal born
Before the Law 1 ” So runs the worn
And specious, lying, parrot-cry . . .
All men are tree —to starve or sigh ;
But few to feed on Egypt’s corn 1
There toils the sweated slave, forlorn ;
There weeps the babe, with hunger torn—
Dear God ! Forgive us for the lie:
“ All men are free 1 ”
That man’s a player, this a pawn ;
One’s born to fustian, one to lawn.
Were they born free ? Were you ? Was I ?
No ; not when born, but when they die
And stifle up the churchyard’s yawn,
All men are free 1
N.B.W.
S. ELLIOTT NAPIER.
YES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What If her lips answered “No 1"
Did nought in your heart make you guess
That the blush and the sparkle and glow—
That all of the woman meant “Yes ” ?
There in the sweep of her form,
Fool, saw you not, vision-dulled,
The yield of the tree to the storm,
The rose leaning out to be culled 7
“ No ’’ was the answer you heard ;
“ Yes ’’ was the light in her eyes ;
“ No ” was her meaningless word ;
“ Yes ’’ was her pulsing and sighs.
Fresh as turned earth and as good,
Red as the vine from the press,
Was the warmth of the rich womanhood,
O dullard, that said to you “ Yes 1 "
N.S.W,

The great snaffling habit seems to be worse
with the Yankees than even with the British.
While. President Roosevelt was blustering about
the massacre of Jews in Russia his own Grand
Jury was bringing to light in the Southern States
a system of wholesale negro slavery with flogging,
torture and sundries, which system the same
august body declared to be “ worse than anything
in Siberia.” Wherever the white race comes in
contact for any great length of time with the
black or the yellow, the white is demoralised.
The white man either becomes a weakling or a
tyrannical brute; very often he becomes both.
Probably the yellow and black men also degener-
ate, but that’s their affair. The point Australia
has to note in regard to its own colored labor
Broblem is that in the Southern States of the
r .S.A. white men of the same origin and blood
as ourselves have been reduced to a frightful state
of moral irresponsibility. They have become
white savages, armed with the weapons and
appliances of civilisation, using the courts of
justice to perpetuate slavery, and given to the
public burning-alive of the weaker race. Roosevelt,
by the way, might also remember that there has
never yet been any contradiction of the statement,
made openly and without shame in the American
papers, that M-Kinley’s assassin was tortured to
make him answer interrogations, and that this
was done with Roosevelt’s knowledge. Even the
Czar never had an accusation like that levelled at
him.
* * * **
An old Sydney home, Cleveland House,
has become the property of the Good Samaritan
Sisters, and will in future be used as a Refuge. The
old house was a wonderful structure, the cellars
being a marvel of strength, with bolts and bars
strong enough to stand a siege or keep in check
the most violent “assigned servant” the “system”
ever produced. Captain Johnstone (of Annan-
dale) took up his residence there once with his
bride. For some years the Government rented
the house as a labor bureau.
**y • •
Pressure of opinion from a few railway
servants, and lack of any opinion at all from the
great majority, have caused the Executive Com-
mittee to drop the scheme of perpetuating the
memory of late N.S.W. Chief Commissioner Eddy
by the erection of an orphanage open to children
of railway officials. The policy of the State has
declared against dragging up children herded
together in an institution, and the supporters of
the boarding-out system, led by Hollis, M.L.A ,
and Dr. Mackellar, M.L.C., succeeded in anni-
hilating the project at the eleventh hour. The
£2500 granted by the Government is to be
returned, and the orphans now on hand are to be
boarded out while the committee gets its second
wind.
* * * *
Judges, both of the Supreme and District
Court, should keep themselves up to date in the
matter of prison discipline. A couple of Judges
admitted, the other day, that they knew nothing
about the inside of prisons. In allotting a term
of imprisonment the Judge should take into
account the varying penalties imposed by the
prison authorities. Nearly every year there are
changes in prison management. “Solitary” is
increased or lessened, dietary scale raised or
lowered, and so on. The average Judge has a
general idea that he is sentencing a man to be
confined for a certain period, but whether he is to
be confined in a drawing-room or a cellar he
doesn’t know in the least. Nor, possibly, does it
dawn on, him that ten years’ confinement with
skilly isn’t the same as ten years with turkey.
i** • *
A wail from the Australian Churchman
(“low-church” Anglican):
What with these vain shows of altars and candles and
crucifixes, and of banners of mythical meaning and
tawdry crosses which are followed by our “learned”
clergy and “ intellectual ” laymen, is it any wonder that
poverty is. creeping over our church ?
Well, the church says that poverty is a blessed
state, but the poverty which arises through
“blewing”all the money on flags and rags isn’t
blessed—nor is it dignified.
• • • ft •
In N.S.W. a good cook would laugh at the
remuneration offered to a nurse. Witness the
rates of pay given in the Government depart-
ment. At Coast Hospital the head nurse gets
£BO a year, the senior up to iB6O, the probationer
down to £25. The hospital cook gets £66 a year.
In the Lunacy Department, asylum attendants
get from £l6O to £93, servants £l4O to £67, nurses
from £9O to £63. In the Charities Department
the attendants get from £135 to £96, nurses from
£9O to £65. The chief training schools for nurses
are always striving to raise the standard required
for a certificate, and the course gets more exacting
and expensive each year. The nursing profession
might reasonably expect the State to set a better
example in the way of salaries.
* * * *
Ballarat Chinamen are busy sending back
their old derelict relatives to the Flowery Land.
Questioned as to why, a Chow said, “Him travel
cheaper than bones.” Corpse railway freights are
four times those of live-passenger rates, and
corpse shipping freights are almost prohibitive.
“ K.K.” : Live in the suburbs. Can’t leave
a spade about without running great risk of losing
it. Have to stay at home when the rest of family
is away or will return to a garden that has been
prospected. Seems as though the rogues keep the
honest folk in gaol.

Bent, the mad mollah of the Irvine
Government, was not longfin quenching his thirst
for big headlines in the papers after being
shunted from the railways to the Public Works
office. He hadn’t been quit of his old depart-
ment a week before he got off a few silly “ideas,”
chief of which is the pulling down of Melb. gaol
and the conversion of the land into business
building sites. Thomas regards the gaol as merely
a “drunks club,” his alleged inquiries having been
insufficient to inform him that the drunk is not
stored there, anyway, but kept at the city or
suburban lockup during his few hours’ incarcera-
tion. Melbourne gaol is for the “ short-timer ”of
various degrees, and as that half-and-half sort of
criminal has to be accommodated somewhere,
Thomas Bent’s plan is presumably to erect
another gaol somewhere else, which would be even
more costly than the present institution by reason
of the fact that prisoners would have to be con-
veyed there in daily “ Maria’’-loads instead of
being just walked into the gaol from the
court next door. Also, if Bent is under the
impression that the present gaol site would
sell at all readily, he must be dreaming one of his
ancient boom dreams. It is right away from the
commercial centre, and nobody happens to be
putting up new business premises anywhere in
Melbourne just now, the reign of Bent and Irvine
having stagnated things worse than ever. As a
sample of Bent’s vigorous public works policy,
this and his other undigested “ideas” about take
the bun for crass stupidity.
• • • •
Either the craze for medallions of cele-
brities is about played out, or Melbourne people
took very little stock in the late Pope Leo. The
medallion merchants hastened to put photographic
buttons on the market as soon as Leo’s early
demise was predicted, and there were fully a
dozen men selling them on the day the gold-and-
white flag was half-masted at St. Patrick’s.
Initial price asked was a shilling, but within an
hour the vendors recognised that they were not
likely to clear much money at that figure, and
they came down to sixpence. A couple of days
sufficed to bring the papal souvenir down to the
modest “thrum,” but even that reduction brought
few buyers, and the day after the Cathedral
requiem Leo’s countenance was going at a penny
a time.
* * * *
Mr Monty Arnold, who for 13 years has
drawn from N.S.W. a public pension of £192 17s.
per annum, presided at a meeting of the Stockton
Coal Company last week. He said that the co.
during the last five years had paid away in wages
alone £202,176, during which time the shareholders
who found the capital to employ “King Work-
ing Man’’ had received only £9006. “The
remedy ” said Mr. Arnold “lay with the share-
holders who should return men to Parliament who
would see that the mine owners received some
little consideration. As matters now stand the
men can do exactly as they please, but if matters
are allowed to go on as they are now, there will be
not only nothing for the owners but ere long there
will be nothing left for the men either.” In other
words, according to Monty Arnold, it is owing to
socialistic legislation* that the Stockton people
have a bad mine. But Mr. Arnold should speak
more respectfully of people who have taxed them-
selves for 13 years to pay his pension.

A Charters Towers (Q.) paper, in seeking
to enforce its lofty contention that “it should be
one of the recognised principles of State that men
in high positions should at least be sober when
carrying out their duties ” :
Queensland is notoriously the drunken man’s Paradise,
In this State men in the very highest positions show
themselves in public places in a condition of drink for
which, if they were any other men, they would be promptly
arrested. How they blunder through the duties entrusted
to them is little short of marvellous. And how State
authorities close their eyes to delinquencies of the sort is
scarcely understandable—probably it is because the State
authorities are very often in a similar condition of “ Oh,
be careful,” and the pot dare not call the kettle black.
Queensland has been in that state for many year;'.
The historic drunks of a certain Acting-Governor',
and his struggles to find the right place to sign
official documents, and his rage when he saw il x e
documents simultaneously, and the liquor he spilt
over important public papers, and the waylaige
matters of State, when he had done with them,
smelt like a bar-rtotn full of curses and the smoke
of twopenny cigars, are joyful memories in Biis-
bane. There are many other strange and whis-
kified memories up there, but that one stands up
like a tower.
Again—this time from Wangaratta (Vic.)
—comes the story of the unaccountable boy who
saw two other boys drowning and didn’t mention
the fact. They were three small brothers. One
was drowned, another hung on to a sapling with
only his head out of the water for an hour and
a-half till rescued by a passer-by; and the third
brother went home to dinner, leaving them there
and saying nothing. If the silence of the Australian
boy at & football match was anything like this
other silence of his, football would be a better
game than it is.

BLIGHTED.
TOMMY: " Ah, Micky, that
woman’s changed the whole
course o’ my life !”
“J.F.D.”: I wish Dr. Ross, or some
other able Parliamentary jackass, would introduce
a Bill which would compel umbrella manufacturers
to build ginghams minus the score of project-
ing rib points. A woman pulls her umbrella be-
hind her, not over her, and the points catch in
any loose pieces of catchable skin a man may have
in his possession. The woman unhooks with a
frown at the man’s stupidity, shakes off the bit of
his ear clinging to the umbrella, and goes on her
way until some other fool of a man is hooked.
But occasionally one hen’s umbrella catches
another hen, and great is the joy thereof.
Whafc a whiff of the unchanging atmos-
phere of the Vatican comes in that quaint cable
message which tells how the news was given to
the world that the first two ballots for the election
of a Pope had failed. At four minutes past 10
the Cardinals met in the Sistine Chapel. At
11.15 dense white smoke issued from the chimney
connected with the stove where the voting papers
were burned, this being due to the addition of
damp straw. It indicated that the first ballot had
been resultless. In the afternoon another ballot,
and this ended in smoke, too; so, and only so,
the world knew there wasn’t a Pope yet. There
are not many doors shut against the reporter
nowadays; but the press has still to stand outside
the Vatican and read what it can out of the
ohimney-top.
* *- * *
Nobody of repute can die in this world
without some sentimentalist heaping bathos on
the event with a shovel. A few days after the
death of the Pope, the cable sent along the tear-
ful yarn that, prior to the dead Pontiff being
placed in his coffin, his faithful followers, the
Noble Guard of the Vatican, individually kissed
the poor dead feet. The news sounded nasty but
humble, and the world, in a general way, let it pass
without much comment. Then a few days later
came the further intelligence that the Noble
Guard declined to watch any longer beside the
Pontiff’s remains because of their decomposed
state. That sounded nasty and proud, and spoiled
the effect of what had gone before. There was a
lack of stage-management in letting the sordid
complaint of the Noble Guard get into print, and
the Guard itself showed a crude and shocking
tendency to publicity. It ought to have breathed
its trouble secretly into the ear of a Cardinal and
got the sordid details fixed up without taking a
whole globeful of heretics into its confidence.
* • • *
Melbourne Board of Shirks lately spent
two whole afternoons discussing the virtues and
the misdemeanors of a high-collared clerk, at £9O
a year. He was a very dilatory young man, it
seemed, and had been reprimanded for being late
in the morning no less than 28 times. Even then
his superiors didn’t robustly fire him out, but
merely suggested that he send in his resignation ;
and his masterly inactivity so appealed to the
gradual Board that it only let him go by a small
majority after much wrangling. And so much
time had been spent in struggling with the
question of the small clerk who was late 28 times
that the question of the pipe combine, and the
exorbitant price it charges for pipes, had to be
hung up indefinitely.

Among Hebrews who “ stick to their
religion” civil divorce is not recognised unless it
is confirmed by a religious divorce from the Beth
Din of the Synagogue. This religious divorce is
named, with sweet and swift appropriateness,
“Get.” There is not much trouble in getting it
on grounds that warrant a civil divorce, but
heavy fees have to be paid to the Beth Din.
Then the un-coupled can “get” in peace.

Discoveries in the world of chemistry and
of physics, now crowding forward, seem to indicate
the need of a very con-
siderable audit of es-
tablished ideas in the
near future, when a
lot of supposed ‘ ‘ facts ”
will have to be “written
off.” Radium is a huge
puzzle. It is a metal
which is radio-active —
emitting rays having
heat and convertible
into light ra.vs —with-
out any outside stimu-
lus, and, seemingly,
without any loss of bulk
or change of character.
It is claimed for it, in
short, that it is Per-
petual Motion which
is, according to all es-
tablished ideas, impossi-
ble. The search for low
temperatures isexciting.
Liquid air is now com-
' ' monplace, and Professor
Dewar is working steadily
towards the “absolute
zero.” At that point, it is
surmised, all chemical and
molecular forces will cease to
act, and atoms be at rest for
the first time since Life began.
It is questioned as to whether
matter, deprived thus of all
energy, will be matter in any
sense, even to the extent of
occupying any space.

N.S. W. Education Act
provides that clergymen of
any recognised denomination
(and you can be a recognised
denomination in N.S. W. about
as easily as you can be an
actor) shall be permitted to
attend the State schools and
explain the inwardness of
Jonah and the whale to
such children as belong to
their denomination for an
. hour each day.... The clergy
carefully avoid taking
advantage of the facili-
ties offered. Their totrl
visits to the schools only average eight per year.
There are about 240 school days in the year, and
in only one of 30 does a parson or priest of any
sort attend to give “spiritual comfort” to the
youngsters. The painful absence of any material
for a collection (a school-boy has even a better use
for his buttons) improbably the reason of this.
NIGHT SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wander abroad over hills and passes,
Drinking the cool of the coming night;
Softly treading the waving grasses
And hollows deep in the fading light.
Eyes still fixed on the lessening far-light,
’Neath haggard limbs of the gum-trees tall;
Onward still in the gathering star-light,
Shining jewels in Night’s dark pall.
Clear is the track through the deep fern hollow,
The Cross shines bright in a cloudless sky ;
While overheard, as I onward follow,
Harshly laughing, the mopokes fly.
The night wind weeps in the hills and passes;
Her trailing robes of an ebon hue
Brush in her track the sleeping grasses,
Bending low ’neath the weight of dew.
A faint sweet scent in the air still lingers—
The spicy breath of the flowers of day ;
A withered gum-tree with ghostly fingers
Stands like a spectre to bar the way.
Onward still in the darkness tender,
Treading the hills and the hollows deep,
High overhead the pale moon slender
Keeps her watch on the world asleep.
