A WOMAN’S LETTER.
My.D.a* Aram.— S7d " er> Aosust *■ lm
Lord Richard Neville and Mr. Robertson
Clarh, representing the Gov.-General and the
btate hx., attended ceremonies at St. Mary’s
Cathedral in honor of the late Pontiff Leo XIII.
and, as they were in uniform and had a “good
place near the catafalque, under the collective
eye of some 200 priests, they couldn’t very well
slip out to see if the weather were going to clear
i U P‘, k° w they and the Lord Mayor and the
horde of Consuls who were present will pray
that the next Pope will not die in their life
time ? The ceremonies began at 10 o’clock and
ended at close upon two. Some eight or ten'
women fainted. No flowers were used, and deco-
rations consisted of a few miles of mourning sashes
draped from column tocolumn. Chopin’s Funeral
March, played by the orchestra, and Cherubini’s
famous Requiem,” sung by a choir of 100 voices,
were the musical features of the High Mass, and
the Head March from “Saul’’played the 5000-
strong congregation out of the cathedral, after
Archbishop Kelly had preached a panegyric on
trie departed Pope. Will the day come, I wonder,
when the death of an illustrious person of good
habits will be celebrated with joyful musicand cere-
monies. Death, according to accepted notions,
means promotion, and we should publicly rejoice
in the deceased’s good fortune. At present, the
passing hence is invested with such terrifying
ceremonies, that it is no wonder one is afraid to
die.
Sydney suburbs, those within 10 or 15 minutes
tram ride from the city, are full of boarding-
houses, and many of these boarding-houses are
nearly full of women—old women, middled-aged
women, and spinsters no lcjnger young—any-
how, women who have not to earn their living
and on whose hands time hangs as heavily as an
anchor. These are always ready for a free show,
and generally get there before the doors are
opened. A Mayoress’s reception at a Town Hall
is a joy to them, and so is such a ceremonial as
the Pontificial Requiem. Probably they like
ecclesiastical functions better than Mayoral. How-
ever they departed from their usual rule and got
up to breakfast (the woman who lives in a board-
ing-house and is not compelled by business to get
up mostly breakfasts in bed) and were at the
church doors by 9.30. Consul for Spain Freehill,
who was one of those in charge, reminded me of
Bishop Hatto in his mouse-tower, so beset was he
by Suburbia’s boarding-housers wanting good
coigns.
On no less an authority than B. R. Wise the
panegyric by Archbishop Kelly on late Leo XIII.
was “one of the most scholarly and reverential
addresses ever heard in Australia.”
The opening of the new Her Majesty’s theatre
'Y a ® as brilliant as the burning down of the
•i u ' i * wo G ‘ overno * s > bemedalled and be-
ribboneu. with Lady Tennyson wearing a white
brocade coat, and Lady Rawson in amber evening
gown, were jn the manager’s bqx, and every now
and then Robertson Clark or Lord Nevill would
come into the picture from the. background. Mrs.
J. C. Williamson in black crepe-de-chine em-
broidered with black butterflies, and Mrs. William

And most everyoneof whom everyonehas heard for
reasons of riches, or fine clothes, or good looks or
good positions in Society (or close to a till with-
out a cash register) was in the 2000 audience. In
the front row of the stalls with Mr. Jack Dailey’s

just returned to Australia. Mrs. W.B. is the one

young man, slight and dark. A late member of
the House cf Commons, now in Sydney, tells of
hearing Sir Michael Hicks-Beach read (some
three years ago) his budget statement, and
with grim humor in an aside to the House
congratulate lmnself upon the handsome sum
which came to the State through the death of
an American gentleman named Smith ” This
man lived frugally at a London club. His heir
was the young man who bought the first seats sold
for opening night of the new theatre.
The house was full of party groups. The Bar-
tons (mother and daughter), the Coleman Burkes
the Burdekins, and Miss Irene Marks helped to
keep the crowd from looking dowdy. Mrs Nor-
man Menzies, with white lilac behind her ears
was there. Miss Nellie Stewart’s six seats were
given to friends, for Nellie herself was at the first
night of the “Midsummer Dream,” robed in black
frona head to heel and waving an immense black
ostrich-feather fan.
There were some humorous incidents at the
the new Her Majesty’s seats, Sydney
for the opening night. When the auctioneer
didn t know abidder, he made the money safe by
requesting him or her to “come round and
settle.” About one in thirty had got this in-
vitation when Mrs. Menzi’es (Dorothy Yane)
made a'bid and gave her name. “ Will you please
come round and settle ? ” said the auctioneer, who
didn’t know the lady and thus argued himself un-
known in the theatrical world. Afterwards Mr.
T. A. Dibbs, manager of the Commercial Bank,
was a successful bidder: “Name, please ? ” “Mr.
T. A. Dibbs.” “Name, please?—l can’t hear!”
‘ Mr. T. A. Dibbs ! ” “ Oh, I wish you people
would speak distinctly. Stand up and let us see
who you are.’’ Mr. T. A. Dibbs stood up in all
his Roman-nosed majesty, and the auctioneer
apologised profusely.
J. C. Williamson’s six months old infant was
chronicled by a Melbourne paper amongst the list
of guests at official opening of the new Her
Majesty’s Theatre (Sydney) last week: “Miss
Aimee Williamson ” was in her nurse’s arms, but
the designation sounded very grown up. What a
new terror will be added to modern life if it
becomesafashion to chronicle baby’s appearance in
public. Why, ever so many mothers would then
come, to all daylight functions at least, accom-
panied by a nurse carrying an animated bundle of
lace and ribbon and yards of embroidered skirt.
It has been a Williamson week. The actor-
manager, the inauguration programme at his new
theatre, and the presentation to him by his com-
bined companies, have filled the public ear and
eye. The presentation was a silver punch bowl
and two Corinthian column silver lamps. Last
look as if they ought to burn before some Grecian

Sultan of Johore and aide-de-camp Dawd were,
through the collision of their vessel Argus with the
Mildura, driven back to land and the Australia
Hotel. They remain in Sydney for another week.
Captain Dawd is very proud of himself as a Bene-
dict, and confesses to liking Melbourne next to
his own native pied (no pun) a terre “because he
found a wife there.” He married a fair Australian
girl a beauty of the footlights—about a fortnight
a £h- The aide is much smaller and a couple of
shades darker than his Sultanic majesty.
Lady Macleay, whose death occurred at Sydney
last week, was a daughter of the late Deas-Thom-
son and a granddaughter of Governor Bourke.
Another death of the week was that of Mr. James
Ewan, a large station owner and chairman of the
City Bank. He was a brother-in-law to Dry Dog
Reid.
An unusual amount of pocket-picking went on
at the Maoriland football match. Some years
ago Mr. Byron Moore, the V.R.C. secre-
tary, showed me a photograph of the Mel-
bourne Cup—taken close upon the psychological
instant when the horses were nearing the post. It
showed a back view of the intent orowd pressed
against the lawn palings and the camera had
‘caught’’ two pickpockets, red-handed so tospeak.
One had his hand in a man’s coat-pocket, theother,
in a crouching position, was evidently operating on
the watch andchainofamanwhostood with his field
glasses to his eyes. Curiously enough a policeman
showed out quite close to one of the pickers, but
he was standing on tip-toe with all his senses con-
centrated on the horses.
When I quoted from “A Chelsea Menage”
National Review) a fortnight ago I did not
know the writer; Mrs. E. T. Cook, had died a
few weeks earlier. That article was the last thing
she wrote. Her “Bride’s Book,” divided under
the heads of Romantic, Idyllic and Domestic, is a
most piquantly written set of essays in which wis-
dom and knowledge lurk among clever persiflage
and good* humored raillery. “ Should Wives have
Professions?” and “The Year of Honey” are
especially attractive chapters. In the Times’
notice of Mrs. Cook’s death it is mentioned that
all the Baird girls “ married men of note.” Mrs.
Cook s husband was editor of the Westminster
Gazette. A brilliant Balliol scholar married
another of the tribe. Miss Dorothy Baird, the
creator of Trilby and original of the well-known
pictures “Wedded’’and “Solitude,” became the
wife of young actor Irving; and yet another mar-
ried our B. R. Wise.
The ex-Crown Princess of Saxony, who caused
so much trouble lately by eloping with
a tutor person, is being treated fairly well. She
has been allowed to keep the infant daughter born
after her flight and is living in a chateau in
j ranee. Her other children are allowed to visit
her on occasions, which is better than being
bricked-up alive. e
Is there any law to protect the birds that build
and breed in the Sydney Botanic Gardens ? Ap-
parently , any small boy who pleases may destroy
nests and steal eggs with impunity. Fdr days I
watched two tomtits building in a fir tree, until
the nest was nearing completion. Then, one day.
I found that the nest had been torn from the tree
and was lying broken on the ground. It was
s.ieer wanton mischief, for no eggs would be found
so early. Last Spring, I saw three blue-wrens’
nests destroyed in the same way. To many
visitors to the Gardens the birds are just as great
\ Pleasure as the flowers and trees, and they
shou.d be protected against young savages.

The person who made use of the horse simile in

“ Marshall Hall is probably the most exacting
conductor whoever waved a baton in Australia
Curing the present year the tall ‘ Prof.’ is officiat-
ing over the Melbourne Liedertafel in August
biede s absence, and he is so determined not to
allow any performance until the society has
thoroughly mastered its work that its concert
programme has been quite disarranged. Mozart’s
Requiem Mass has been in rehearsal for six
months past. Two minor musical lights schooled
the Liedertafel at the earlier rehearsals, after a
dozen of which the time was considered ripe for
Marshall Hall to come along and put on the
finishing touches. The first time he heard the
Requiem, he started pulling his hair out by the
roots and distributing strands of it among the
astonished amateurs, what time he informed them
that they were singing, like k lot of ‘bottle-oh’
men, and desecrating the memory of Mozart.
Since then the Liedertafel people have grafted
like Trojans for four months, but they have
started wondering whether they will ever have
nother concert.
• 7, Th P exodus of blood and bone from Victoria
in the form of the stalwart sons of the State has
raised a problem for the women. There stretches
out on all sides the long, sad- line of surplus
females, and there trail off on every outgoing
steamer s. track the most desirable males. What
are our girls going to do? The most sensible if
somewhat undignified, course would be to pack’up
a wardrobe instanter, and, with kit on shoulder
to follow the brutes to their newer fields.

ot it. Girls, get aboard a lugger and the swain is

“ Why do so many who get treated in private
hospitals give their illness as a sprained ankle ?
Mrs. Evelyn Gough, of Melbourne, now pro-
poses to seek election for a seat in the Senate.
The lady is fully qualified for the position as far
as self-confidence goes. She is holding meetings
to test the public feeling in Melbourne.

“There is joy in Art circles at the return of
Bernard Hall to his Art Gallery directorship. He
took a trip Europe-wards when his health was
poor, owing to grief at the loss of his charming
V. • Abroad he set zealously to work, and
friends have been apprised from time to time of
the fruit of his inquiring into the training systems
current at all the. best English Art schools.
Bernard Hall is a stimulative guide to students at
the Gallery. It is a pity that the slie-students
cannot bring their work up to the standard of the
less sentimental males. The average she-novice
f D ii «is a slow, mediocre, unillumined drudge,
full of good intentions and bad performances.

“The florists were making a display of wattle
week, what time the chiffon houses
> into Spring shows. What stood out in one
busy establishment was the spot craze. Veilings,
laces, nets, muslins, voiles, foulards —all showed
small dots. The so-called Irish linens are to have
a vogue, and so is lettuce-green. The cool color
appears even in the sprouting season’s gamps.
That is the climax of the craze, for not even the
most exquisite and perfectly-done complexion—
one guaranteed quite weather-proof—can risk the
sunlight that streams through a sharp green
parachute.
“Melbourne’s Mayoress publicly placed her
sympathy on the side of the complaining she by
appearing at a swagger Town Hall evening, the
other night, garbed in a warm wintry out-door
dress buttoned to the line of the ear-ring. And
the Mayor followed suit enveloped in Polar furs.
Lady Silverlocks, bare-necked and satin-clad,
drew a feather boa closely round her throat and
hooked over her cloak, but even then seemed to
feel the cold currents of air swishing aoout her
ears. The girl who knows a thing or two now,
when going to the Town Hall in winter, wears a
warm costume and a long, nice cloak to cover it
completely.
“ A former singing favorite, who now sits high
and dry in the past, is making a list of the names
of all debuting on the Melbourne platform this
year and noting hoiv many times each renews her
debut. The lady has a certain bitterness of soul
caused by a fickle, vacillating public through
many years of concert vicissitudes. Legions of
vocalists storm the city halls, many reappear time
after time as novices, and few succeed in holding
the fort for longer than a brief day. So the faded
singer, whose glory tracks are all covered up by
the imprint of multitudinous new-comers’ feet,
smiles with acid satisfaction as she notes the
trumpeted arrivals and the muffled platform exits
of the vocal army. 1
. “ Bridge is turning some of our smartest worqen
into hawlc-like card-sharpers, and sending not a
few to the dogs—or, rather, to the Jews, ‘Some
months ago, when the story leaked out of a dame
of a fashionable quarter south of the Yarra raising
a loan from one of the tribe of Benjamin on her
household effects, on account of Bridge losses, the
rumor was discredited. But since then various
other stories have been afloat, and some gain
credence. The Bridge gamble goes on in some
drawing-rooms till the small hours of the morning.
Then there is a settling of accounts, a recording
of debts, and play is resumed probably the next
night.
“‘Man never is but always to be blest.’ A
Melbourne girl, who, in the parental home, had to
turn up her sleeves every day of her life, was fond of
saying 1 give me servants and I will never grumble
again.’ She married, and went with her husband
to a station where she had control of a married
couple, housemaid and Chinese gardfener. For a
week life went smoothly. Then the housemaid
confided to her that the married couple were
* thirsty ’ souls. Young Madam lectured them
and left the kitchen. Presently, a blood-curdling
dm was heard, The housemaid rushed in
pale as death, shrieked that the couple were
murdering the Chink, on suspicion of tale-
telling, and fell at Missus’s feet in a fit.
The harried matron tore to the kitchen, where
Ah Sam was making noise enough for 20 Boxers,
while his assailants threatened him with an axe.
When that row was over the boss happened home,
and ‘sacked’ the couple. A few days, and the
housemaid again rushed in and went into fits at
the bedside of her grass-widowed mistress. This
tinie Ah Sam was the trouble. Pleased with
Trixie’s defence of him against the married
couple, he had started to give her ‘plesents,’ and,
as she explained to her surprised hearer between
her yells, when a Chinaman starts to give pre-
sents to a white woman, next thing he does is to
claim her as a bride, and, when refused, he ‘ takes
the chopper to her ! ’
Unaccompanied singing is becoming the
vogue. At a St. Kilda corroboree the other
day, a local amateur whistled her symphony and
gleed solus. At the crowded Orpheus Concert on
Thursday, a feminine quartet tootled minus
instrumental help, to the apparent joy of a
friendly audience.

difficult to some.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The death of Pope Leo leaves Archbishop
Murphy, of Hobart, with the longest record as
prelate in the R.C. world.
Kingston’s health is such as to cause great
anxiety to his friends, more particularly as he will
never spare himself, no matter how ill he may be.
It is the musical Miss Saumarez Smith,
author of many hymns and plaintive songs, who
married the Primate’s chaplain in London. Her
husband, the Rev. Griffiths, takes a position
there.
Mr. “Bill” Lyne, Minister for Home
Affairs, is now in his sixtieth year. Twenty-
three years of his life he has spent in Parliament,
and it is difficult to see that Parliament is any the
better for it.
Acting C.J. Stephen, of N.S.W., retires
to England when C.J. Darley returns. The
Acting C.J. is nearing 75. He became Associate
to his father at 17 ; and has been thus a court
fixture for nearly 60 years.
Sydney Evening News firmly resents
Brother-in-law Henniker Heaton’s title of “ mem-
ber for Australia.” It asks in its leading columns
who gave him the title, and why ? Why not offer
a half-crown prize for the best answer ?
The oldest minister in Adelaide is Rev.
F. W. Cox (Congregationalist), who, although 87
years of age, still walks sturdily about the city and
every morning, directly after dawn, goes for a
“constitutional” trudge several miles in length.
The murdered Queen of Servia was a
woman to the end. The last thing she did before
the butchers broke in was to write a message to
her dressmaker telling her that she hadn’t a thing
to wear, and must have the new gown she had
ordered by Sunday.
Old identities gone over during the week :
P. L. C. Shepherd, M.L.C., native, aged 72
years; James Ewan, philanthropist, aged 60
years; Francis Mooney, 82 years; John Hawks-
ford, aged 75, in business in Pitt-street, Sydney,
for half a century ; C. A. Scrivener, aged 72 ; R.
T. Barnes, of Balmain, an old salt, 85 years ; Mrs.
Ellen Blacket Goodman, relict of a former Under-
secretary ; George Russell, of the late firm of
P. N. Russell and Co., aged 83; Robert Shank-
land, aged 71, formerly of Thompson and Giles.

J. C. L. Fitzpatrick, M.L.A., begins to
take a high place among N.S.W. Assembly bores.
He has put about sixty queries to Ministers
already this session, and has not yet evoked
any useful information or elicited a fact which
was not already known to the crows.
Melbourne charities are seemingly in luck
through the will of the late George Thomson, of
Challicum Station. Testator, who died three
years ago, left a life-interest in his property to his
widow and two sons, the charities to get the re-
version, and already the sons and widow are
deceased.
Archdeacon Tyrrell, resigning from Mait-
land (N.S.W.), has been presented, on the verge
of 8t>, with a new dinner service. Most men of
that age want new stomachs. The Archdeacon is
a nephew of the Bishop Tyrrell who cultivated
flocks and herds, and bequeathed much wealth to
the Newcastle diocese.
Recently deceased Donald McNicol, aged
91, the oldest pastoralist of Camperdown (Vic.),
arrived in Victoria in ’39, and subsequently
resided at Camperdown for 63 years. Brother
Duncan lived to be 93, and brother Peter 85. The
habit of remaining a dreadfully long time in one
place was rooted in the family.
William Wasteneys, Maoriland’s lawyer-
baronet, has gone bankrupt in London. Waste-
neys is one of those baronets whose names, in
books that deal with titles and ornaments, are
printed in small type, accompanied by an explan-
ation that the person in question claims the title,
but that there is no general enthusiasm on the
subject.
Abraham’s progeny are in the ascendant
in Adelaide. The Mayor (Cohen), the President
of the Chamber of Commerce (Jacobs), and
the President of the Chamber of Manufactures
(Lawrence) are all Hebrews, while Cohen and
Scherk moved and seconded the address in reply
to the Governor’s speech in the Assembly the
other day.
Governor Rawson’s gift of a Challenge Cup
to N.S.W. cricketers has roused fully a score of
other sports to place their “fine old English
games ” under the vice-regal eye. “ I’m waiting, ”
said a functionary of the establishment, lately,
“for a letter from the secretary of the Cock-
fighters’ Club ; and, by the way, is there no Dog-
fighters’ Club? ”
Miss C. H. Spence, who for just over 50
years has been the best known woman in the
political and literary life of South Australia, is
seriously ill. Forty-nine years ago she published
her first Australian story ; but for many years
past she hasn’t touched fiction—the little time
she could spare from works of philanthropy she
has given up to advocating her amiable pet hobby,
“effective” voting. The recent death of her
brother, J.B., chairman of the State Bank, and
her companion for 75 years (Miss Spence cheer-
fully owns to 78), was a stunning blow.
Professor Harper,
Sydney University’s Pres-
byterian ciack, was a Vic-
torian civil servant for
years. He quitted that for
journalism. He left jour-
nalism for the post of school-
master. He gave up that
for the clerical line, and
now expounds theology.
As he is still vigorous, he
may yet give up theology
for medicine, or engineer-
ing, or the banana trade.

with tit. Joan of Arc, who

Church moves with geologic
slowness. That is one
source of its power. A
Church that waits 300
years before beginning to look into a small
matter shows a calm, stolid, overwhelming con-

Ex-Premier George Republican Dibbs
should write his reminiscences. He entertained
a company the other evening with word-pictures
of his twelve months in the debtors’ prison at
Darlinghurst. He kept a record of his visitors,
totalling something like 4000. Then, as now, he
was a favorite with ladies, and his cell was kept
well decorated with flowers. He had a lathe, as
he has now, and among other things turned out a
presentation cup for Henry Parkes, which duly
went to theßankruptcy Court assignee.
The statue just unveiled in front of Ade-
laide University to Thomas Elder, who died six
years ago, has taken long to complete. Elder
gave £lOO,OOO to the University, £25,000 to the
Art Gallery close by, £36,000 to various charities,
and £25,000 for a special fund for building work-
men’s homes. The statue is the work of A.
Drury, R.A. Of the three panels surrounding
the pedestal, the most generally interesting is one
showing a string of camels in the desert, to recall
the fact that Elder, at his own cost, equipped the
Warburton, Gosse, Giles, and Lindsay expe-
ditions. He was a noted yachtsman and breeder
of racehorses.
John Carrufch, aged 30, born blind, re-
covered his sight, about two months
back, in the Glasgow Ophthalmic
Infirmary, through an operation by
Dr. Ramsay. This is the first case of
the kind on record outside the Scrip-
ture. Carruth’s experiences of the
new world, to him, are, of course,
intensely interesting. He picks up
the nature of things quicker than
scientists would anticipate, but he
was exceptionally intelligent as a blind
man. He felt as if he could touch
the clouds, and was overwhelmed with
the loveliness of everything. He
had formed no idea of what water
was like, to look at.
“Larry” Harnett, N.S.W’s
beautifully-frilled Sergeant-at-Arms,
is now 61, and looks in the forties. The
general opinion in the ladies’ gallery is that
he should be Premier.
An old identity died at Eudunda (S.A.)
last week in the person of “ Dr.” Thomas, a
nomadic healer, who had the reputation of having
been an army surgeon in the Crimean war. He was
85 years old, and had made the round of country
towns right through the State for over 30 years.
Rumor said that he once killed a man in a duel,
and so had to flee from civilisation.
The lighthouse-keeper at Cape Wil-
loughby, Kangaroo Island (S.A.), died after a
short and sharp illness on June 28. He had
become so attached to his lonely life that his last
request was that he should be buried on the
island. Despite hard times the Jenkins Govern-
ment complied with this desire, and sent an
undertaker thirty miles to bury the body.
J. L. Nanson, leader of the Westralian
Opposition, has resigned that thankless job,
partly because the Morning Herald, of which he
is editor, eats up too much of his time, partly
because he has got far ahead of most of his dull
crowd on half-a-dozen leading questions. Nanson’s
personal policy includes the abolition of the Leg.
Council, State control (to a great extent) of the
liquor business, a graduated tax on the unim-
proved value of land, and, pretty generally, the
adoption in other respects of the Maoriland land
laws. The Westralian Opposition is largely made
up of country members of the old type—the type
that shudders when a land tax is mentioned—and
Nanson concluded at last that the leadership of a
party carrying all that dead weight was no job
for him.
“ WHERE WILL HE GO?"-ANXIETY AMOUNTING
TO TERROR.
The late P. L. C. Shepherd, M.L.C., was
a Sydney native, born at Shepherd’s Paddock,
which was a nursery on the Newtown Road
opposite the University. In the seventies the
nursery was cut up and built upon, the unearned
increment being productive of much wealth. Mr.
Shepherd at one time had a seat in the Assembly
for the Nepean. He camped in the Upper House
for 16 years.
Andrew O’Keefe, the Victorian contractor
who built the Outer Harbor railway, Adelaide, has
been taking a look at the country along the proposed
transcontinental railway route. The 90,000,000-
acre land-grab has attracted him. Even now he
is a big landowner, what with the Yamma station,
in the Riverina, and a deal of property in
Seddonland; so, if he happens to get the
90,000,000 acres, Pierpont Morgan, perhaps, will
shake hands with him.
J. A. Andrews, who died of phthisis in
Melbourne Hospital last week, was, perhaps, the
most philosophical of the men whose agitation has
brought the Labor movement in Australia to
its present pitch. Andrews had no sym-
pathy with any spasmodic law-breaking,
and one of the most ignorantly cruel things
that the N.S.W. Dibbs Government did
was to gaol him on a technical charge on the
ground that his teachings were dangerous.
Andrews was a deep thinker, a good if somewhat
too solid writer, and had an indomitable energy
for propaganda work. At one time he cut out a
full set of wooden types to print his pamphlets on
Anarchy.
The officer whom the Victorian Treasury,
in view of the falling due of the five million loan,
has sent to London to talk
to Cohen and Co., pawn-
brokers, about the great na-
tural resources ofthe country
is T. W. Gaggin.
The name suits
the job, any-
how, even if
the man
doesn’t.
THE WORK AUSTRALIA HELPED
IN S' AFRICA.
One of Bent’s blacklegs—an ex-cohen-
tingenter, of course—was heard from the other
day as defendant in a maintenance case in a
Melbourne Court. Though still earning Bent’s
“princely wage’’ he had persistently left his
wife and child to starve. He was loyal to the
Hempire and extra-loyal to his Government, but
his loyalty stopped short at his own home.
Farm-burner, blackleg, wife-deserter —what a
record!
W. J. Maxwell, sculptor, Edwardstown
(S.A.), is dead, aged 61. Arrived in Melbourne
in ’75, and. four years later went to Adelaide.
Amongst his principal works are the statue of St.
Patrick, and altar and font, St. Mary’s Cathedral,
Sydney; and a statue of Burns for Adelaide.
After finishing a statue of Industry for the Ade-
laide Savings’ Bank, he undertook a commission
for the M'Douall Stuart statue, but this had not
been commenced at the time of his death.
Died at a considerable but uncertain age,
Benjamin Leopold Farjeon, novelist. The books
of reference seem to preserve a strange silence
about Farjeon’s .birth-date, but he put in a little
time in Australia and then went to Maoriland,
where he helped to start the Otago Daily Times
in ’6l. For many years he resided in England
laboriously turning out third or fourth-rate litera-
ture, so, though he has been claimed as belonging
to this part of the world, his connection with it
was very temporary at best. *,
“Lieutenant-Colonel” Reay, whose chief
merit as a journalist is that he doesn’t drinkj has
been made a trustee of the Melbourne Art
Gallery and Public Library. Whereupon he
publishes in the ’Erald a complete list of the
trustees. These twenty-five gentlemen are pre-
sumably the most cultured of marvellous
Melbourne, and the most competent to select
books and pictures for its marvellous citizens.
Among the art critics appears the name of
Thomas Bent. The idea of Bent selecting a
bright red and green work of art at the top
of his voice, and complaining because there
wasn’t more than Jib. of paint on the whole
dashed thing, is humorous.
A comparatively rich Melbourne citizen,
Edwin Hall, of Orient House, Glenroy, has died,
leaving £19,877. His will bequeathes ,£250 to
each of his children, ten shillings a week to his
wife, and £2 a week to another woman for the
maintenance of herself and a family of six “as
long as she shall remain unmarried or shelter any
man to be boss over my children.” If that last
clause of the ungrammatical testator’s will is read
literally, it certainly seems to state that the other
woman must keep a strange man in the house to
whack the deceased’s illegitimate family, or else
lose her annuity. The Bulletin didn’t'know the
deceased, but it would swear that he was strongly
against the engine-drivers in the recent strike,
and heartily approved of Bent. •

has found M.L. a happy hunting-ground for

now carts the boat over-
land for show in country
townships. His world-

prompted one Felix Tan-
ner to announce a similar
attempt in a barrel-
shaped cockleshell, 12ft.

sail. Tanner is an eccen-
tric genius, who once
followed the 40 days’

never fled owing to lack

a man up all right,
but didn’t break his

has been working at

counting seagulls for
the Harbor Board,
or something. '
Kingston, though
a lawyer, and a sound
one, has never cared for
lawyer politicians, and the
surprise is that he agreed so
long with the many lawyers of
the Australian Ministry. When
running S.A. he was sometimes the
only lawyer on his side of the House.
He has frequently said that he carri >d re-
forms because he kept away from political law-
yers.
Just outside Hobart a crank has set up
business as a hermit, but he doesn’t encourage
worshippers. He is busy on an alleged scheme for
for the transmutation of metals. Vandemonia
says that this is about the maddest new delusion
it ever heard of. V.D.L. being a backward place,
this delusion looks quite fresh there.
ERYSIPELAS.
“Four years ago that unsightly disease, Erysipelas, attacked me,” writes Miss Ellen Musto,
34 Flora street, Erskineville, Sydney. “My face swelled greatly and my cheeks and neck became
tender and inflamed. Then I lost my appetite. Indeed, my stomach was so upset that it threw
up almost everything I ate. I continually spat blood, too, and this naturally alarmed me. The
swollen flesh pained me terribly—the slightest pressure caused me agony. Although able to leave
my bed, I was too weak and exhausted to be of any use. At last I had to go to the Glebe
Hospital, but the treatment there did little good. I was told to have my teeth extracted but I
would not consent. I was in terrible pain when my mother persuaded me to try Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People. Some were bought for me from Mr. Limon, chemist, of the Glebe.
In ten days I was a lot better. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills purified my blood wonderfully. Then
they made rich, red blood for me and increased my appetite. They soothed the inflammation and
made the swelling go down. Soon I was able to retain my food, the blood-spitting stopped and I
was strong and well again. Two boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills completely cured me. Now
my skin is perfectly clear and fresh and my personal appearance has improved wonderfully. I
cannot recommend Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills too highly for diseases of the skin and blood.”

IN SEARCH OF FAME.
LITTLE JIM (in a stage whisper) : “I say, Joe,
let’s flatten ’im out, and get our names writ
in ther papers.”
In the little town of Cooktown (N. Q.) the
majority of the general stores are owned by
Chinamen, not to mention numerous little shops
that sell groceries only. The Chinese also own
every fruit shop in the town and all vegetables
and fish are hawked round in baskets by the pig-
tailed race. The chief laundry is run by a Manila
man and one of the jewellers is of a glossy black
hue. The only thing a white man can keep to
himself in Cooktown are the pubs, very near two
dozen of them. Most of them employ Chinese or
colored cooks.
“ Scotty the Wrinkler ” on rain problems :
I wrote to Mr. S. M’Caughey a couple of years ago
(like a gentleman, he answers letters) and to the Worse-
ship-fool Mayor of Broken Hill (who does not reply to
whalers) before the M'Carthy climax, re not rain-making,
which is rot, but rain-encouraging. My theory is that
you cannot inakz rain, but if the rain-clouds are'low and
passing as waste to the hills and coast, you may—only
may—nobble them.
The American attempt was not altogether a failure,
but the aluminium shells were worth £lO each. The
kites only go up in strong winds unfavorable to the delay
of the “cumulus” (cirrus and stratus are not much
good as rain-bearers). The Stiger Vortex is humbug. I
want concussion among, not below, the vapor, and like
the others, the initial cost is too much tor practice
without certainty. But my plan is cheap and has reason
on its face. Do you remember little tissue-paper balloons
which would carry a squib or cracker up to the housetops
by lighting a piece of tow or cotton on the cross-wires at
t ie bottom ?
Do you also remember Montgolfier being “ hoist with
his own petard ”? If a lOst. man can be elevated 500 ft.
by hot air, why cannot a pound of dynamite be sent up
a mile? And the whole cost of a balloon of varnished
paper would not be a crown. With a time fuse any one
co;dd, in two or three tries, estimate the length, and the
calculation of the lifting power is simply that the bulk
varies as the cube of the diameter.
When, day after day, we see the storm sweep by to
expend itself where it is not wanted and pray for rain, we
are like the boy who cried for the Moon. Now, I give my
brain-work in the matter for nix, but expect the system
to be named after me; say, “ Scotty’s Rain Trap's,” or
the “ Wrinkler’s Rain-Jammer." Virtue, notoriety, kudos,
fun and silver are their own reward.
Of course, my power would not be a plug of tow.
Methylated spirit burned as a “ uniformly accelerating
force ” (that’s dynamics) would probably do.
Anyhow, “ Ex nihilo nihil fit,” and I’d only shoot when
the game was there. We had 20 to 30 suitable days last
year and the year before and no rain. After my statue
a< a benefactor has been raised on the banks of the
'Bidgee we’ll sing with Horace.

“ Periwinkle ” : Know an enthusiast in
the honey line—Lawrence is his brand— who
resides at Georgetown (Tas.). He has a great
local reputation as an apiarist and bee-charmer.
He knows every one of his insects, and, when out
walking, can pick up and handle any of his bees
with impunity, but woe betide the slightest
familiarity on the part of a stranger. In fact,
however well he knows the pollen-hunters of the
family apiary, they appear to know him just as
well, and will buzz after him all the way home.
He sells bees, and has been known to recognise
“ prodigals ” that had returned to the parental
hive from their rightful owners’ apiaries—months
after. Ihe mutual understanding that exists
between the Georgetown bee-charmer and his
hobby is beautiful to behold, even at robbing
time ! Coming nearer home, I was interested—
financially and otherwise—in the hive of bee-
burglars who caused such a sensation in Melbourne
some years ago by breaking into a Prahran jam-fac-
tory and making off with a quantity of cochineal-
colored saccharine matter. It was ultimately
traced to the raiders’ den, transformed into crimson
honey. Few insects are more intelligent, toil
harder, and serve betterasanobject lesson generally
to the human tribe. Their communal system is
excellent; their horological capabilities are deve-
loped early—in fact, the average worker can tell
the thyme right from swarmhood ! To the bees
belongs the honor of having discovered the “thumb
imprint ” for identifying criminals, for it is notice-
able that when a strange bee visits an apiary, a
special bee officer is soon on his tracks, exami-
ning his imprints on the wax, and if he is
not registered as a member of the hive, he is led
to the door and given the ‘‘ to quit ” pass-buzz !
Once made a trustful bee awfully tipsy on the
dregs of a hot toddy glass that had cooled. Bee
sipped the nectar till half-seas over, and then the
fun commenced—no human ever looked so piti-
fully ludicrous. Then I put him on the hive
stand. Laboriously, in zigzag fashion, he crawled
to the opening, where he was instantly taken in
hand by a couple of sturdy drones. A few
m jments later a glimpse through the glass slide
at the back showed him sleeping in a cell! He
hadn’t been able to get bail.

“ Ses ” writes :
The statement (B. 2/7/’O3) that Casino (N.S.W.) is sur-
rounded on three sides by big stations, that come right
up to the municipal boundary, is hardly correct. On the
northern side of the river, selections, farms, &c., extend
for many miles beyond the town boundarv, north and
west; a narrow strip of Tomki run touches Irvington
(three miles below Casino), on the east, and it is hemmed
by farms and selections right down to the homestead.
This run (thanks to the late Mrs. Clarke Irving) has given
up almost the whole of its river frontage—something like
IOJ miles at one time—to farmers. Towards Lismore, the
first station is Bentley (a small place across Norton’s Gap),
the boundary of which is six miles out; up the river
(N.W.), the first is Runnymede, boundary eight or nine
nnies, a big area of which has been cut up into farms, and
the balance will probably be served likewise ; Kyogle
township has been built quite recently a few miles above
it, and that is completely surrounded by small holdings.
A mile of South Casino boundary was touched, eight
years ago (it may not be now), by'one paddock of AVoo-
roowoolgen run ; but, for miles along the river, that
station’s frontage has been cut into small farms and sold.
As to the Grafton-Lismore railway passing for 30 miles
through one holding, Wooroowoolgen is doubtless meant.
But that once vast run only touches the line at long
intervals. Deep Creek, one of its most important sub-
stations, is hemmed in by selectors, whose land touches
the boundary of the small horse-paddock. Camira, Bun-
gawalbyn (towards Coraki), and Gordon Brook (Clarence)
runs are also thickly studded with small settlements.

“J.P.”: Going to White Cliffs, I saw a
pack-horse bike—the first I have seen. The biker
had a huge swag strapped on, over, under, and
round his second jigger, which was attached to the
riding bike by a curious outrigger contrivance. I
really expect that a time will come when the wool
will be hiked in from the far-out station on huge
pack bikes, with a live bikist on each side.
“ W.” : A backblocker arrived at Eden
the other day, wearing snake skins round his hat
for pugaree, and boots made out of tanned goanna
skins, laced with strips of wallaby
hide. He was a patriot.

A letter from Sheol, by “ Vigilans ” :
The other day I arrived at Eucla (W.A.), which I
frequent periodically, and found things in an uproar.
Eucla is a repeating station situated in the Great
Australian Bight. The district is large, and the white
population sparse, niggers predominating. The operators
are not a happy family, and adopt a kind of “ I-won’t-
play-in-your-yard ” policy’. White women being few,
lubras are much in evidence. A steamer arrives here
quarterly from Albany ; there is also a coach running
monthly from Fowler’s Bay (S.A.). The chief industries
are hunting the nimble kangaroo and cutting sandal-
wood, and a man, 60 miles away, tries to raise sheep,
but owing to dogs, rabbits, and the scarcity of
grass this is not a paying industry. When the
steamer arrives from Albany, many of the kangaroo
shootists, sandalwood getters, biacks, and what
not, assemble at two humpies two miles from the settle-
ment, and pandemonium reigns supreme for five or six
days, in fact as long as the snakejuice lasts, when they
return to their respective camps, until the arrival of the
next boat. When I visited Eucla things w'ere very lively
indeed ; liquor was being consumed by the gallon';
whites, yellows, brindles, and blacks lay about in all
directions. No licensed house is found here—which is a
pity

“Jimmie Pannikin”: A Tibooburra
blackfellow, who lately returned with his boss
from Adelaide, was asked what he thought of the
saintly city. He had not seen a capital before.
Said he, “ Dis Tib’burra too much blurry dirty
country. Dat vulla Adelaide like clean white
tchirt, my wud.”
“ W.J.A.’s ” enthusiasm :
Let me strike the lyre concerning the bush-musician.
I was staying at the Oxford Hotel, Bourke, when an
itinerant individual arrived, per foot, from goodness
knows where, and played the piano daily for his “tucker.”
His favorite piece was a military march, which I had
never heard be-
fore or since.
He would strike
up—the treble
loud the bass
scarcely audible.
One could dis-
tinctly hear a
military band-
pipes shrieking
and drums beat-
ing behind
came the tramp
of feet. -.Almost
i mperceptibly
the treble would
die away, the
bass becoming
correspondingly
louder. The
band had
marched past,
its sound was
gradually dying
away in the
distance, and
rhe battalions of
infantry were
coining nearer
and nearer. By-
and-bye, they
would reach us.
The bass was
then strenuous
—one could hear
the tramping of
feet and the
jingle of accou-
trements. The
diminution of
sound was 60
gradual that it
lequired no
stretch of imagi-
nation to realise
that an armed
battalion was
steadily march-
ing by. I could
see them! Dusty
they were, but
with feces brown
and, stern!
Whilst they were
passing, and ere
the last gun had
lumbered up, an-
other inspiriting
band was heard
in the distance,
coming towards
us. Uncon-
sciously we
would forget the
men and guns,
and with
strained ears
listen. They
were coming-
corn i n g—com-
ing ; one could
be almost
tempted to go to the door and look expectantly
along the road. As they came, we would suddenly
discover that the tramping men had got quite a
distance away— nothing but a faint tramp, tramp,
tramp, and an occasional rumble, could be heard. How
we would pick up our feet as the inspiriting music
became gradually louder. It always gave us a priok-
ing sensation. He stayed with us a couple of months,
and then, picking up his bluey, he disappeared over the
horizon towards the setting sun and Madagascar.
THE ANCIENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O father of all hoary-headed chestnuts
Who minglest with our midday meal a million
Of aged quips and worn-out repartees,
Think you that jokes, like wine, improve with keeping?
00, rub the cobwebs from your puns decrepit
Nor dream that we, though we applaud from pity
(11 e laugh but at the mere idea of laughing),
Have never heard your musty old traditions
W hich in the graveyard of forgetfulness
•Should long ago have laid their weary bones.
Mark how he crams his gullet with provision
And swamps it with the dregs from out the billy,
Drains off his pannikin, and, gulping quickly,
Wedges some battered patriarchal jest in
Betwixt the crannies of our conversation.
“See here,” he cries, “hark now, and clo3e attend me !
I have,’ he says, “ the most amusing story—
Ila, ha ! By Jove, you know it’s really funny !"
(lie never notes the air of jaded boredom,
The weary r , harassed look that seizes on us,
I he mutual sign of fellowship in suffering
That passes round among us.)
“ Ila, ha ! You know—ha, ha 1 I die with laughing I
Ila, Ha ! Ahem. Well, once I knew a fellow—
(You 11 laugh all right; just wait, the point is coming)—
W ho asked his boss to give him half-a-day off
To go to see his grandad’s funeral ..."
And so ad infinitum.
Eve, in Eden,
Said once to Adam, “ Husband, such a story 1
I heard it from the jackass just this morning ;
So funny ; and so new , dear; are you listening?”
And Adam leaned his back against a tree and
Bent his tired ears a half-yard further forward
The while she brightly babbled the ancestral
And white-haired, wrinkled, senile pleonasm
That {vide Darwin) came down from the monkeys—
A long-inherited family possession.
One day, when William lumbered in his mirth,
A lady came to beg our thrippences
To help along the Deaf and Dumb Asylum.
Then rose in bitter wrath our meanest member :
“ The deaf,” he howded aloud, “ the. deaf I envy I
But here, behold, take all of it —a sixpence
I give to the encouragement of dumbness !" ~ ,
When wild amaze at the unwonted offering
Had scattered, and our dazed wits were restored,
We turned upon our ancient bore in fear
Lest he might have dropped dead in sudden shame.
He calmly added, “ Well, as I was saying .. 1”

THE OLD J.P.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
See him walk up the street, with his nose in the air,
None so pompous, so proud or so haughty as he,
When he dons his tall hat and carries his gamp
He thinks he’s a duke, does the old J.P.
He sits on the Bench with a marvellous frown,
Though the case is as simple as simple can be,
And, though he can’t read, yet he puts on his specs
When he serves out the law, does the old J.P.
Watch his eye how it glares on the poor old drunk,
“ This time is the third you were brought before me,
I’ll fine you a week, or a pound in the gaol 1 ”
Then he goes and gets tight, does the old J.P.

“ J.B.N. ” : I can well un-
derstand how annoying “Jig-
gers ” (Bulletin, June 20) must
find the awful zeal of the trades-
people he has come in contact
with. But perhaps he wouldn’t
have remarked on it had he ever
been served by the tradespeople
of Cooktown. Here you go into
a shop and ask to see a certain
article. They go and bring out
one, mostly a very poor speci-
men too, and you ask if that is
the only one they have, “No,
we have more,” and with much
reluctance they bring out one
more. In another shop you
knock for a quarter of an hour,
and then the missus, a meek-
looking, worried woman, appears
with a broom and a plate and a
dish-cloth in her hands, and tells
you that “Pa” has just gone
to the pub at the corner to have
a drink. In a bookseller’s shop
you inquire for a certain book.
He hasn’t got it, and he makes
no effort to sell you anything
else. They have a habit in Cook
town, when you ask to see an
article, of inquiring first whether
you want to buy it. Unless
you are quite sure that you want
to buy it they regard the weari-
ness of lifting it down as best
avoided. If it is an article that
weighs over 71b. they always
prefer to describe it to you.
If it is somewhere where you
can walk to it conveniently
of course the position is different. But they hate
lifting things.
* * • *
“ Periwinkle ”: Strikes me rather forcibly
on a tender spot that the inherent vandalism of
the Australian youth often gets encouragement
at the seat of learning! Bulletin “par-fixers”
have written glowingly on the object-lessons
pupils have to undergo in the way of maps, cow-
sketches, etc., etc. This week an ancient spinster
at the local school gave an object lesson to the
infant class, the subject being the bee. The
youngsters could understand all about the insect’s
honey-gathering propensities, and the sting of its
business end—but they couldn’t remember how
many legs and wings the “ flower-kisser ” had.
Soothe spectacled old vandal told each pupil to
bring a bee to school next day. Consequence—
forty little to_w-headed ruffians were chasing over
the countryside, mangling, maiming, and killing
every honey-hunter they came across. Result —
40 bees at school next day, mostly minus a wing,
or leg; about 150 damaged insects missing from
the hive-roll-call, and an object lesson in refined
brutality. _ Would to goodness it was legal to
dissect this old maid !
* * * *
During the Maori trouble in M.L. years
ago a party of sappers had been working for days
towards a fortified pah. When they had got
about half-way a native appeared with flag of
truce. “ What do you propose ? ” asked the captain
in charge. Next moment there was a half-fainting
officer sitting on tho ground.’ The native's pro-
posal was that, as the British were too slow and
incapable, the Maoris were willing to come out
and undermine their own pah for the enemy at ss. a
day.

“K.K. Barbara Sutherland’s remon-
strance with Bulletin artists for invariably
depicting busbies as the wearers of short trousers
and long beards seems to show that the bush has
not given up the follies of fashion from convic-
tion. Long trousers in the country are as ridicu-
lous as long skirts anywhere. But a woman
everywhere must show either her feet or her
vanity.
* * * *
“Tika”: Weasels threaten to become a
curse in M.L. Can’t understand why no idiot has
yet introduced snakes to keep down the rats, or
tigers and leopards to provide shooting for tourists
in the extreme north. If there had been no devil
in the Garden of Eden I believe Adam would
have imported a pair and turned them loose to
breed. We badly need an Undesirable Animals
Bill.
* * * •
“ Deeby ” : Amongst the curiosities of
bush-waggon nomenclature I once came across
“ The Black Bird ” painted bright green ! Also
“The shamrock so green” illustrated by a sprig
of the sorrel in gold leaf ! Just imagine a yellow
shamrock ! Yet this carrier was no Orangeman,
but a pugnacious Tipperary person, who would
resent any aspersion cast on his national vegetable
with his fists and both feet.
* * » »
In spite of the “ general rains ” and
“complete break-up of the drought” which the
Sydney papers have been reporting during July,
a full third of N.S.W. had under an inch, and
that mostly in ineffective splutters of a point or
two at a time.
The most free-and-easy gaol
on record was the Thursday Is-
land establishment under
“ Jimmy ” Simpson’s regime.
“Prisoners” could roam where
they liked during the day, but,
according to a notice on the
gaol-door, any of them not in
by six o’clock would be locked
out. Only one defaulter was
known, and what part of his
carcase the ’gator couldn’t digest
was picked up on the beach
some days later. Long Monty
Dunn, of artistic fame, put in
a few months as warder there.
He would occasionally take out
a prisoner with him for an
evening’s spree. One night
Dunn’s companion could not
get him to come home, so he
went himself. Jimmy (inside):
“ Who’s that knocking ? ” Pri-
soner : “No. 15.” “Where’s
the warder?” “Down at the
pub.—can’t get him home.”
Jimmy (severely): “Go after
him again, and don’t dare to
come back without him.”
* * * *
“D.H.R.” : At Walgett,
a few days ago, the police pro-
ceeded against one James Taylor
for having an incorrect travel-
ling-stock permit in his posses-
sion. Defendant was in charge
of 15 horses travelling from
Bourke to Narrabri, and when
he arrived at Walgett he had
16 head. Evidence proved that
the man had privately purchased
a horse en route, and had had
no opportunity of getting the
document alteied. The receipt
for the animal was produced ;
but the Bench found the charge
proved and fined the drover 10s.
What a magnificent row there would have been if
Kingston had prosecuted an importer on so paltry
charge as that!
Every man knows a good use to which
some other man may put his money.
The director of Adelaide Zoo has made
the important announcement that one of the
married zebras is in an interesting condition. If
the little event comes off all right the foal will be
the first of its species born within the limits of
the Commonwealth.

....Industry: The amount of loan money spent during
Reid’s term of office was about £7,000,000. If the
Ministry always came in exactly at the end of the
financial year when the accounts are made up it would
be possible to give the figures more accurately. This is
not including revenue deficiencies. The revenue showed
up thus : June, 1895, surplus £19,494 ; June, 1896, deficit
£607,523; June, 1897, deficit £207,367; June, 1898,
surplus £5473 ; June, 1899, surplus £10,676 ; net result a
deficit over the whole Reid period of £779,247. This
is not taking account of two broken periods of a few
months each in ’94 and ’99 The Runner: Not much

Paragraph from S.M. Herald of last
Friday:
The twenty-year-old son of a farmer at Horbling, Lin-
colnshire, has just accomplished the remarkable feat cJ
clipping 100 sheep in a day. He was engaged about 17
hours from start to finish.
Fancy going all the way to England to borrow the
“remarkable” story of someone who shore 100
sheep in a day, and bringing the narrative all the
way out to this land of Billjim ! Does the Herald
know a sheep when it sees one ?
“P.T.F.” writes :
Some people have hard luck. This happened t’other
day in the rain. He was unemployed and “ broke,” and
had been foodless for 24 hours. Then he “ put it on ” a
friendly person for a “ sprat,” and got it. Clutching the
heaven-born coin firmly in his hand, he made an excited
bee-line for the nearest cheap food-shop, a glorious vision
of roast beef and spuds going before him. Crossing Pitt-
street he cleverly dodged a tram with fully 2J inches to
spare, but the effort caused his hand to relax. Now that
“sprat” had the whole Commonwealth to drop in, and
it was surely the quintessence of hard luck that it should
drop on the tram-rail. I thought him a pathetic figure
as he stood there amid the rain and the traffic ruefully
regarding a flattened-out piece of metal, and the vanish-
ing vision of a dinner that might have been.
But where was the pathos when a sympathetic
person like “P.T.F.” was there to give him
another sixpence ?
THE EXTREME PENALTY.

Letter received by a respectable N.S.W.
citizen, who wants to know if there are lunatics
about: —
Dere Mr. , I heerd you was out stroking with my
Melia Anne on Saterday night, having met her at the
raleway station just before the male train left the station,
and her been under the age of consent, having been born
on the 14th of August, wich makes her 16 on the 14th of
August, coming 1903, for wich I mean to make you sit up
for. Taking a young girl out to the cemetery, wich ain’t
a fit place for Melia Anne, who was born a twin at 2
o’clock in the morning on the date, the other poor little
girl been dead, and Saturday night been a dark night
and Melia Anne been under the age of consent, my poor
dead husband what ain’t cold in the grave if he was alive,
having died through having eat too much poisoned sar-
dines, for wich the docter wich was a quack couldn’t cure
him, he having been pretty good with his hands having
one time beat Dick Davis, wich was the best man at
Bullocky’s Bend, he would make you sit up, and Dick
Davis what we called Nosey Dick because he had wort on
his nose, wich my ole man called a beauty spot, it having
made Dick wild, if he had hadent eat too much of the
poisoned tin-fish, wich having no dinner on that date it
made him hungry, and he died quite peaceful! and happy
while the quack pumped him with a stumick thing they
call a pump, and he having said me ole man was full of
beer, wich was a lie, the sardines having poisoned him at
12 o’clock at night on New Year’s morning, he was buried
at the Bend, and Dick Davis went to the funeral as best
man, leastways he helped to carry the coffin, wich was
heavy, my poor dead husband having been 14 stone
in his socks, if he didn’t have eat the fish he would smash
your ugely jore for having taken Melia Ann out on Sater-
day night, wich was a nice night for a young girl under
the age of consent, wich is 21, to be stroled about at the
cemetery, wich no young girl should be took to, and me
a poor lone widow wich had the twins on the 14th of
August, at 2 o’clock in the morning, aint going to have
my poor little girl what is too young to go out taken out
walking by a counter jumper like you, what aint fit to
take no decent girl to the cemetery, wich you did. I will
take proceedings against you, and if Dick Davis comes
over here, for wich I mean Melia Ann to marry when she
is over the age of consent, he will make you sit up. lam
your obedient servant, .

David Harum : “J.F.D.’s” par. on eggs
and a restaurant (Bulletin 23/5/’O3) is reminiscent of an
experience of mine in an Adelaide hasn-house. Saw on
the board outside, “Ham and egg, 6d.,” so went in,
ordered, received, and ate same, which was “one slice of
am and three heggs,” a; the curling-pinned waitress
classified them. Put a shilling on the counter as I went
out and waited for the change. “That’s right, sir,” said
the woman with the booky’s bag. “’Am and heggs you
had, that’s a bob.” “But out in the front, ma’am, it’s
lum and eggs, 6d.” “ Ah, yes, ’am and hegg, 6d.; but ’am
and heggs is Is. See, you ’ad ’am an’ heggs. Hegg ~ is
plural.”
“ Wiljar”: In my part of the Mallee there is a
storekeeper who, by reason of his never-rush-’em policy,
is considered exceptionally “ white.” He is reputed to
have over £BO,OOO on his books, and to-day is down so low
that the “ stock ” (exclusively for “ good marks ”) is kept
mostly under the counter. He made the mistake, in the
first place (about 12 years ago), of advancing as much as
£l5O in a lump sum to men whose only security was 610
acres of (leasehold) unimproved jungle, and the cocky,
instead of wiping off' the debt—there were a few
moderately-good seasons about that time—in most cases
increased it. If it hadn’t been for that blunder (made
through a blind faith in the Mallee) there wouldn’t be a
parish or two already mortgaged to him, some of the
blocks in which, if sold at the impossible figure of 20s. an
acre, wouldn’t clear him. The Mallee isn’t worth selling
up just now, but next year, if Russell’s forecast holds
water, most of three counties will be under the hammer.
* * * #
The Arbitration Court learned something about
the joys of mining at Broken Hill. One man had been
poisoned by lead dust, several times he had been partially
suffocated by fracteur fumes, and once by charcoal fumes
(from the smouldering fire underground), and he had
developed a lung trouble through the violent changes of
temperature. Explaining the last trouble, he mentioned
that, whereas the temperature in his stope (working
place) averaged 91 degrees, it averaged 70 degrees just
outside—in the place to which he and his mates had to
retreat when shots were fired.
* * » *
An Adelaide sharebroker found himself some-
what in a fix the other day. His office boy, after reading
some hundreds of penny-horribles, cleared out from
home, with (after paying his fare to Melbourne) just
2s. 9d. in coppers and small change in his trouser
pocket. The sharebroker didn’t worry much about the
affair till he came to operate on certain scrip, and found
that, with the intention of gaining more votes at the
meetings of a certain mining co.,he had bought 5000 shares
and put 1000 in the name of each of the clerks in the office,
and had neglected to get them to sign the usual transfer
on the back of the scrip. Without the runaway boy he
could not touch the 1000 shares in the latter’s name.
The boy, being written re the affair, refused to come
back and sign till some practical persuasion had been
tried.

“Archaic” upon instinctive knowledge and
the things that happen in the house-agency business :
“ In my callow youth got a billet in a house-agent’s office.
When the boss was away I had to interview the clients.
The men panned out all right, but the women emphati-
cally resented any intrusion into their private affairs. I
learned to trust to my instincts. One morning a lady in
deep mourning, with a Madonna face, a low voice, and a
good bit of cambric handkerchief, called. She mentioned
incidentally that she was a costumifere. I took to her
instinctively— and she took the villa. Two evenings later
a wild-eyed man roused up the boss (who lived two ter-
races up the street) and commanded him to follow him.
Boss recognised him as Brown, next-door tenant to the
villa, and, scenting epi-
sode, complied. Sounds
of music and waltzing
greeted them as Brown
dragged the boss up a nar-
row passage where a thin
gleam of light proceeded
from an aperture in a dis-
arranged blind. Boss, peep-
ing, discovered six whirl-
ing couples, most of the
men in pyjamas; the
women—well, they hadn’t
that much. ‘ Dancing
long ? ’ whispered the boss,
with his eye glued to the
chink. ‘Good bit,’ said
the wild-eyed man—‘was
watchin’ ’em about an
hour before I came round.’
At early dawn the police
visited the Madonna-like
lady.”
* * *
There was nearly a
riot in a Sydney suburban
street, the other night,
when a crowd of indignant
residents held up two of a
money-lender’s Dailiffs and
threatened to do them
serious bodily injury. This
is the present aspect of
the Sydney shark industry.
A sum of money is ad-
vanced on the household
furniture, over which a
bill of sale is taken. At
first the instalments are
collected regularly, but,
when the loan is nearly
paid off, the collector
never appears on the
horizon, and unless the
borrower takes the money
to the office, a pound or
two soon becomes overdue
Then some evening, when
everybody is out, the
bailiffs break in and re-
move the furniture. Magis-
trate Payten has declared
that this procedure is
quite legal, on the grounds
that the goods are the
money-lender’s, and he is
quite justified in breaking in to get them if the
people are not there to give them to him when he
wants them. All the same, one would like to know what
would happen to Magistrate Payten if, finding himself
without money between midnight and 1 a.m., he broke
into the bank where he had money on deposit and helped
himself, on the ground that there was no bank oflicial to
give it to him when he wanted it. A magistrate that
keeps law like that about his clothes is a danger to the
community. The breaking-in idea has this great advan-
tage for the money-lender, that it enables him to get the
furniture when there is no householder present to tender
payment of the amount due and thus, possibly, spoil the
shark s legal position.
A great industry m Germany is the manufacture
of potato spirit for lighting and for power purposes. It
makes one of the cheapest illuminants and fuels. The
Commonwealth can grow spuds as well as any place on
earth, and might acclimatise this industry.

. problem of the household cleaning is begin-
ning to be solved. Latest achievement is the vacuum
nf/nf/V svvee P s . oufc rooms, &c., by simply sucking up
the dust into a receiver by air suction. You run a rubber
brush attached to a hose (which is connected with an air-
exbaust chamber) over carpets and floors, and the dust
rushes to a proper receptacle without fuss or trouble.

The steamer Rotomahana, at present running
between Wellington and Lyttelton (M.L.), is a notable
vessel in inter-State shipping. Built for the Union Co.
by _ Denny Bros, in 1878, she has practically been
racing all her 25 years’ life. She and her sister
ship, on their first arrival in Australian waters, were
severely criticised by skippers of the old school as being
too narrow and sharp-stemmed—“coffin-ships,” they
were called. But they proved their speediness, and the
Rotomahana flies the company’s greyhound at her main
to this day. The only vessel that has beaten her is the
Mararoa, and on that occasion the margin was so small
that the Rotomahana still holds the dog. She is fitted
with a single screw, and performs her ferry duties at a
15J knot gait—for compound engines something to boast
of.
* * • •
The Australian public is gradually getting the
opportunity of knowing, through the brewing journals,
what its beer is made of. This struck the eye in a paper
of that class the other day (in its queries column) :
Dividend. —“We are thinking of introducing a raw-
grain system of brewing into our brewery, on account of
the saving of expense. Would a beer brewed from an
admixture of rice taste ‘ thin ’ and ‘ watery,’ or would it
bear comparison with other beers not so prepared ? ”
(There are many ways of using raw grain, and raw
grain may be either received by the brewery in an unpre-
pared or in a raw state. Both these kinds of grain have
been used for many years past in England, America, and
Germany, and, as a general rule, the beer so produced,
while being more profitable, bears comparison with any
other beer on the market. Roughly speaking, beer can
be produced with raw grain some 30 per cent, cheaper
than with malt and sugar only.)
The Temperance people, if they knew their business,
would circulate a few hundred thousand copies of one of
the brewing journals, with its disquieting instructions
how to top off and sweeten swill with chemicals, and pass
it as beer, and how to fake spirits with essences, and
manufacture wines without grapes.
* * • *
The Victorian association of authorised news-
agents is cuddling to its very select bosom what promises
to become (if it is not already so) a damnable monopoly.
To be a member of this narrow set is to have a greatly
enhanced value placed upon your “rag’’-stand as against
the independent trader who merely wants to live and let
live. The latter can only retail on suffrance papers
bought from the authorised shop. The wealthy dailies
have perforce to recognise the monopoly, and dare not
supply traders eager to buy and sell—on a cash basis-
outside the newsagents’ring ! In some Victorian coun-
try towns there are several stationers, all men of
reputable credit and standing ; yet, when a customer
requires an Age or an Argus, he has to wait till the shop-
keeper’s lad runs down to the “ recognised ” rival’s shop
for the paper at retail price. No wonder newsagencies
are advertised as worth £2500, £2OOO, £l5OO, £1250, £lOOO,
£9OO, £BOO, down the gamut, when in the “ authorised”
monopolistic ring. The tentacles of this trade octopus are
actually extending to the sale of State school requisites,
which are more often than not only procurable first-hand
at an authorised depot through red tapeworm acquies-
cence. So solid is the monopoly of the association that,
in a town of over 3000 inhabitants, only one man is allowed
to deal in the Melbourne papers, and, as the main street
is about a mile long, it is often a Sabbath day’s journey
to his shop.

A Yankee book agent lately waited upon the
head partner of a big Sydney firm, and, after introducing
himself, requested permission “to feel the pulse of the
Australian public through such a great channel as Messrs.
Blank and C 0.,” in other words, to canvass the employees
for orders for his book. Blank refused, and the canvasser
canvassed the building without permission, but only-
secured one order. He returned to the head partner and
expressed himself thusly : “ I’ve tried your gol darned
pulse for the past three hours, and I guess it’s that tar-
nation feeble that if you don’t employ live men y-our one-
horse firm will simply peter out from business anaemia.”
Then he was violently dragged through the doorway by
the porter and three assistants, who had never. heard
their tin god thus addressed before.
• * ♦ *
“X. 0.”: Touching the registry office swindle.
One Australian labor (!) agent used to (may still, for
all I know) run an extensive business apart from his labor
office, but under another name. His terms were 2s. 6d.
for registration, and half first week’s wages when work
was found for a victim. His second business employed
many hands, and he would send men to his manager,
who would put them on, deduct their half week’s screw,
and turn them adrift again on some pretext or other after
a week or so. The registry office, I believe, was a mere side-
show- or after-thought, started to provide cheap labor for
the factory, and it represented a cut of about 25 per cent,
in wages.

A writer in Melb. Tocsin suggests; sensibly
that the Australian Parliament is the only body
in the Commonwealth which should be dignified
with the title of “ Parliament,” leaving jthe State
legislatures with the titles of “ assemblies ” and
"councils.”
The Ai.ments that rise from chills,
As coughs and colds and other ills,
In Winter months are prevalent most,
Caused by wet, cold winds, and frost.
A Mother’s troubles then begin ;
The children cough, grow pale and thin :
The thoughtful parent should procure
A bottle of Woods’ Great Peppermint Cube.*
The British Government proposes to so
largely increase the National Debt sinking fund
that it will suffice to wholly extinguish the present
British debt in 50 years. The British Govern-
ment has many valuable assets to set against its
debt—its posts, telegraphs, Crown lands and
interest in the Suez Canal, for instance, yield over
£4, 000,000 a year net; but the fact that part of
the debt is represented by permanent and re-
productive works doesn’t strike the Britisher as a
reason for not paying the debt.
The way of the transgressor has been
made very hard in the case of Hugh Watt, for-
merly well-known in Australia—the man to whom
Kingston offered personal violence when he made
an offer which C.C.K. interpreted as a bribe.
Watt went astray with Lady Violet Beauchamp,
who wrote (among other things) that she would
only be happy when he was “ quite away from
that creature ’’—meaning his wife ; she unkindly
added that his lawful possessor was “ a real devil
and criminal in human form.” He somehow
allowed this letter to get into his wife’s hands ! On
it (and other evidence) she divorced him. Then she
lent it to Lady Violet’s husband, who divorced her,
recovering £lO,OOO damages against Watt. Then
Mrs. Watt started afresh, and on the “libel ” con-
tained in the words above quoted got a £5OOO
verdict against Lady Violet. This was reduced
on appeal to £l5OO, the Court remarking candidly
that it was practically an action for the loss of a
husband, and that Watt was “no great loss.”
Perhaps a man who could leave a letter of that
kind lying around deserved all that happened to
him. The obvious moral for a lady is that, not
even When most confidential, should she put on
paper all that she really thinks about the other
cat, especially if she believes that cat is a devil.
THE SLATTERN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Commonwealth owes, reckoning its public debts
alone, something like £200,000,000 to foreign creditors.”—
Plain English, July 9.
The Little Boy at Manly asked Australia what she owed
Unto the Hebrew men that run the pawnshops down the
road.
The slattern raised her uncombed head (she sat upon the
floor),
“ Perhaps two hundred millions; or, maybe, a trifle
more.
“ Excluding private loans that is,” the slattern further
said;
The Little Boy at Manly asked how much they totalled.
“Not much,” she mused, “five millions here, p’raps
ten or twenty there.”
To her dull eye the millions blew like rubbish in the air.
The Little Boy from Manly looked around her house and
said:
“ Where’s all the splendid furnituVe you had when you
were wed?”
The slattern dropped her novelette upon the floor and
yawned—
“ Some’s burnt for firewood, mister, and the rest is lost
or pawned."
lie said: “ Where are the boy and girl you had last time
I called—
I do not see their cradles here—have they been pawn-
wards hauled?"
She drooped her head, the slattern thing—“ The birth,
rate’s failin’ low,
I don’t know why ; the papers say there are no children
now.”
The Little Boy from Manly cursed the slattern to her
face,
He kicked her tawdry novelette across the streets of
space,
And then he sadly turned away to think the matter
o’er,
And left behind an uncombed wench a-borrowing on the
floor.

A COOKY’S SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now this is the song of the cocky chap
By the storekeepers understood
They live through the times of the dreadful drought
On the credit of seasons good !
A long, long credit their credit may be,
But ’tis reckoned os good, I trow 1
For they only wait for the rains to come,
Then they’ll settle the debts they owe 1
Oh ! the hope that eternally springs for man
Is the thing which they live upon—
Like the poor, it is never away from them,
For they’d die if that hope were gone!
And though it is true that the drought is sure,
Yet the rain, it is certain, too !
And they trust, till it comes with its blessfed boon,
That their credit ’ll carry ’em through !
ULOOLA.
SALES DAILY INCREASING.
A. E. Lawrence, the well-known chemist,
Oxford-street, Paddington, writes:—“The sales
on Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy are increasing
daily.” Reports of the same character are being
made all the time. The people want Chamber-
lains Cough Remedy, because they know it is
good, and does not contain any narcotics.*
