POLITICAL
POINTS
Wanted, Bullock Driver with literary turn
to write articles on Kingston. Apply Argus
Office.
Premier Barton nearly lost his temper
when asked if it was true that Robert Reid was to
succeed Kingston as Minister of Customs.
Melbourne Trades Hall has had Irvine’s
infamous Strike Suppression Bill framed and
hung. There was a lot of competition for the
privilege of hanging it.
Judging by some of the men who are
running the sectarian ticket with a view to the
Federal elections, it seems that religion is now
the last refuge of scoundrels. ''
It turns out that the recent feelers with
regard to land-grant railways in the nor’-
west of Westralia were due to “certain sug-
gestions” made by the boss Westralian syndi-
cator, A. E. Morgans, once the State’s Premier
for a minute or two.
What Australia helped to conquer South
Africa for. An offer has been received at
Johannesburg from Hong Kong to supply
200,000 Chinese laborers for the Transvaal mines,
and Governor Milner has asked for 10,000 Indian
coolies for railway work.
Sydney Field is unkindly recalling to
public memory the fact that Carrutliers, who is
now so indignant at the See stone-breaking for
unemployed, was responsible, as a member of the
Reid Ministry in 1895-6, for the useless scrub-
cutting and sand-sliifting jobs provided for the
Bame unemployed. The curseof N.S.W. politics is
that every party which has been in power has be-
haved with sinful extravagance, and the only
party which has never been in charge—the Labor
party —hasn’t sense enough to declare itself an
anti-extravagance organisation.
GAUGING HIS INTELLECT.
"Do you know what time it is?” “ I haven’t the least idea.”
“I know that; but still I thought you might know what o’clock it was.”
The all-pervading terror of Cohen ! A
daily, paper writer, complaining of the want of
gallantry Australians show in leaving women to
stand while men sit in Sydney trams, says that
such conduct unfavorably impresses the British
visitor and cannot but have a bad effect op the loan
market.
Thomas Bent lately had prepared this list
of matters he has to deal with as Vic. Minister
for Works : —Local Government, railway con-
struction, electric light, ports and harbors,
fisheries, width of tyres, Commonwealth and
State buildings, river snagging, and pounds.
Come to think it over, Bent would make an ideal
pound-keeper.
Recent questions asked by Dr. Ross in
N.S.W. Assembly (1) Does the Government
propose to subsidise cat shows, which “may be
of some use in helping citizens to keep down the
mice and rats.” (2) How many dogs died in
Sydney last year ? Dr. Ross’ insatiable thirst for
knowledge will yet lead him to ask for statistics
as to the average number of cockroaches to the
square mile.
Tasmania is planning-out a scheme to
celebrate its centenary by doing (apparently) as
many childish tilings as possible on as many
public holidays as the most enthusiastic of the
idiots can get Propsting to proclaim. On a spot
where Lieutenant Bowen officially landed, away
out at the back of everywhere in a remote
suburb, a monument is to be erected. Here and
elsewhere there will be much swizzle and a bun-
struggle or two. Considerable money will be
wasted, and as there are a thousand national
works in urgent request that waste will be
very characteristically Vandemonian.
The Senate should very closely scrutinise
the agreement between the Commonwealth and
the Eastern Extension Cable Co. before giving its
assent. The E.E.C. Co. —as shown by the way it
fooled N.S.W.—has extra-smart officials, and its
agreements have a habit of proving more strin-
gent in practice than they seem in draft. The
Commonwealth is in the position, practically, of
having to make some agreement to get rid of the
incubus of the old State contracts ; but it should
be very wary as to every line of what it signs.
The hysterical Argus in announcing the
effect produced by Kingston’s resignation :
A general feeling of joyful relief swept through the
city. In the Flinders-lane warehouses the principals
practically made a half-holiday of the afternoon, recog-
nising that the tension to which commercial men have
been subjected ever since Mr. Kingston took charge of
the Customs department had ended. No firm had been
safe from a police-court prosecution under his reign.
Reads as if the criminals of Melbourne were
rejoicing over the reported disbandment of the
detective force.
Perry, N.S.W. Minister for Instruction,
said the other day that he is “a loyal supporter
of free, secular and compulsory education.” Yet
the system he bosses charges children 3d a week,
authorises and invites clergyman of all the creeds
to expound their doctrines in the schools, and so
laxly conducts its compulsory regulations that
about 20 per cent, of the State’s children avoid
attendance. Perry’s opinions may be free, secular
and compulsory, but the department he runs is
threepenny, religious and straggling.
M.L. Premier Seddon, on Chinese labor
in the Rand :
If this sort of thing had been foreseen, no contingents
would have gone from here to South Africa.
But it was foreseen—by the people whom
Seddon metaphorically “bonnetted” when he
was in the delirium of Mafficking fever. Sed-
don’s repentance is too late. The time to try
a person is before, not after, you have hanged
him ; otherwise you are always liable to find out
after the event that his accuser was the really
guilty party.
Alleged Senator Reid, of Victoria, does
not intend to make a fight for his seat at the
forthcoming election. It is practically certain
that he wouldn’t get the seat if he did
fight for it. His connection with the Irvine
Government would damn him in any contest
where there was no property vote to back him,
and his adventures with the Customs, his illegal
grabbing of a senatorial job, and the blank, dead
wall of mental dullness he has displayed in his
unlawful capacity as a bogus Federal member are
all so much additional cast iron around the neck
of his chance.
Nanson, who resigned the leadership of
the Westralian Opposition because he grew tired
of trying to keep a depressing crowd of Tories in
active motion, has moved a resolution, the general
effect of which is that, if Westralia had known its
little contingent to South Africa was fighting for
Chinese labor, it would have seen the Empire
hanged first. That matter, however, is one that
the Australian Parliament should take up. The
voice of all Australia should tell Chamberlain
that if it had known, it would have seen the
Empire hanged first.
The proposed new standing orders, which
are intended to relieve the congestion of business
and check the dreary wash of eloquence in N.S.W.
Assembly, promise well in some respects, but in
others they look like a grievous waste of oppor-
tunity. Three urgent reforms have been lost
sight of—the abolition of the Addres j-in-Reply ;
prohibition of all debate on motions of censure,
save only the speech of the man who proposes the
motion and of the Minister who replies; and a
new system whereby answers to questions will
simply be printed—not read out to the House.
The London Times’ New York correspon-
dent, G. W. Smalley, says that lynchings are
now so common in the United States that the
newspapers only trouble to record them when
they contain features of an unusual or sensational
character ; the fried nigger is only reported if
he is fried something extra. So rampant indeed
has the horror become, the same authority alleges,
that influential pressure is being brought to bear
on the Federal and State authorities to put a stop
to the barbarities. Surely the Russian and
Spanish Governments haven’t been shocked into
action by American cruelty !
WHAT’S BURIED CAN BE DUG UP AGAIN.
“And if there’s to lea big Maritime strike—that’s
where I can come in.”
Treasurer Turner having announced how
much money he will be able to repay to the States
this financial year, the State Treasurers have risen
almost unanimously to say that Turner is over
cautious, and has under-estimated the revenue,
and that there is no need to practise economy or
or tax the big Queensland or Victorian landholder,
for things are sure to pan out somehow better
than is expected.
Queensland Government talks of taking a
referendum on the subject of Bible-reading in
State schools. If the Referendum is taken at the
State Election this move may catch the religious
vote to some extent. When the referendum is
over, the soured and defeated sectarians will realise
that they asked for bread, and were tendered a
brick ; but they may vote for Philp and co. before
they discover that it is a brick.
Clause 3 should utterly damn Dcakm’s
Arbitration Bill. It reads :
This Act shall not apply to the Public Service of the
Commonwealth or of a State, or of any public authority
constituted under the Commonwealth or a State.
The politician who professes to think that it is a
good thing to substitute justice for violence in the
arbitrament of industrial disputes where private
employers are concerned, but not where the larger
body of workers under the Governments, the
various State boards and trusts, and the municipal
councils is affected, should harness up with Yes-
No Reid.
Britain, under its muddle-through-some-
how Government, is making a nice mess of the
little war in Somaliland. A despatch dated June
16 said :
The Abyssinians are dissatisfied with the lack of co-
operation on the part of General Manning. The Abys-
sinians moved to Gerlogubi, at the express desire of
General Manning, who, however, is now unable to assist
them.
Chamberlain has brought Britain down to the
point at which it must nod only have nigger allies,
but lie rebuked by them for military inefficiency.
Yet Australia is asked by Barton to accept Cham-
berlain as an unerring guide in Imperial matters !
If blundering “Janny” Leahy, Queens-
land’s ignorant Railway Minister, had ordinary
self-respect he would promptly resign. The other
day apropos' of nothing at all, but simply to
“ nark ” the other side, he produced and read to
the Assembly a list of labor-union secretaries who
•had been prosecuted for various offences. Every
part of the House cried “Shame,” and a day or
two afterwards, on Cowley’s suggestion, it was
decided to drop Leahy’s cowardly and venomous
contribution out of the official report. But
Leah v doesn’t seem to realise that that decision
reflectsbn him at all-—which, of course, is all the
worse for Leahy.
Affleck’s Leprosy Bill has had good luck in
N.S.W. Assembly and will probably fare as well
in the Council. The Bill provides that persons
affected with leprosy, or suspected of being
affected with it, shall be removed to the Govern-
ment lazarette. The Public Health Board re-
ported against the Bill, but the Government stood
to “ Wully.” The existing Act enables the Board
to declare a private house a lazarette, but this
humane arrangement is now to be done away with.
Leprosy experts and alleged experts differ so
much that it is difficult to form an opinion as to
whether the Health Board or Affleck, M.L. A., is
in the right. N.S.W.’s experience is that Affleck,
M.L.A., is generally wrong, but then the Board
is generally wrong also.
Looks as though Bully Bent may at any
moment be blown from his jiosition in the Irvine
Government by a storm of public ridicule. The
roaring fat man says the absurdest things in the
most uncivil way, and the newspaper men have
seemingly got orders to present the plain
unvarnished Bent in his habit as he talks. His
so-called “strength ” lies in a foolish vulgarity of
utterance; his activity is senseless rampageous-
n'ess ; his expressed notions of needful reform are
contemptible in character. When he finds a case
where things should be smoothed down he talks
wildly of “shaking’em up,” and his balder dash
is reported under a cross-heading every time. The
wrath of Bent, the addle-pated aggressionist, has
ceased to be a tolerable joke.
The Commonwealth Arbitration Bill “ does
not provide for the inclusion of the employees of
State Governments, because the Federal Govern-
ment felt it could not dictate to the States how
they should appropriate their revenue.” A
strangely lank and attenuated excuse. The real
trouble is, that the Barton Cabinet is suffering
from an innate dislike to do anything unpleasant
to the people it meets at the club. It meets
Irvine and the awful Bent at the club, and rather
than do anything to irritate those objectionable
parties it is going to do a rank injustice to several
thousands of its own constituents, if it can. It
doesn’t meet those thousands at the club; but it
will meet them at the ballot box, and that innate
dislike to do anything nasty is going to prove the
ruin of Australia’s Nobbled Son.
Stated by English papers that the
Servian assassinations have not disturbed
the Courts of Europe, socially, in the least.
Any other royal death would have thrown
the public engagements of every surviving
monarch into some sort of disorder. But
the Obrenovitehes were not “in society,
so far as royal families were concerned.
The Swedish king has, to some extent,
become acquainted with his fellow-poten-
tates, though he is the great-great-grandson
of a police court lawyer, but the descen-
dants of the Pig-Dealer were never properly
introduced.
Like his blacklegs, Premier Irvine—
he of the famous backbone —has fulfilled his
usefulness. Even the Victorian Tammany
—the National Citizens’ Reform League—
has been speaking of him in anything but
an encouraging way lately.
Order will never be obtained in
Parliament until offensive interruptions are
suppressed, and the best way to suppress
them is by money fines. If Speaker
M‘Court or Chairman Cann were empowered to
say to an offensive interrupter, “ Two pounds,
please, and vacate your seat till paid,” decorum
would be practically assured. The present sys-
tem of withdrawing and apologising not only fails
but makes debate ridiculous.
As a sample of the art of putting things,
the cable that during last year New York labor
unions blackmailed employers to the extent of
£400,000, takes the oalce: When the representa-
tives of capital inform a hundred thousand workers
that their wages are reduced by sixpence per day
no cable idiot rushes to explain that Fatman has
extorted £780,000 blackmail from the workers. It
is euphoniously spoken of as a “ decline in tha
wages rate.”
Trial Bay (N.S. W.) prison, a “permanent;
and reproductive ” public work, constructed some
years, back at a cost of £lOO,OOO loan money, has
been finally closed up as a gaol. Also, the three-
parts finished breakwater, a costly and difficult
attempt to construct (out of loans; it has been
washed away once, and repaired also out of loans)
a harbor for the prison, will probably be aban-
doned likewise. The Public Works Department
is scratching its head to find a use for the estab-
lishment. This makes two abandoned gaols
on N.S.W.’s hands. The other —the £50,000
loan-money Cooma gaol—has never had a single
prisoner inside its walls.
On July 29, 40 years ago, N.S.W.
abolished State aid to religion. At the com*
mencement of the settlement the C.E. was the
only sect aided. In time aid was extended to the
R.C. Governor Bourlce insisted on all-round fair
play, and extended aid to Presbyterians and
Wesleyans. In 1862 all aid was abolished, but the
Bill did not take effect till the middle of 1863.
The Act arranged for pensions to all preachers in
office while they remained in office and within the-
State. The first year’s pensions came to £32,372.
They have dwindled down to under £4OOO. Alto-
gether, taxpayers have paid to parsonical pen-
sioners over £750,500.
That Heaven-inspired reformer, Yic. Pro*
mier Irvine, has lately brought over a Mr.
Smith from S.A. to set things straight in
Taverner’s Lands Office. Smith is vaguely under-
stood to be some sort of underling in the S.A.
Department, and he spent just a week in Mel-
bourne bossing Taverner’s subordinates about,
and repeating the suggestions which have been
made ad nauseam in the Yic. Lands Office itself.
Smith’s main qualification for his reform job is the
fact that S.A. does about a seventh of the land
business carried out in the course of a year in the-
Iceberg State. It is “upto ” some member when
the Vic. Assembly meets to ask Irvine why Smith,
left home.
RESIGNATION OF KINGSTON.
LITTLE BOY FROM MANLY: “Fatty, what art
you crying for?” IMPORTER : “Joy."
A letter from Tasmania :
The Propsting Government doesn’t roost so high on th*'
tree of popularity as it did. It has shown an indisposi*
tion to meet Parliament—in itself sufficient to excit*
suspicion. It has manifested a puerile timidity in one of
two emergencies, and in several matters it has shown*
disastrous disposition to play the fool. Parliament
meets, unduly late, some time in August; and some time
later the fate of the new team will he decided. It is very
rarely that a perfectly new Government goes and hides
itself behind a stump in this protracted manner and looks
so anxious to avoid even making a beginning with it*
programme. If it sticks on a big absentee-tax, fight*
through its Early-Closing Act, and otherwise lives up to
itshustings promises, the Propsting Ministry will probably
win through, but unfortunately a large section of its sup-
porters think it was elected solely to take off necessary
taxes. What will happen next nobody pretends to know.
The Lewis Administration went out bag and baggage, and
the present germ of Opposition is as sick and hopeless a*-
an incurable cat.

“ PUBLIC PRONOUNCE EXCELLENT.”
This is what J. Travers, Rydal, N.S.W., writes
of Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy. He also
reports a constantly increasing sale. The secret
of the success of Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is
that it will do all the Manufacturers claim for it,
and also that it contains NO OPIUM, which
makes it a valuable adjunct to all household
medicine chests. Sold by all dealers at Is. 6d.*
“ War E.” : Minister Crick’s latest (N.S.,
Wales) Land Bill proposes to allow homestead
selectors the privilege of converting their holdings
into conditional purchases. This proposal is
advocated on the ground that the “penniless”
selector who takes up a H.S. is debarred from
raising money on it; the holding, being merely
leased from the Government, is not negotiable
property. Therefore, declare the advocates of the
CLP. system, the penniless selector cannot develop
his holding—by putting a hopeless mortgage on
it. So it is proposed that the penniless selector
shall pay, perhaps, £5OO to the State so as to get
a title t 9 his land, that he may borrow, perhaps,
£3OO on it and thus be £2OO more penniless than
he was before. The idea of a penniless selector
being greatly oppressed because he isn’t allowed
to pay a large sum of money and acquire the free-
hold of an estate is doubtless a great hardship.

The tendency to potter miserably over
Bill’s dirt-money seems incurable in N.S.W.
The other day the time of the Assembly was
taken up with eleven questions about one laborer
at Fitzroy Dock, and a day or two later two more
questions were asked—one of them being whether
the Government would hire, at some expense, a
competent outside engineer to inspect the laborer
in question. N.S.W. Assembly makes one radi-
cal mistake; it is a Parliament, but it is under
the delusion that it is a rouseabout, light porter
and office boy. Is there any other Parliament in
the world where the Premier would gravely report
that 2491 dead dogs had been fished out of the
harbor in six months ? f
Premier Barton occasionally makes a show
of dignity. Having decided to extend the Van-
couver mail service for two years at an additional
subsidy, he didn’t officially notify either N.S.W.
or Queensland Government, so Premier Philp
cheekily wrote that the additional expense should
not have been incurred without consulting him !
Whereat Premier Barton:
Mr. Philp seems to think that the business of the
t ederal Government and Parliament should be carried on
subject to the veto of each of the State Governments.
However desirous I am of preserving the most friendly
relations with the State Governments—and I am desirous
of doing this—l cannot Carry matters to the length of
substituting their opinions for those of the Parliament to
which I am responsible.

purity of Sunlight Soap, and reports that
it contains no adulteration whatever.

When the Super. Cracks a Joke.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Poets of the backblocks mostly paint the bushman
in their rhyme
A melancholy, dismal sort of clown,
Who frets, and broods, and worries ’neath a gum-
tree most the time,
When he isn’t on a roaring spree in town.
And I rather think the poets dip in truth their
rhyming pen
When branding him a “ pessimistic bloke ”;
Yet I wonder did they ever hear the roar of
laughter when
The super, of the station cracks a joke?
Oh, they fairly shake the rafter
Of the woolshed with their laughter
When the super, cracks a joke—
When he’s pleased to crack a thousand-year-
old joke!
And the same bards sing of bushmen as a sad and
dreamy crew,
Who care not for the life in lighter vein,
And who dearly love the sadness, and the solemn
silence, too,
Of the lonely bush and sun-grilled western plain.
And I really think these ballads are the songs of
truthful men,
Who do not wrap their Billjim in a cloak ;
Yet I don’t believe they’ve listened to their soul-
ful dreamers when
The super, of the station cracks a joke.
Oh, they leave off sadly dreaming,
And they rend the air with screaming,
When the super, cracks a joke—
Thinks it fit to crack an idiotic joke !
And these poets also tell us in a melancholy strain
How the bushman scorns the very thought of jest ;
While comedy or mirthfulness would cause him
bitter pain,
And he only acts in tragedy Out West.
And I freely will acknowledge nought is more
depressing than
The mopoke’s dirge and curlew’s dismal croak,
Yet I fancy that these minstrels never saw their
Hamlets when
The super, of the station cracks a joke.
Oh, they ring the tragic curtain
Down at once, you may be certain,
When the super, cracks a joke—
Cracks some stale and shallow pantomimic
joke!
Bushmen, spite their dreamy sadness, then we
hear the poets say,
Answer loyally their Union Battle-Cry,
And nobly stand together in the fight for better
pay,
And to hoist the Flag of Freedom up on high !
And I grant amongst the shearers are, indeed,
some gallant men,
Who bravely try to break the tyrant’s yoke—
Yet I swear these bards have never heard their
western heroes when
The super, of the station cracks a joke.
Oh, they shake the woolshed rafter
With their joy and with their laughter
When the super, cracks a joke—
Some old forgotten mummy of a joke.
Well, we’ve, too, been up the country—my old
mate, Bill Mayne and I,
And have felt the thing the poets rhyme about,
And have heard the plaintive curlew, and the
Union battle-cry
When the leaders called the Western shearers out.
But we’ve left it, for we couldn’t keep a job like
other men,
And were mostly humping swag, and stony-
broke,
Simply just because we, somehow, couldn’t burst
out laughing when
The super, of the station cracked a joke.
Oh, you have to grin and bear it,
And grin loud, so they can hear it,
When the super, at the station cracks a joke—
Some old Egyptian tombstone of a joke.

COLLECTIVE INVESTIGATION.-The ex-
perience of millions of people in all quarters of the
habitable globe during the last quarter of a
century has confirmed the value of Hunyadi Janos
as the best and safest natural aperient. Invalu-
able to diners-out.*
The Bulletin, having attended an
address by Mr. H. F. Wyatt, Envoy of the
British Navy League, on Imperial Naval Defence,
came away with somewhat this impression of his
speech:—
When the mighty fleets of Palestine ranged over the
confines of Atlantis and brought the terror of the sea-
power to the conscience of the perfidious Aztecs, then it
was that the germ of the British navy was born. Since
then it has fructified in manliness, it has weltered in the
blood of foes, it has stormed the dizzy Himalayan heights
of grandeur, and its nodding plumes have waved over all
the battle-grounds of the world. What the old oak
walls of England have not done no mortal eye can
imagine—no mortal mind can hear at the top of its voice.
What they have done is writ in imperishable brass on the
Forehead of Time in clear and lustrous characters ; their
oars have beaten every sea ; their smoke has painted
every sky with the deep-graved lessons of British
might; their sails have brought to every nook of the
earth a flaunting defiance and a white-winged message of
hope and reliance and gin. The British navy—to which
you are asked to subscribe the small and absurdly in-
adequate sum of £200,000 a year, a sum which, in propor-
tion to the merits of the cause, is but as a grain of sand
to the bulk of the star Sirius—is the heir of all the ages
anl the hope of all posterity ; it is the sure refuge for
Fr -edom wherever menaced ; the screaming of its fog-
whistle is a clarion note against oppression, a strident
declaration of British infallibility, and as sweet a music
as ever an angel hanging down from heaven piped from
his golden lyre, affirming, as he did so, that the Bible and
the Navy were the secrets of England’s greatness. You
will now put your little £200,000 on the plate whilst the
choir sings the Bulldog Treed.
Breakers ahead ! the cold and rain
Will soon be with us once again ;
Soon Winter’s breath will freeze the air,
For coughs and colds we must prepare,
Complaints that cause so much distress.
Yet evoryofie will soon confess
That the remedy, good and pure,
Is some Woods’ Great Peppermint Cube.*
Blackwatfcle Bay, whose awful stench
keeps three or four members of Parliament con-
stantly rapping .at Ministerial doors, washes a
portion of historic old Sydney. Dr. Bowman, an
early surgeon, received the ground running round
Glebe Point as a reward for services, and worked
the estate by convict labor. Some of the wildest
scenes about Sydney occurred there. Byndhurst
Hall, the Bowman mansion, was purchased by
Archbishop Polding and turned into a boys’
college. Archbishop Vaughan afterwards sold it.
Even now one may come upon hooks and bars
and old convict-branded blocks of stone, telling
that the estate was once “ well-worked.”
W. N. Willis, M.L.A., as a land agent
needs no black-tracker to guide him through the
devious paths of the Land Acts. He fights all
his cases, and the harder the task the better he
like it, and wins all the time. 21 Bligh-street.*
The latest authority to veto the germ-
infested Word, usually handed to witnesses in
courts, is the leading doctor of Charters Towers
(N. Q.), who declined to guarantee his own veracity
till a clean and healthy Scripture was procured.
The next Australian medical conference should

STEELE RUDD’S
“ On Our Selection.”


SPORTING NOTIONS.
Marmont’s popular victory, at Caulfield, last
Saturday, gives him the distinction of being the first
horse to win the two G.N. Hurdle Baces, a distinction
which Colonel Shilinski last year missed by a head, when
Itawdon beat him for the V.B.C. event. On paper,
Shilinski still looks the better of the two champions, see-
ing that he carried 111 b. more than Marmont’s burden
round the long course at Caulfield, and subsequently
won the Steeplechase with 13.2 on his back. It remains
for Marmont to make a name as a ’.chaser, nnd, even
if he should beat Shilinski’s record over the bidr
sticks, he will have to do better than that,
before he can. rank beside Bedleap, the daddy of them
all. In the meantime, the six consecutive wins of Mr.
M'Arthur’s brilliant jumper have persuaded his jubilant
followers that they never saw his equal. ■ Before he
Started for the V.B.C. Grand National a month ago,
carrying a comfortable 9.1, Marmont was said to have two
stone in hand. Now it is said that three stone more
than his handicap would not have stopped him. If he
goes for the Hurdle race next Saturday, and wins it,
backers of “ form ” will shout loud enough to burst a
rain-cloud, for a cut-and-come-again Marmont happens to
them only once in many years.
* * * V •
Such consistency of success in obstacle racing
draws attention to the absolutely rotten state of the
governments which pretend to guard the morals of flat-
racing in Australia. Marmont starts a hot favorite, the
field is large as a rule, Ikey Mo would arrange a defeat
of the good thing by foul means if he could, but Marmont
wins every time. Hurdle and steeple racing don’t offer
easy opportunities for “ interference.” The men who
risk their lives in a game where accidents must
happen are bound by considerations of mutual interest
to deal fairly with one another until the last obstacle has
been cleared. By that time the best horse is rarely in
danger of being jockeyed out of his rights. And so a
red-hot favorite wins hurdle races where he would be in-
terfered with and prevented from winning if the hurdles
were out of the way. A good jumper has a fair chance
of beating a big field of horses even when the “books”
stand to lose heavily by his victory. A hot pot for the Mel-
bourne Cup, or any other big handicap run on a circular
course, has not a fair chance. Among 20 flat race jockeys
there is always one young ruffian, or two, or more, bent
on “ dealing it out” to the favorite should an opening
for foul play offer itself. And until racing is strictly
policed— i.e., stewarded—this form of Turf rascality will
continue to flourish.
* * * *
“ Tout” : Know of a man who started backing
Marmont in the first of his successive lepping triumphs.
He collected £8 over the initial win at Caulfield, the odds
to a sovereign. Winnings were played up at Williams-
town, odds being sto 2. This made his capital £2B and
it all went on Marmont for the V.B.C. Grand National at
threes which swelled the money in hand to £ll2. In the
Doutta Galla Hurdles the punter took £l5O to £lOO and
£lB to £l2, thus increasing the accumulation of wealth to
£2BO. Haven’t heard how he fared over Marmont’s
V.A.T.O. G.N. Hurdles Saturday, but his mind was fully
made up to throw the lot in again, and for the last time.
* 4* * *
V.A.T.C. G.N. ’Chase, coming Saturday, looks
almost as good for Freedom as did Hurdles for Marmont
last Saturday. In recent V.B.C. G.N. Steeple he was
there all the time, making the pace and jumping with
cat-like ease and grace. Being asked to win from end to
end looked a more than severe task, but the nag’s
stamina and lepping capabilities probably justified it,
especially as the field included a few speedier ones. It
was Freedom’s forcing that brought about the shattering
of all prior time feats ; and the previous fastest belonged
to him. Unless there is a Grafnell in the Caulfield event,
and with ordinary luck, Freedom should be first past the
post. ,
Pace set by Examiner and Rawdon made
V.A.T.C. Grand National Hurdles, Saturday, very fast.
Time (6.57) was within Jsec. of fastest— Bealgar’s,
five years ago. Had Marmont been slightly pushed near
the end, new figures would surely have resulted.
* * * ♦
Hitherto unreliable T.M.S. turned up at
Bosehill on Monday after a long spell, showing bigger
and heavier than ever, and, backed from tens down to
fives, beat a particularly warm field —if they were all
“on it.” Said that nag belongs to the gentleman whose
initials T.M.S. are, and who, according to the card, will
henceforth be known to Turfdom as “ Mr. Marvel."
* * * *
Restrictions placed upon “ Tatt.’s ” don’t
seem to weight him much ; already three £25,000 on Ade-
laide Grand National Hurdles have been drawn, and a
fourth is said to be filling fast.
* * * *
Prominence of 13. T’other day, at the Hutt
(M.L.), a backer put his coin on because there were 13
letters in the nag’S name (Shrapnel Shell), 13 in the
names of his sire and dam (Son-of-a-Gun—Dudu), 13 in
the name of the race (the “Parliamentary” Handicap)
and 13 lined up at the start.
* * * •
The English Jockey Club has certainly improved
—at last—on the Australian starting-gate innovation by
giving the starter authority to signal a “No-go” and
recall the gees if their machine-made start proves a
failure. Australia still sticks to the original rule that the
first uplifting of the gate must be final, and thus the
purpose of a wise invention is defeated-in some cases.
At Flemington or Bandwick it sometimes "happens that
the gate-start is a worse start than was usually permitted
under the bygone rule of the flag, when a specially un-
happy despatch was immediately cancelled by the
starter’s “ Come back.” And it looks as though the
lifting-gat&is likely to be replaced for good by a barrier
which works on the principle of railway gates. Why not ?
At the pressure of an electric button the gates fly open
quick as lightning and the start is signalled.

* * * *
The enterprising; youth of Adelaide, emulating
their elders, run “■ books ” on the races, and doubles of
7s. 6d. to a penny are common. The police recently con-
fiscated a notebook in which a lad of about 14 had
recorded a most complete series of transactions at these
odds. • ,
• • • *
The winning instinct in some horses is very
strong. A hurdler lost his jockey half way through at
the last Wellington (M.L.) meeting, but plodded on, ran
into a handy position in the. last half-mile, and in the
final pinch timed his run to' a nicety, and just got home.
* * * »
The well-groomed amateur jock who travels to
every race meeting, and who iS labelled “Mr.” in press
reports when he gets a mount, is being brought up with
a round turn in M.L.', and-not before it was time. Some
of him have been sailing under false colors for years, and
pros, have used highly decorative language on the sub-
ject, and asked the authorities to inquire into the bona
fides of a number of the gentry. The conference has
now decided that no weight allowance shall be made
amateurs when riding against pros., and a new rule
dealing with the whole question is to be drafted. The
gentleman rider, as a rule, is a most amusing circum-
stance, but there are many in M.L. who can more than
hold their own anywhere.
4r » * * *
During M.L. trotting season just closed 32
meetings were held, and £18,378 given away in prize
money. Tote investments reached £159,147.
J 4c ' * * *
Crack M.L. jock C. Jenkins rode 85 winners
during last season—a record for the State.
* * ♦ *
At Boulder (W.A.) races t’other day three
brothers (An win) were up on first, second, and third nags
in big event, and same thing occurred in last race of
programme. Has this a parallel ?
D. J. Price, owner of the champion M.L.
trotter Bibbonwood, writes from ’Frisco that the famous
training track at Pleasanton is from five to seven seconds
faster than that of the Metropolitan Club (Christchurch),
where Bibbonwood put up a record of 2min. 6sec. for a
mile. He reckons that Bibbonwood would cut out half-
a-mile at Pleasanton in lmin. easily. Price was endeavor-
ing to arrange a match with Clipper (2min. 6sec.), any
distance, any number of heats, for any amount, contest
to come off in M.L. next season, and there’s a probability
of the challenge being taken up. If not, Bibbonwood will
be shipped to’Murka, where his sanguine owner reckons
he’ll beat anything in his class. Apropos: There is a
likelihood of the speedy Almont being brought over from
Melbourne to meet Bibbonwood shortly, and the tussle
should be a memorable one.
* * * *
The Adelaide Racing Club shows in its annual
account a profit Of £5BO for the year, and has £2625 in the
bank. It gave £7625 in stakes for seven days’ racing, and
made a profit on all of them, totalling £2038, (The profit
was really greater than that, but this club debits a pro-
portion of wages and salaries to the meetings.) Gate
money and sundries brought in £7075, and totalisator
commission £5424, the latter item, which has in some
years been as large as the former, showing some falling-
off this time. The A.B.C. has 451 tWo-guinea members,
and pays the City Corporation £BOO a year for the use of
a strip of the Park Lands to race On. The S.A. Jockey
Club has 258 three-guinea members, and an overdraft of
nearly £5OOO, but then it owns its own course of 162 acres,
with all improvements. It raced seven days, giving £7890
in stakes, and collecting £5448 from the tote and £6719
otherwise. The profit on the race meetings was £2016,
and on the whole year’s transactions about £7OO. The
S. A. J.C. administers a Disabled Jockeys’ Fund, which relies
principally on the “ fractions” from the tote. The fund
has £T6OO out at interest and is still growing. It took in
£285 last year, and only distributed £l5O cautiously
among 19 deserving cases, so it is apparently making
ready for a time when all the jockeys in S.A. shall be
smashed up and need assistance simultaneously.

Pilgrim’s Progress-Bright Eyes 4yr.-old mare
Bright Beauty, when properly labelled for anything
during coming Spring, will be worth something better
than an ordinal investment. Since beginning of year
her successes have been Caulfield Trial Handicap, Wood-
cliff Handicap (lm. 3f.) 6.7 Up, and V.B.C. Farewell
Handicap (lm.) with 7.1.

New Turf year opened Saturday. Imported
Isonomy nag Pilgrim’s Progress tops the list of success-
ful sires insofar as prize money Is concerned, his total
being £12,661; but Lochiel’s get, with £12,016 to their
credit, won 158 J races as against Isonomy’s progeny’s 49,
though it took 56 of them to do it, while only 17 suc-
ceeded for Pilgrim’s Progress. Noteworthy that for 7
years Lochiel has been first in number of winners and
races appropriated. Past 6 years have seen horses got
by Lodhiel accumulate 820 events.

» * * *
Grafton’s position among winning sires is worth
referring to. Though only 8 years old, with none of his
stock over four years, 34 of last season’s winners were
sired by him, and they gathered 65 races, worth £10,367,
placing Grafton fourth on the list. There was heaps of
variety in these successes, which ranged from country
sprints to V.B.C. Grand National.
* » » *
The “great fight” which the Maoriland anti-
gamblers threatened to put up in the-House of Beps.
when the Tote Abolition Bill came on was merely a
small-boy sham-fight after all. It was a sudden-death
debate, the friends of the machine saying nothing, and
the Bill was cast out by 34 to 14. The proposal to take a
referendum of the people on the subject will meet a
similar fate.
* * * *
Corizann, who followed up her runaway win of
three weeks earlier by scooping Tattersall’s Club Handi-
cap (ljm.), in Adelaide, recently, is the only one of
Thomas Elder’s importation Nautilus’s get that has shown
any form at all. Bace-goers generally voted her a squib,
but they’re forming different opinions now. Said
Corizann’s lessee (an Adelaide storekeeper) has done par-
ticularly well through the mare’s successes.
* * • *
M.L. statistics for year just closed place Step-
niak ahead of all other winning sires with £8095, followed
by Hotchkiss (£6780), and St. Leger (dead) £6515. Biggest
winning owner is Mr. G. C. Stead—he gathered £7145 as
the outcome of his nags’ successes. Mr. George Clifford
ranks second with £4420, and Mr. J. Mark third (£3730).
* * * *
A. Spence: “Off-side Mao’s” par. on M.L.
Bugby team (Bulletin 9/7/’O3) is merely a bundle of mis-
takes wrapped up in bizarre phraseology, and his lament
over the absence of the rib-smashing, neck-cracking
element has brought a violent blush over these little
islands. Maoriland has never made a study of what “ Off-
side Mac” terms “sanguinary” football, and the history
of that branch of sport, as far as it has gone in this State,
can fortunately be told in a few sentences. In the
nineties there was a North Island player who plied
the knife for some meat-freezing concern, and—
such is the all-conquering grip of environment-
made an attempt to introduce “shop” on to
the football field. After a year or two of purely
local practice he led a team south as far as Invercargill, and
then, taking the island from the reverse end, played his
way home again,, leaving a wake of blood behind him.
Of course, all the teams met were taken on the hop, went
on to play football, got down to rushes, &c., and, for the
most part, didn’t know any more until they awoke in the
dressing-room with the doctor standing over them. For
this the man who had mistaken the football field for his
ordinary place of business claimed great credit. - On two
occasions, by the way, a surviving player from the beaten
side assaulted the hero when the game was over. [But if
this is the whole history of gory Maoriland-football,
whence comes all the present bloodshed ?—Ed. 8.l
* * * *
A new football experience happened at Echuea
(Vic.) police court the other day, when three prominent
members of the Echuca football team were arrested before
an important match, charged with having no lawful
means of support. When before the court the defending
lawyer stigmatised the arrest as a partisan move on the
part of the supporters of the Echuca East’s team, which
is leading for the Northern premiership. Various
citizens who took an interest in the pursuit of
the globeful of wind offered to employ the accused
forthwith, and in the end the magistrate said-the evi-
dence was insufficient. A large number of footballers,
and persons who didn’t play themselves but looked on
and howled, watched the proceedings with interest.
* * * *
“B.” : Feeling in N.S.W. ran very high against
the court-martialled M.L. footballer for rough play
recently, but in the cases of rough play the shriekers
overlooked the fact that in each instance the same
N.S.W. player was participant in the trouble. The M.L.
style of forward play in big matches differs so greatly from
N.S.W. play that it seems brutal; still, followers of club
football in Sydney have seen more spiteful play in com-
petition games—notably Eastern Suburbs v. Newtown,
when the pass-word was “ Play them at their own game ”
—the “ own game ” signifying roughness. The game is
football with the visiting M.L. team, and they play it for
all it’s worth.
* * » •
Gloriously fine weather favored Vic.-S.A. foot-
ball struggle on Adelaide Oval, Saturday, and 10,000
looked on. Match wasn’t stirring at any stage, for in
first half visitors fairly spread-over home team and scored
everything. Wheat Staters couldn’t get moving at all.
Victorians shaped with heaps of dash, and literally went
through the enemy by dint of fine combination, brilliant
marking, sure long and short kicking, rapid passing and
marked activity generally, which contrasted strongly
with opposing team’s fumbling, lifeless play and decided
lack of confidence. Only once (in the third quarter) did
S. A. side make a show,‘then the effort was so brilliant
and well kept up that Vic. was in trouble till the
end ; but ’twas too late. Visitors won 9 goals 6 behinds
to 5 goals 12 behinds.
* * * *
Who says Rugby isn’t popular in Sydney ? On
Saturday, when grade competitions were resumed, after
four weeks of Q.-N.S.W. and M.L.-N.S.W. roughs, nearly
7000 watched the play in pouring rain at Sydney Agricul-
tural Society’s Ground, and upwards of 3000 more at
Wentworth Park, while something above 1000 were at
’Varsity Oval. Under the conditions only in a few caSes
were matches interesting, but this made no difference to
the onlookers —’twas football.
* * * *
Last week, prior to going Brisbanewards, M.L.
footballers roihped over Sydney 15, at ’Varsity Oval,
Winning by 33 to 3. At half-time, Sydney had the better
of things (3 to nil), mainly through playing with the
wind, but this advantage was with the Maorilanders in
the next half, and they did as they liked, despite spunky
efforts of Oxenham, Ogard, Manning, and Johnston.
Though home side was deplorably' weak ; the visitors
Outplayed themselves. Their passing rushes were
triumphs of combination and a source of much delight to
the 15,000 spectators,
» * * *
Q.-M.L. match, Brisbane, Saturday, attracted
12,000 spectators. Visitors averaged 12.13, and home
combination 11.9. Though M.L. notched 6 to nil in first
half, Q. played up well, and game was far more even than
score indicates. Wallace, M‘Gregor, Wood, Asher, and
Kiernan did great work for M.L. —Wallace’s long punts
into touch were a rare treat. Full-back Bedwood, three-
quarters N. Colton and M‘Lean, and Gralton (behind the
scrum) operated effectively and at times brilliantly' for
Q., while best work in the pack was done by O’Connor,
Nicholson, Anderson, and Bichards. M.L. forwards
showed better than on any previous occasion this tour,
but backs hardly rose to first Sydney match form. Game
ended 17 to nil in visiting team’s favor. Gate was £460.
* * * *
Carlton-Geelong football contest (a match in
Victorian premiership) at Sydney Cricket Ground Mon-
day, speedy and versatile, was infinitely ahead of Fitzroy-
Oollingwood display. Last 15min. were intensely exciting.
Carltons pulled up materially and looked more than
possible winners just when success seemed assured for the
others. In the end Geelong won 55 points to 45, other-
wise 8 goals 7 behinds to 6 goals 9 behinds. The excellent
play has gone far to popularise Australian rules in
Sydney. There were 5000 onlookers.
* * * * %
The Sydney “cop” wakes up occasionally.
The humble poker-machine, with its mad thirst for three-
penny pieces, adorned many hotel counters for months
past with a “ For Sale ” ticket tied round its villainous
body. As a sale never took place. Flossie bringing the
other Hebes to look at any fool who asked the price, the
law decided the machine was still working in the interests
of the publican, and fined a few of the machine owners a
few weeks back. Pitting a “For Sale” ticket on the
machine shows the publican’s opinion of the law’s intelli-
gence, and a publican ought to know. After the fining
process the machines were put under the Counter for a
day or two, but they are now in full blast again.
» * * *
That very latest insanity, the walking-match, is
still going strong in Australia generally, and is breaking
out in more unexpected places every day. The abso-
lutely last crowd of idicts to contract the disease are the
Adelaide barmaids and waitresses ! A gathering of these
angels got together the Sunday before last and toddled
away with great earnestness, many squeals, and much
perspiration. Accounts don t say whether or not the
lady competitors had gone into severe training for the
event, but the affair was so great a success all through
that another event of a like nature was set down to take
place in the Sturt district on a convenient Sunday.
Unluckily for the sport, however, the pious local W.C.T. U.
has “ risen ” up in horror, and, at the annual convention
then sitting, decided todeputationise Adelaide’s Mayor to
put a stop to the sinful proceedings at once. But Ade-
laide’s Mayor Cohen isn’t very likely to see anything
specially sinful in walking on Sunday.
* * * •
In all the talk about wise selections for the
forthcoming English Eleven there has been no reference
to the most important question of sobriety, and the next
important question of pluck in the individual cases.
Maclaren’s team, also the two previous teams captained
by Stoddart, inciuded four or five untrustworthy mem,
who drank too much, smoked too much, and slept' too
little. One boozer pleaded in excuse for himself that
there was “ nothing for a fellow to do, except knock
around,” by which he presumably meant to say that in
England he had some sort of a home during part of the
week, but in Australia he was a grass widow on the
everlasting loose. Another foolish pro. boasted that he
was none the worse for drinking whisky until 3 a.m.,
because he could sweat it out of him in the next day’s
play. In these cases the unreliables had good nerves and
bad habits, whereas the trouble with two or three of the
others was over-anxiety to do their best in the field when
they hadn’t made special endeavors to put their nerves
right. If the Warner team is to be composed of chaps
who travel with a strong thirst, or a faint heart, or both,
then it will fail miserably, like Maclaren’s skiting crowd,
* * * *
Poidevin followed up his cabled 172 (not out)
against Lancashire with 67 and 64 against Gloucester.
London Express says he is “ bang in the first flight of
batsmen.” He will be back home in Sydney in time to
play next season. It was Poidevin who made the big
best score against Maclaren’s last team in Australia—lsl
(not out), and he would have played in the first Test but
for an injury to his hand.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Rowing Association can lay claim
to having the most “ tony ” set of office-bearers possible
to squeeze, together—from the State Governor to —well,
the politician ; yet it never seems to go very much ahead,
and the balance in hand is but a “fiver.” There’s too
much “ haw ” about it; and the real aim of the Bowing
Association seems to be the gathering together of a few
of the very naicest people. As a result the interest of the
“ working” portion of the members in the doings of the
Association has sunk a:good deal, and threatens to sink
deeper during the coming season.
• » * *
Inter-State table tennis tourney, running at
N.S.W. Sports Club all last week, proved easy for home
team with 1293 points to Queenslanders’ 1088. Surprises
were thick all through. N.S.W. rep., W. A. Gregg,
secured Australian supremacy, defeating A. B. Jones
and Horace Elliott 16 to 9 each in conclusive bouts.
Yet only two or three nights before Elliott had
accounted for Gregg easily when contesting honor of
runner-up in N.S.W. premiership, which A. B. Jones won
previous Saturday, squelching both Gregg and Elliott.
Mixed doubles supremacy of N.S.W. went to Miss
Ivy Jones and S. L. Jones, while Gentlemen’s Doubles
championship was appropriated by brothers Horace and
H. K. Elliott. Though table tennis has experienced a big
drop in popularity since last winter, play shown this year
excels that of last season. Probably the game won’t be
heard of 12 months hence outside of a few homes.
* * » *
The Summer Nights Amusement Committee —
which, in conjunction with the N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen, controlled the importation of Major
Taylor and the Sydney Thousand bike-race—evi-
dently means business this season. Three meetings
are proposed (November, January, March), at which
£2500 will go in prize money—the huge Sydney Thousand
race making a hole in that sum. At present there’s an
agent on the Continent endeavoring to induce a few of
the best riders to make the trip ; but better control than
that which characterised last season’s fixtures will be
required to make the venture a success. Yet the fixtures
cover a length of time too far distant to reckon on—
as at present the governing body of the N.S.W. League
of Wheelmen threatens to suicide.
* * * *
Entries (194) for Dunlop road-race, Warrnam-
bool to Melbourne (165 m.), top world’s record for similar
class of bike event. M.L. handicap test over 112 m. to
find a rep. for the tussle saw J. Arnst, second man home,
do best time—7h. 20m. —and behind him were fore-
fronters Balston and Calder, who both shaped pro-
minently in last year’s “ Warrnambool,’’ which makes
Arnst look a sure top-noteher. Up to moment of writing
W.A., Tas., and N.S.W. tests have not been held.
At last! World's two miles running record in
W. 6. George’s name has been knocked out. When
(19 years ago) W.G.G. put his 9.17 2-5 up all the world
wondered ; subsequently he covered the distance oil grass
in 9.30 2-s—another record ; and ’twas said that had
George laid himself out for it Lang’s professional figures
(9.11 J) would have been wiped out. ; that George did beat
both Lang’s 2nt. and his own mile (4.12 jsec.) in practice
was then an open secret. In the fulness of time English
amateur champion Alfred Shrubb has, by getting over the
journey in 9.11, topped all bests—amateur or professional.
* * » *
On the occasion of Andrews-Truscott fight at
Sydney National S.O. last week the club was in the pain-
ful position of having to refuse money. The fact of thfir
being “recognised top-notchers of different city centres
was the cause of the crush. Betting was all Andrews’
way (6 to 4 and, later, 2 to 1 on) and he did much the
better with a clean straight left that troubled Truscott
considerably, though neither had much respect for
t’other’s blows —they carried too little power. It was
mostly a scuffling, scrambling ’rastle, exciting always
because of its vigor and determination. Both made
the pace severe front the juipp and sustained it; they
were nearly as strong at the end as at the opening. Bar-
ring the 18th and 19th rounds wherein Truscott probably
held his own, it was all Andews’ fight, but the referee’s
verdict was a draw, and the great bulk of the crowd
cheered the decision. The public doesn’t know how
points are scored : as long as it sees two men making
matters “ willing,” and each doing his best, it is satisfied.
* * * »
Old-time, bare-knuckle fighter, Chris. Dunn,
whose list of successes in and about Sydney tots up
nearly 200, is almost at his tether’s end, and will be bene-
fited at Sydney Gaiety on '1 hursday night, this week.
He had a stirring scrap with Bill Lancaster at P.vrmont
one Sunday morning in the long ago, which lasted 35
rounds. A most stubbornly-contested and interesting
bruise, which ended in Dunn’s favor mainly through
superior condition. Police were strangely absent from
the neighborhood, but immediately after the sponge
went up they swarmed, but there was no one to arrest.
* * * *
Belmore (Sydney) Coursing Club ran a 04 dog
stake for the coin pooled and £5O added by Sydney
publicans. Conditions allowed only dogs that had never
won three courses in any stake to compete, which equal-
ised things so that last six dogs left in didn’t include one
that had been backed at card call prior to opening round,
and 20 to 1 was obtainable about ultimate winner (Miss
Licorice) evening before the final. Usual peculiar things,
but no official notice taken of them ; and yet coursing
people are wondering why the public is not interested.
Bathing Towel (the outsider in each instance) put Yabba,
one of the two first favorites, out in the second round,
and next day went within half-a-point, of treating the
other (Atalanta) similarly.
• * * *
Curtain-raiser at Sydney National S.C., Satur-
day night, was hottest fight seen for a long time, and
certainly the most torrid “preliminary” that club has
ever put on. Bight through, little Maorilander Gray and
Sydney’s tough morsel, Jim O'Neill, flew at each other as
though worked by springs. Both showed exceptional
capacity and unusually good bodily fix. Excitement
of the great onlooking crowd was intense after first
few blows, and remained so while, first, Gray dropped
O’Neill, and then O’Neill shook the Maorilander from
neck to heel. Gray came again like a wild cat.
O’-Neill’s remarkable grit and stamina alone enabled him
to live to stretch the enemy out in the fifth round and
win decisively in the sixth. O’Neill’s classic mug was a
picture—a convincing testimony of the potency of the
blows of the vanquished.
* * *
English billiardist Stevenson recently met
N.S.W. amateur champion J. B. Belfield, and conceded
him 500 in 1000. They were together at 744, and, ere the
finish, Stevenson got in a 238 run, ultimately winning 115
to the good. Best breaks were : Stevenson, 79, 39, 69,
114, 23, 65, 24, 50,85,238,79, 73, and 19 (unfinished);
Belfield, 20, 35, 37, 31, 20, 25, 35, 25 , 20, 22, and 28.
Belfield fell considerably short of his best form, otherwise,
the game might have gone to him.
**. * *
Stevenson-Gray billiard match (former conced-
ing 1200 in 5000) in Brisbane ended Saturday night, with
Stevenson best by 14 points.
* • * *
One of billiardist Stevenson’s best: Red hard
up against cushion, immediately behind spot, player in
hand. Bed must be potted in right hand bottom corner
pocket. Stevenson gets it from top right hand cushion,
but can’t make anything like a “cert” of the shot,
though he’ll wager ’tis done once in 12 attempts.

Dill-Mackyism at its Sydney annnal
demonstration was able to attract to its plat-
form these members of Parliament: Law,
Affleck, Mackenzie, Coleman,' M‘Coy, Hurley,
and Jessep. The little list admirably illustrates
the fact that it is only the politician having abso
lutely nothing else to rely upon who hitches him-
self to the noisome sectarian car. Of the seven
the majority are absolutely unknown to the
public. The average man, asked if there were a
N.S.W. M.P. named Coleman, or M‘Coy, or
Mackenzie, would promptly answer “No.” Those
of the seven who can boast a little notoriety
have won it in derision only.

Canada offers another remarkable example
of the level-headedness which invades a country
consequent upon its adoption of D.O.L. Whisky
as a national beverage. Every brainy Canadian
drinks D.C.L. as a matter of course. With the
result that for the past seven years Canada has
not added a penny to its public debt. D.C.L.
spells wisdom to the individual and wealth to the
nation.*
When a proprietary article gets to the stage of
having-jokes made on its name by music hall artists its
reputation is established. That is what has happened to
the “ Apollinaris ” Natural Mineral water. At London
Criterion the haughty stage dook commanded the varlet
at the farm at which he was staying to bring him a
whisky and polly in lieu of breakfast. “ Please, yer
grace, there’s no whisky in the house, and the girl’s
name is not Polly.” This splendid water, for which
Messrs. Orme, Keigwin and Co. are N.S.W. agents, is
naturally so effervescent that there is no need for arti-
ficial aeration. By means of an ingenious patent stopper
the bottle can be recorked and the freshness and effer-
vescence maintained for several days.*
Senator Higgs has begun a good fight in
the Senate against the naval tribute by a series of
questions calling attention to the fact that
Premier Barton, before leaving for England to
succumb to the Chamberlain mesmerism, was
distinctly pledged not to commit the Common-
wealth to any act or policy. And yet he came back
leaving with Chamberlain a P.N. for £2, 000,000 en-
dorsed by him on behalf of the Commonwealth.
Senator Higgs suggests that the proposed tribute
should be referred to the electors of Australia
before the agreement is ratified. It is a matter on
which the people have not yet been allowed to
express any opinion.
Premier Barton, opposing the amendment
substituting leasing for alienating of land in
Papua, wound up with a specious promise .-
He was only desirous that a decisive step should not be
taken, and if the amendment was not pressed he would
allow no alienation to take place before the fullest infor-
mation had been obtained from those in the best position
to judge.
M‘Millan backed this promise up, and said it was
entirely satisfactory. But what does it amount
to? Barton believes in land sales. Barton will
see that the “fullest information” is the kind
that encourages land sales. Barton will decide
that -‘those in the best position to judge” are
those who approve of land sales. And, anyhow,
Barton doesn’t promise to stop land sales even if
the “fullest information” is hostile to them.
Meanwhile, land sales are to be legalised.
Recently a very aggressive merchant (one
of the gentlemen who objected to being “herded
with common drunks” under the Customs ad-
ministration) made very loud and objectionable
charges against the State Taxation Department,
which charges were resented in vigorous and out-
spoken language in an official minute. In conse-
quence, one of the violent old importer’s friends
has given notice of the following motion in what
Kingston used to describe as “the Lethal
Chamber ”:—
That, in the opinion of this Council, it is desirable
that reports from officers of Government Departments
for presentation to Parliament, or for publication by
Ministers of the Crown, for the information of the public,
should be marked by that dignity of tone and courtesy
of expression appropriate to any official statement made
by the Minister himself.
The one valuable fact that appears in the
assault-and-battery story of King O’Malley and
the angry carpenter is Premier Barton’s prescrip-
tion for a congested liver. On the night when
the carpenter addressed him with a tomahawk,
King O’Malley had spoken to Barton about hts
liver, and the Premier recommended a strong dose
of hot milk and water as a certain cure. The dis-
tinguished banqueteer spoke from experience.
Life is not all beer and skittles for Australias
Nobbled Son ; there are intervals of hot milk and
water.
Kingston’s secession from the Ministry of
Australia’s Nobbled Son opens up some possibili-
ties. He must necessarily be, in fact if not in
name, the leader of the Labor party in the House
of Representatives. The Labor party must,
sooner or later, realise the necessity for widening
its basis, so as to become an all-round Democratic
party; a platform which isn’t wide enough to take
in a man like Kingston when he is available is too
narrow for practical purposes. And if Kingston
becomes attached to the party, the last semb’ance
of any policy of sinking the fiscal issue must dis-
appear. Kingston is too downright a man to
sink anything. If the Labor party will get a
wider basis, and an uncompromising financial
policy, there is before it a chance to form the
next Australian Government, with the best states-
man in Australia for leader.
Australia helped to subchowgate as well
as subjewgate the Transvaal.
As anticipated, the Education vote in
Philpland is to be further hacked into during pre-
sent session. Thus Brisbane Figabo, usually
“inspired ”: —
A Royal Commission is expected to be asked for this
session, to inquire into the working of the Department of
Public Instruction with a view to its thorough recon-
struction. The immediate reduction in the amount of
the vote, now standing at £310,000 per annum, will also be
dealt with, and all persons who are in a position to pay
for the education of their children will probably' be
reached by the collection of a weekly charge.
Late Chief-Justice Lilley was prime author of
the admirable Q. Education Act. It is enough
to make the old warrior turn in his grave to see
his democratic measure torn up by Cribbs and
Philps and other unimportant monosyllables of
their calibre.
A Melbourne pressman propounds this
view :
Me the Kingston surprise, this may be noted for future
verification. The surprise was only for the public, there
was a fairly good understanding inside. The Federal
elections are now casting their shadows before, and when
they come they will be minus Barton. His haven is a
High Court Judgeship. It has been fully recognised by
the leading men of the Cabinet that going to the elections
this time on the same basis as three years ago wouldn’t
by any means be good enough. There has to be a recon*
struction, and upon Barton’s retirement the succession
goes to Deakin. He, however, was the first to acknow-
ledge the stronger position of a Government led by
Kingston, whose present withdrawal paves the way for
the subsequent move. Just before the elections, and
with Barton out of the way, Deakin will advise the
Governor to send for Kingston, who, with a free hand to
form a new Cabinet, will be able to get rid of at least
three members of the present team who are regarded as
weaklings ; and with a strong Protectionist Government,
having also the solid support of the Labor party, can go
to the country in proper form to deal with the man of
the eye-glass.
The Bulletin doesn’t bet on this forecast, but it
hopes that it may be true —hopes in a hopeless
sort of way though ; for why should this thing be
expected of Deakin ?

“P” writes :
The re-modelling of the loco, branch of the Victorian
railways has been talked about vigorously since the
late strike. New by-laws have now been issued showing
how this is to be done. Hitherto the enginemen’s hours
have been nine daily. They are now eight. This alleged
eight looks all right—on paper. But no time will be
allowed for getting engine ready before or putting it away
after the trip. Three-quarters of an hour before and the
Same after have hitherto been allowed for this work (it
was increased from half an hour some years ago), and
often the men have had to be on duty from one to one
and a half hours before starting to get some of the
engines ready especially since the policy of
neglecting repairs and cleaning has been the
rule in the department. The three-quarter hour
allowed to put the engine away is also often exceeded—
notably in large sheds where several engines are waiting
their turn for coal, water, etc. Thus the new day’s
work, though nominally 8 hours, is really 9j ! Further
for “ standing under steam” only half time is allowed,
unless the day is made up without it. Then no time is
allowed. Hence if Jack Smith, driver, and Tom Brown,
fireman, are sent on a train to a place where they have to
wait 5 or 6 hours before returning they (though responsible
for their engine the whole time) are only paid at half
rates; or, if the journey is a long one, and they make
their day while actually running the train, they are paid
nothing at all! The possibility of Smith and Brown
leaving their engine go to pot, and the engine getting
short of water and blowing up, or the gauge glasses break-
ing ; or somebody coming along and starting the derelict
off on its own; or many other contingencies of an alarming
and humorous character—all these are worth reflecting
upon. Also S. and 8., not being paid to get their engine
ready, and nobody else being paid to do it, they are
likely to go out with their engine partly oiled, or the
piston gland nuts unchecked, or big end set screws
Blacked back, or pins out of the link motions, or a sheaf
loose on the crank axle, or any one or more of dozens of
other things may be out of gear—any one of which would
be fatal to the progress of the train. When on top of all
this “ remodelling” of the loco, branch the recently ap-
pointed blacklegs are trusted out to drive engines the
position will become more interesting than ever.
Sydney clairvoyants are up to date in
political matters. One recently pleased a client
by prophesying that he would be in a few
years Prime Minister of the Commonwealth.
The client, who is secretary of a debating club,
has already started to live up to his future.
“E.F.” : What I like about that address
presented to the Chermsides by the State-school
children of Irvinebank (N.Q.) is the religious
sentiment of the concluding lines—“We wish you
both all health and happiness now and hereafter.”
Everybody hugs the hope of enjoying a healthful,
jolly Hereafter, but you never hear the future
state spoken of in that way by the parsons. With
them it is either damnation or regeneration. The
idea of a cheerful three-meals-a-day Eternity is
never suggested, not even to vice-regal sinners.

About a cold country :
The rush of the English emigrants to the land in
Canada isn’t the unmixed blessing it seems to be when
viewed from a distance. A dejected band of would-be
landlords returned to Liverpool lately after trekking
across three-parts of the Dominion and considering its
national resources from all points. The dismal aspect of
things in general, the awful north-west climate, and the
unutterably inferior quality of the land offered drove
them back. England is a good deal worried over the
episode. The British islands are mostly uncultivated, and
great stretches are now marked on the map as unin-
habited, but there seems to be no hope of bringing the
land into cultivation again without Protection, and with-
out dispossessing the big landlords and replacing them by
a peasant proprietary. The latter idea is too horrible
to be thought of in a country which worships its old no-
bility, so the British agricultural population must go. The
essential idea is to prevent it going, out of the Empire.
Australia is remote and barren ; South Africa is barren
and cursed with every insect plague vinder heaven ;
India and most other British territories are overflowing
with niggers. If the pallid snow-wastes of North
Western Canada won’t attract the people, then the
British agriculturist is likely either to leave the Empire
for good, or else stay in Britain and furnish a horrible
and dangerous object lesson on the evils of landlordism
and Freetrade. The Times, in a struggle to earn its
bacon and codfish honorably, has spoken severely to these
returned immigrants, and assured them that they weren’t
so cold as they imagined, but no good appears to have
resulted. They knew exactly how cold they were, and
all the eloquence the Times can use beside its comfortable
office stove doesn’t alter the case.
