The Holy Red Herring—Again.
The Protestant flag must flj’ on every election hustings,
both in Federal and State politics. Those who were not
for them were against them. There must be no blending
of colors—orange with the green. Orange color was good
enough to stand by itself. Though he was in public life,
he cared not whether a Laborite or a Protectionist was
to be returned, but he must be a Protestant.— Wiles,
M.H.R.
The Protestant Defence Association would bring about
a revolution at the next general election. (Cheers.) He
believed the female vote would elevate the morals of the
State. The Roman Catholic vote at the next election
would be a tremendous one, and every Protestant woman
who failed to use her franchise would be giving a vote to
Roman Catholicism. He implored them, therefore, to
come out as true women and do their duty.— Parson
Dill-Mackt.
Last 12th of July the Orange coterie made
a more than usually violent demonstration, and
announced that its flag must fly something extra
this year with a view to the forthcoming elections.
It stated, on no visible evidence except its own
word (which is certainly not good enough), that a
vast effort (not at present visible) is being organ-
ised by the Homan Catholics to capture the
Federal Legislature for some purpose not speci-
fied, and it urged all Protestants to resist this
alleged effort to the uttermost. In view of the
facts that in Australia Homan Catholics only
number about one-fifth of the population, that
even if every Catholic voted and only one-
half the Protestants did so the latter would still
have a majority of two to one, that the Catholic
vote was not strong enough to secure for the
local head of the church even tenth place on the
list of N.S. W. members at the Federal Conven-
tion, and that the alleged boss R.C. schemer
and conspirator has gone away to Italy, leaving
the Federal election to take care of itself—
in view of all these facts, the evidence
looks none too good, but that may pass. The
Orange crowd says, at all events, that a special
R.O. effort is being made, and that it is necessary
to elect good howling Orangemen—men like
Francis Abigail and Wilks and old Tory Simon
Fraser, and draper Law, of Balmain—-in order
to make the country safe and prevent the Lord
tottering on His throne. And there have been
such violent hysterics over this matter that the
calm, sordid, business-like aspect of the case is in
danger of being lost. The bray of the wild ass of
incoherence has hitherto drowned everything else
and made it quite impossible to obtain particulars.
* * * *
This paper, therefore, asks the Dill-Wilks
crowd, and the Abigail elan, and the other people
who believe politics to be “ more popery than
fiscal” to simmer down and come to facts and
dates, and to say exactly what will happen if they
are returned to power in the Federal Parliament,
and what will happen if they are not. If Aus-
tralia takes the advice of Wilks and drops all
questions of its own material prosperity, and
leaves out of consideration tariffs, the promotion
of local industries, black labor problems, defence,
debt consolidation, graduated land-tax, absentee-
tax, land settlement, old age pensions, transcon-
tinental railways, etc., and returns men solely
because they are good Orangemen like Francis
Abigail— what are these extraordinarily good men
going to do ? What will happen even in a religious
sense that wouldn’t happen otherwise? In a
political sense it seems fairly probable that things
will go to the dogs, for these extraordinarily good
men are to be elected without giving any guarantee
that they will look after the people’s material
interests in any way, and are to travel into
politics on their Protestantism only; but there
ought to be religious compensations. In short,
what is Protestantism, as distinct from Wilks
and Simon Fraser, going to get out of it ? It
isn’t worth while electing a Parliament solely for
its Protestantism if Protestantism is to gain
nothing visible or definite.
» * » *
The first question that calls for consideration
is this : Supposing it were possible (which it
isn’t) for theßoman Catholic element, representing
one-fifth of the people, to get any large control of
the Australian. Parliament, what do Dill-Maoky
and co. allege is the object in getting that control,
and what particular or startling thing will happen?
This is an important question, and so far, despite
all the foggy shrieks about the intense resolve of
the .Catholics to make themselves felt at the
coming election, there isn’t a word of information
about what harm, if any, will follow on their
success. The second question, of course, is : What
will happen if that violently Orange Legislature
that Dill-Wilks demands is returned ? Does it
propose to disfranchise all Catholics ? Or to debar
them from sitting in Parliament ? Or to shut them
out of the Ministry ? Or to eject them from the
Civil Service ? Or to fire them out of the country ?
Does it propose to endow Protestant churches and
Protestant schools, and to subsidise Protestant
bishops ? Does it propose to do anything in parti-
cular—anything that, say, R. E. O’Connor might
not do just as well? If it doesn’t, then the public is
to get no fun for its money; it is to sacrifice every-
thing else for the joy of electing Orange fanatics
and get no profit, entertainment, dog-fight or
circus, whatsoever; it is to neglect all its
material needs and political ideals to howl for
Protestantism and get no extra Protestantism in
return for its howl. On that basis the Dill-
Macky and Wilks business is a confidence game,
a political and religious three-card trick, a very
disreputable kind of “ two-up ” business, a bunco-
steering job, and something akin to the passing of
bad money in a churchy sort of way.
* * » *
Of course the 12th of July mob can always take
refuge in hazy generalities. It can say that it
wants to get control of the Australian Parlia-
ment, not that it may do anything especially Pro-
testant, but that it may prevent the other crowd
doing something rabidly Catholic. Unfortunately
it is quite as difficult to persuade the Dill-Wilks
crowd to say what it is going to prevent the other
crowd doing, as to extract from it any information
as to what it is going to do itself. It doesn’t profess
to say that anybody aims at disfranchising Pro-
testants ; at expelling them from the Parliament
or the Ministry or the Civil Service ; or at
charging Cardinal Moran to the consolidated
revenue. N.S. W. has a partly Catholic Ministry
and it has the Fitzroy Dock scandal. It had a
wholly or almost wholly Protestant Ministry some
years ago and it had the Hornsby lands scandal.
The two Ministries scattered the loan money in
about equal degree. But N.S.W. didn’t get a
penn’orth of religious joy or satisfaction or practi-
cal conflagration out of either of them ; for all the
help they were in an official capacity to their faiths,
O’Sullivan might as well have been a Mohamme-
dan, and Abigail a Buddhist. There is very
little pleasure in rallying round the faith when
the faith is just in the same position whether it is
rallied round or not. The joy of battle is a thing
anybody can understand ; but the joy of waving a •
gun on an uninhabited island, and declaring that
one will fight to the last when there is nobody to
fight with, only appeals to a very humble order of
intellect.
* * * *
What the Dill-Wilks gang hopes to get out
of the deal is obvious enough. It asks for office,
Ministerial and Parliamentary salary—for all the
loaves and fishes that are going. The axe it
is grinding is a very visible axe indeed. In
return it promises vaguely to avert a crisis or
something like that, but it doesn’t produce the
crisis, and it doesn’t say what it is or how it is
going to be averted. If Parson Dill-Macky has
a crisis in his pocket he certainly ought to bring it
out. The public wants to know exactly what the
Bill crowd, if it gets into authority, is going to
do to the Catholics, or exaotly what it is going to
prevent the Catholios doing to other people. The
public seeks something more definite than mere
mental flappings about “ Romish supremacy; ” it
is anxious to find out what Romish supremacy the
country is threatened with if Wilks and foolish
old Simon Fraser are not elected. If the Catholic
clan is supposed to have too much power, it wants
to know exactly how much power that clan
has; how much it should have; how much
it possesses over and above its fair share;
and how that obnoxious surplus is going to be
reduced. It wants to know, for instance, whether
R. B. O’Connor will be simply deprived of his
Ministerial position because of his religion, or if
he will also be expelled from Parliament; and if
it isn’t proposed that any of these things should
happen, then it wants to know what it is proposed
shall happen. There is no visible use electing
Wilks, Simon Fraser and Co. on the cry of
keeping Catholics out of power and then leaving
an eminent and influential Catholic in power.
Also there is no use electing Wilks, Simon Fraser
and co. on the wail about Catholics in the Civil
Service unless Wilks and old Simon, if they
get into authority, will either discharge Catholics
from the service on account of their creed, or else
establish some kind of religious test to prevent
more Catholics getting in. And they don’t
promise anything; they don’t even guarantee to
throw a brick through the window of St. Mary’s
Cathedral, nor to burn the Cardinal’s palace that
roosts on the bleak hillside at Manly, nor to sup-
press the Mass, nor to abolish convents, nor to
close up Catholic schools and orphanages, nor to
prohibit the teaching of the Holy Roman creed,
nor to sool a dog on to a priest—in fact, they don’t
guarantee, if the people put them into political
power, to give Protestantism any kind of a lift
whatever. Apparently the only change will be
that the public finances and other public affairs
will be entrusted to Wilks and co.
Thk particular feature of Kingston’s Federal
Patents Bill that stamps him as a genuine,
practical Australian patriot, is contained in
clause 83, which enacts that the patentee or his
representatives must, within five years after the
issue of the patent, manufacture or work his
patent, in Australia, in such manner that any
person desiring to use the patented article may
obtain it in Australia at a reasonable prioe. And
no article manufactured under patent may be
imported by the patentee into Australia after
four years from the granting of the patent. If
the patentee hasn’t, by the specified date, got his
own Australian factory going, he may.be com-
pelled to grant licences for the manufacture of the
article in Australia. This is, to a great extent, a
copy of the German law ; but it might reasonably
go further, and declare that if the manufacture of
the patented article is not commenced in Australia
by a certain date the patent shall become abso-
lutely null and void. The principle that no man
has a right, even if he possesses 1000 patents, to
retard the growth of Australian manufactures
can’t be laid down too emphatically. _ His patent
granted by Australia gives him the right to be the
sole manufacturer in Australia, and to charge
pretty well what he darned well likes in Aus-
tralia, for the clause about a “ reasonable price ”
is very vague and non-committal ; and that is
quite sufficient. Any evasion of these liberal
provisions should result in plain forfeiture. Yet
Kingston’s Bill is a large move in the right
direction, and is sure to rouse the wrath of the
importer class, whose feelings, when any mention
is made of Australian manufacturers, are too deep
for words.

NO NEED TO DESPAIR
You may have been suffering for years from R h eum at ism,
Gout, Neuralgia, Persistent Headache, Lum-
bago, Sciatica, Backache, Anaemia, Blood
Disorders, Gravei, Stone, Bladder Troubles,
or some other of the many ills, pains and symptoms of
URIC ACID POISONING,
but, if you will consent to assist Nature, you can yet be cured. The source of all such troubles is
defective action of the kidneys. When the kidneys are healthily performing their functions none of
these disorders can exist, any more than a pebble in a shoe can hurt your foot after the pebble is
removed. The kidneys filter from the blood of a healthy person about three pints of water every day.
In this water are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten or twelve grains in weight of uric acid, and
other animal and mineral matter, varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the
kidneys, from any cause, are unable to do their work properly, then some of this solid matter remains
in the system and becomes actively poisonous. Minute crystals of uric acid form in the blood and are
deposited in the joints and tissues of the body, causing the acute pain experienced when we have
Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, &c. Sometimes the uric acid crystals
aggregate together in the kidneys and form Gravel and Stones, and cause Kidney Colic and
Irritation and Inflammation of the Bladder. In fact, nearly all the aches and pains to which
the human body is subject are uric in their origin. It will be easily realised, therefore, how important
it is that we should
WATCH THE KIDNEYS,
and make sure that they are properly doing their work of removing these urinary poisbns from the
system, as it necessarily follows that the absence of the poisons means absence of the pains they cause
The secret of the suceess which has attended the treatment of uric ailments by
Warner’s Safe Cure
is that the medicine aots in a scientific manner. It is a specific cure for all diseases of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER.
So intimate is the relation,between the functions of the kidneys and those of the liver that it is
necessary, in order to cure of either of those organs, to employ an agent which possesses*
an equally curative action on estch. In'other words, when the kidneys are sick, the liver is sick also,
and the contrary is likewise true. Warner’s Safe Cure is the only known remedy which possesses the
remarkable property of being of equal efficacy in cases of diseases of the kidneys and diseases of the
liver. By establishing a healthy and vigorous action of both the kidneys and the liver. Warner’s
Safe Cure cures not only all diseases due to uric poisoning, but Indigestion, Eil ousness,
Jaundice, Sick Headache, Anaemia, and Debility, which are all caused by the liver failing to
deal efficiently with the biliary waste products of the used-up tissues in the body.
Mrs. A. J. L. Barnett, Brighton Road, Glenelg, S.A., says:—“l suffered greatly from mv kid-
neys and liver, and after consulting several doctors and getting no relief I had given up all hope of

The commonest first symptom of kidney disease is Fain in the back. A surer test is to place
some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass and let it stand until next
morning. If it is then cloudy or there is a brick-dust like sediment or if particles float about in it,

Broken Hill Warden’s Court has two “jump-
ing ” cases before it at the present time. One concerns
the old Mayflower Extended mine, owned by one of the
Barrier’s identities, William Blunt, for the past 17 years.
The mine, worked almost singlehanded the whole time,
has yielded a lot of good ore, and is believed to hold a
good deal more. The alleged jumpers have hurt their
own case in the public mind by pegging out the claim
while guests of the old man, to whom the mine is the
only thing on earth worth living for.
* * * •
By the number of metallurgists and practical
mining men to be seen in Sydney of late one would
assume that there was quite a boom or that some-
thing of the sort was looming. Such is not the case,
however. All these men are mixed up in an im-
portant law-suit which is proceeding. Some dissatis-
fied shareholders in the Lake George United Mines
have instituted a suit against the principal pro-
moters of the co. for the recovery of money paid for
shares. They allege that the prospectus misrepresented
the property and that the promoters knew from the out-
set that it was worthless. The case is to be tried in Lon-
don, and the most eminent counsel of the day are
engaged. Evidence is being taken in Australia on com-
and quite an array of Sydney and Melbourne
lawyers is employed. The evidence and “ exhibits” will
be bulky enough to necessitate the chartering of a special
steamship to convey them to Bull’s island. Bulletin
expresses no opinion on the merits of the case ; but does
not hesitate to say that it is more than a pity that one
quarter of the money now being spent in law was nob
made available to prospect the mine at deeper levels to
determine whether or not there is a richer zone below
present Workings. As an ore body the Captain’s Flat lode
is without rival in N.S.W. for width and length, and were
it found to be one per cent, richer in copper the mine
would become one of the first in the State.
* * * *
At the Lloyd copper mine, Burraga (N.S.W.),
Manager Corbould is applying the new broom with
- much vigor. The proportion of officials and bosses to
actual workers is being considerably reduced and all-
round retrenchment made. But Mr. Corbould can’t sack
the concentrating mill, and this is the incubus which is
going to turn the scale against the hopes of shareholders.
This mine affords a striking example of the fallacy of the
assumption that modern men and methods necessarily
mean success where old-fashioned systems would mean
failure. The late Lewis Lloyd’s methods of working the
mine were primitive in the extreme, but they were so
successful that it did not give the practical Welshman
much concern when copper went as low as £4O per ton.
The co. has spent about £lOO,OOO ; it has had the benefit
of copper at an average of nearly £6O, yet it has never
got within coo-ee of paying. Had Mr. Lloyd lived and
retained possession of the mine the chances are that he
would have made profits almost equal to the profitless
capital expenditure of the co. Mine is as good as ever it
was, and the one hope for the co. is for the London
directors to cease their meddlesome harassing policy
and give the new man reasonable time to demonstrate
what can be done with the show.
* * * *
The full text of the will of the late James
Dick, the Glasgow man who made an immense fortune
out of Balata belting, has been recently published. It
discloses many magnificent benefactions to public insti-
tutions and to old servants. Among the latter is Mr.
David Manwell, the manager of the Mitchell’s Creek
mine (N.S.W.), who has the right to purchase the mine
for £42,000 within such time and on such conditions as
the trustees may decide, Lucky Manwell! The newly-
erected plant must have cost close on £30,000, and the
mine must be returning a profit of at least £lO,OOO a year.
How this news must gladden the heart of “Tommy"
Dalveen, an ex-partner in the mine !
* * * / *
Cobar Chesney chairman announced at recent
meeting that 80,000 out of 180,000 shares in the co. had
been forfeited for non-payment of calls. What a satire it
is on the alleged speculative: spirit of Australians—Vic-
torians in particular—when men will sacrifice their
interest in a mine of such great probabilities rather than
pay a 3d. call 1 In magnitude of proved lode and in
average value of ore Chesney is one of the big mines of
Australia. Some day, either by design or by accident,
the right-method of treatment will be hit upon, and
then the men who have dropped out for 3d. will
be tumbling over each other to get in again at £l.
Several fair “ rises” have already been made out of deals '
in Chesney forfeited shares, and one of these days some
knowing people will gather in a big “ pot.” There is al-
ways thechance of some enterprising outsider making a
fair offer for the mine. This will be something more than
a chance—almost a certainty—should the big Cobar deal
eventuate, and of this those in the know profess to have
no doubt- Capital of co. to be increased by 2s. 6d. per
share. Manager Woolcock asserts that the concentrating
plant is the most complete of its class in Australia,-but he
cannot work it three shifts owing to inability to get com-
petent men to look after it.

Doyle and Fynne.y, Kanowna (W.A.), have
treated another 12 tons for 1430 z. This makes the total
for 18 months 27640 z. from 141 tons.' For some time the
outlook was gloomy, but recently very rich stone was
again struck, and some very high returns may be looked
for.
* * * *
White Feather Main Reefs (W.A.) is looking
rather dicky in the bottom level. The main reef at 750 ft.
is very poor, and, if the east reef doesn’t come to the
rescue, the returns will get a nasty set back. The
opening up of the east reef is being eagerly pushed ahead
to try and avert a collapse. Mine under Nat. Harper’s
management has had a pretty successful career, having
paid £58,000 in divs., the last in April. Current profits
range from £lOOO to £llOO monthly, so there is not much
margin for any decline in the grade of ore. All other
mines on the same line of reef have been utter failures.
The White Feather Reward, of which great things were
predicted, is no good at 500 ft., to which depth the new
main shaft was carried ; and, after stoping out. a narrow
chute of pay ore and spending the balance of available
cash, the co. must again collapse. Possibly it may be
reconstructed, though the mine does not justify it.
* * * *
At the Atlas, Kalpini(W.A.), in breaking down
stone for the mill, it is found that the ore at the bottom,
or 300 ft. level, is very poor, and it will be quite impossible
to maintain an output of 1000 tons monthly of lOdwt. ore.
Any serious decline would render the proposition a drain
on John Bull’s pockets. The July return will be all right,
but, after that, anything. The mine is splendidly
equipped for economical working, but machinery can’t
make gold, as many mine managers have found in-the
land of sand and sorrow. Manager Irvine, who has,
unfortunately, like many other practical men, had no
luck off the fields, has resigned, as the prospect 6f
running a mine without cash is not enticing.
* * * *
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), will start
crushing with its 10-head mill early in August, and should
yield very high returns judging from trial parcels. Owing
to the promising outlook the £1 shares have been split,
and co. now consists of 200,000 shares of 2s. each. Profits.,
should amount to at least £5OOO monthly, and regular 6d. .
divs. be distributed every month. The £1 shares have
changed hands at £8 to £9, so the split shares will come
on-the market in the neighborhood of a sovereign. There
has. hitherto been no free market in the scrip, which is
all held locally, but, once returns begin, this will pro-
bably be altered. The mine has been developing for the .
past four years ; a large tonnage of high-grade ore
has been revealed, and the ore going underfoot in the
lowest workings is the richest in the mine. Returns
are expected to run up to 2000 oz. a month.
v - * * * *
Associated (W.A.) is pushing on hard with the
erection of its second half-dozen Merton furnaces, the
first half-dozen having ajready got to work. In spite of
making alterations in the treatment plant, the output of
6000 oz. monthly is maintained, and it is expected that it
will be raised to 8000 oz. from September. The mine is
opening up splendidly in the lower levels, and the ore
reserves are now so large that a long period of prosperity
and divs. may be looked for. Besides its present ore
reserves, the co. has the chance of cutting the continua-
tion of the Oroya chute in its lower levels. Regarding
this chute, if it maintained its dip, it should enter the
leases at a depth of 900 ft., but it can’t be found. Oroya
has still 250 ft. to drive to its southern boundary, and
when this drive is complete, the mystery will be solved, y
* * * *
Murchison Associated, Day Dawn (W.A.), has
just been reconstructed for the sixth time in eight years,
its capital being increased from 300,000 shares of 6s. each
to 400,000 of similar value. Go. owns a property named
the Rubicon, about a mile north of the Great Fingall
Consols, and once owned by that co., and is generally
admitted to be working the same reef. At least the color
and character of the quartz and the country rock are
identical. The reef is very large, and rather low-grade,
just as the Great Fingall reef was in the oxidised zone,
and that is the only zone really tested so far. Possibly,
as in the case of the Great Fingall, opera-bouffe mining
will, some day, give way to real development; and, when
the day arrives, the mine may make a name for itself.
* * * *
Vosperton (W.A.), an absolute wild-cat, has
reconstructed for the second time, with a capital of
£120,000 in 10s. shares, with a 2s. assessment. First-class
headgear and a 10-head mill were erected some three
years ago, and photos of these were displayed at the
co.’s meeting to gladden the hearts of shareholders and
induce them to put their hands in their pockets. Mine
never produced any gold, and it is very doubtful if it ever
will. Such alleged gold mines merely serve to damage
the reputation of a country, and should be squelched.
* * * «
Great Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) continues
to develop splendidly, and everything on the mine
runs smoothly. The eighth level, at 1100 ft., is being
opened up with the usual satisfactory results. The
L.Y. Consols has been proving the southern end of the
Perseverance property, and, as rich ore has been found
in both lodes at deeper levels close to the boundary, the
Perseverance’s prospects look very rosy. For some
occult reason, the usual quarterly div. of Is. 3d. a share,
or £87,500, due in July, is not to be paid till the middle
of August. Manager Nichols has been inspecting the
South Kalgurli, prior to re-starting operations, and has
strongly eulogised late manager Isles’ work, and condoled
with the latter over the scarcity of gold. As Nichols has
had a ghastly experience with the Great Boulder South
and Boulder Bonanza, his sympathy for Isles is readily
explained.
* * * .
North Kalgurli (W.A.) is pushing ahead with
the erection of its new 10-head mill and cyanide plant,
and should have a trial run by the end of August.
Management estimates that the ore reserves amount to
30,000 tons of 15dwt. stone, but that seems a pretty tall
order. There are in all 11 levels driven, and, in most,
the ore is very patchy and difficult to estimate. Once
stoping commences the true size of the lodes will be seen,
and it may be found, as in the case of its neighbor (the
Hainault), that the lodes bulge to enormous proportions
in places, and greatly increase the reserves. The co. is
now practically without funds, and the future of the
mine depends entirely on mill returns.

Associated Northern (W.A.) management has
practically abandoned hope of finding the ore alleged to
have been cut by the drill at 912 ft. last Nov. The official
report said the lode was 12ft. 6in. wide, of which 6in. was
worth 60z., Bft. lldwt., and 4ft. 21dwt. How such a
large body of ore, if it ever existed, could abso-
lutely vanish is mysterious. Yet crosscutting, driving
and rising have failed to locate it, even down to
950 ft. The diamond drill was also said to have cut Bto
10ft. of ore worth soz. per ton at 70ft. west of the shaft at
a depth of 1100 ft. Further drilling is now being dope to
see if any such body of ore can bp located. Boring is also
being prosecuted on the Iron Monarch and MedindieHill,
and, although a large body of pay ore was said to have !
been found at 300 ft. on the former, people are now i veryi®
sceptical. Chairman Landau’s resignation, and the ’drop*,
in the price of the shares, since Capt. Rodda’s death, of
about 405., equivalent to £700,000, shake the confidence'
of the most confiding.

W.A. Government, rhas practically agreed to
reduce cost of “scheme 1 ’ water from 6s. to ss. per 1000
gallons to Kalgoorlie mines on condition that the cos.

Mines guarantee “to take 500,000 gallons daily at the

* *■ * * ,
Sons of Gwalia, Leonora (W.A.), looks as if it
had a long period of prosperity before it. Six levels are
now being operated—Nos. 7 to 12—and an average of
nearly 800 ft. of development is being done monthly, of
which one-fourth is driving levels. At No. 7 level pay ore
is being won nearly 300 ft. south of where the lode pinched,
in the upper levels, and about 700 ft. south of the shaft.
At No. 8 level nearly 900 ft. of ore has been opened up, of
which about one-third is north of the shaft. This is the
only level in which the lode has been followed to the
north. Bevels 9to 12 are all being driven south, and in
each the lode runs from Bft. to 10ft. wide, and can safely
be valued at 15dwt., and shows much higher assays in
places. Although nearly 8000 tons of ore is being treated!
monthly, the reserves are increasing at practically the
same rate, and are now two years ahead of the mill.
Working costs.have been greatly reduced. The co. looks
good for about 4s. a year in divs.
* * * *
Briseis tin shares have slightly improved ins
value of late consequent on the assurance given by Mr.
Lindsay Olarke that he is confident that he can remove
the overburden expeditiously and economically. ,The
same confidence has been expressed by other managers.
If the co. changes managers often enough and can put up
money enough, the overburden will be removed in time,
and this having been done, the next thing will be to
recover the tin. There will be a greater disillusionising
in this respect than there has been over the removal of
the overburden. Anyhow, the powers that be must have
a poor opinion of the show when to maintain a semblance
of interest in it they have to resort to the doubtful policy
of changing managers annually. . .

Queen Centrals (Charters Towers) have within a,
month slumped from 28s. (paids) to about 19s. This is due
to shaft not having so far bottomed on the rich Victoria
lode worked in the adjoining Queen Cross. It is now
thought that some formation passed through at about
930 ft. is the Victoria ; but the shaft (at time of writing
about 1000 ft. deep) is to be continued another 100 ft. in
the hope of cutting the Brilliant reef, supposed to exist
in the Central ground at about that depth. This done,
the formation above, if the Victoria reef is not met with
in the me£htiMhj'' J \Mli , %e' driven upon. Present appear-
ances of the Cross make the Central’s chances look
healthy, and it is a curious fact that in the Towers it is
the exception rather than the rule for a straight shaft to-
bottom off stone. The Brilliant owes its career as a
dividend payer: to-.a few feet of driving on an apparently
worthless vein, while the Queen Cross’s position is due to
a cross-cut into the Queen Cross footwall.

The flag was hoisted at the Inglewood (Vic.)
Great Columbian mine on the 23rd ult. in honor of the
co.’s first dividend. It is 12 years since a registered co.
in the Inglewood district (the Maxwell’s Extended) paid a
dividend. The directors travelled up to Geo. Lansell’s-
house, at Bendigo, to formally declare the div., the old
battler having stuck to the co. for years.

Sydney musical public will learn with regret that their
veteran basso, Tom Rainford, now in his 73rd year, has
been stricken with paralysis. The plucky old fellow was
in harness until recently.
Tom Buckley, the popular Australian Irish comedian of
yore, tells how Nellie Stewart came to him as a young
damsel to finish her juvenile education in Irish jig danc
ing and side-drum playing. “And didn’t she just work 1”
says Buckley. The girls who are pretending to be artists
nowadays don’t understand the meaning of the word
“ work ” as Nellie Stewart understood it.
Melba’s re-appearance in London after her Australian
tour seems to have impressed many people with evi-
dence of her “ further advance as an actress.” Before
that masterful woman’s voice is fairly on the wane she
will probably be accepted as the finest of operatic trage-
diennes in two or three of her favorite characters. It she
couldn’t sing a note, Melba would be good' enough to
head an ordinary dramatic company in “ Camille.”
Olive Lenton, the youngest of the young members of
Williamson’s Musical Comedy Co., is a daughter of the
late Lance Lenton, actor and journalist.
“Wongim”: Watching the public at the Mutoscopes
came to the conclusion that the machines labelled “ High
Kicking,” “Peeping Tom,” “Maiden’s Midnight Romp,"
&c., would soon break down through overwork, whilst
those entitled “ Riding with Kitchener,” “ Coronation
Scenes,” &c., will probably die of starvation. As I pos-
sessed a penny at the time, had a look at one of the first-
mentioned, and was shocked. When I have another
penny am going to get shocked again.
Speaking of the death of comedian Charles Lauri from
consumption, M.A.P. says that the nature of Lauri’s work
fostered, if it did not really engender, the disease from
which he died. His animal mimicry necessitated his
wearing a furry skin, into which he was tightly laced.
Wrapped in this almost air-proof leather garment, Lauri
was in the habit of cavorting for hours in the heat of
the footlights, performing acrobatic and muscular feats ;
so that, when he cast off the outer animal at the close of
his turn, he was habitually bathed in a violent perspira-
tion. M.A.P. sums up Lauri, in fact, as a man who died
through pretending to be a beast.
Some of the late Davison Symmons’ smartest work was
done impromptu. At the close of the Gaiety Co.’s season
in Melbourne, seven or eight years ago, he wrote some
encore verses for Louis Bradfield to the song “She
Wanted Something to Play With.” Bradfield sang them
to a packed house on the last night, and was roared at
for more. He went back to sing the last verse again, and
as he did so bumped into Symmons, who was standing in
the wings. “I suppose you couldn’t grind out another
verse while I’m at this?” said Bradfield hurriedly. Sym-
mons grabbed an old envelope, and when Bradfield came
back he had a new verse ready. The house was still
howling for more. Bradfield went on to the stage holding
the envelope in his ever-present top-hat and sang the new
verse, which gained almost as much applause as all the
rest of the song put together.
“Nani”: The bills and advertisements re opening of
the new Her Majesty’s announce that the manager,
J.0.W., will appear in Buokstone’s farce of “ Cousin Joe.”
Buckstone never wrote any farce of that name. The title
of the piece is the “ Rough Diamond,” and Cousin Joe is
merely one of the characters in it. This changing of the
name of a play in order to give prominence to an actor
appearing in it is the reverse of artistic, and is only
another instance of the feverish anxiety many actor-
managers have to pose as the Great High Cockalorum
of the concern. Fancy an actor changing the name of
“Othello” to that of “ Iago” simply because he happened
to be playing that part.

“ Hamlet ” : A certain English actress, imported by
one of our leading managers, is malting herself disliked
allround. She treats the “small parts” and walking
ladies as if they were mere cattle, and openly throws in-
sulting remarks at them. In a certain Australian capital,
t’other day, some ladies from the localConservatoHiim of
Music, specially engaged to sing in one production, came
down the stairs to the stage. Going up the stairs, $as the
imported star, and as the local ladies apptostonetl she
drew to one side, saying to her companion, “ Let Its wait
till the rabble has passed.” The rabble hasn’t quite , de-
cided whether to cowhide the speaker or to publish her
father’s occupation, which was honest but humble, and
not at all commensurate with the daughter’s frills.
A well-trained elephant is to a big circus what a
hammer is to a carpenter. Wirth Bros, have an
elephantess, Ghuni Sah by name, who, in addition to
being a big show item, is woman of all work, housemaid,
porter, and washer-up to the show. At country towns
Ghuni Sah draws the menagerie, a dozen cages at a
time, from train to playingground. She pulls up the poles,
raises the canvas, takes along a string of two or three
heavily-loaded lorries, and exl racts troublesome stakes.
On several occasions during the recent Queensland tour,
when shunting engines were not available, Ghuni Sah
managed all the shunting herself.

“New South Wales carries the bundle
every time,” the vacuous Bill Wilks shouted at
Treasurer Turner in the middle of the Budget
speech ; and Turner noticed him just enough to
say that the statement wasn’t true. As a matter
of fact, apart from the special Westralian
tariff, the average customs collections for
1903-4 are estimated at £2 ss. sd. per head
throughout the Commonwealth ; but in N.S.W.
the collections will, it is estimated, amount to
only £2 3s. lOd. Bunching the three years 1901 to
1904 the figures are—for the whole Common-
wealth, £6l9s. ; for N.S.W. alone, £6 14s. 4|d.
As to total receipts, the position is just the same;
for the three years N.S.W. has averaged 3615 per
cer.t. of the Commonwealth’s population, but nas
either contributed, or (as for the current year)
is expected to contribute, only 35"3 per cent, of
the Commonwealth’s revenue. No wonder a
man who makes such stupid, mischievous state-
ments as Will Bilks made in this case wants the
public to forget what a. political fool he is, and
keep its eye glued on the fact that he is solid on
the Yellow Pup question.
The pretty Sydney suburb of Bondi owns
a park, and the dirty municipal council of
Waverley keeps a tip there. Some miserably
unreliable person seems to have decided that so
long as the tip was covered it was quite healthy,
so a thin disguise of sand was put on top
of the festering mound, which is (or was)
balanced on the side of a hill. The late heavy
rains, of course, washed off most of the disguise,
and also washed a great part of the tip downhill
<on to the adjacent cow-grass and into a stream
that cows drink out of, and Bondi has now about
half an acre of naked stench and typhoid on its
hands. We are a primitive people.
Bishop Webber has at last dropped ex-
hausted in his 17-year-long chase after enough
■coin to build a cathedral at Brisbane —his death
is reported this week. He was a man who
had to be begging for something and promoting
something. If it hadn’t happened that he was
born in a holy atmosphere, and was subsequently
headed into the clerical rut, he would most likely
have developed into another Tattersall and run a
monster tote; or lie might have gone ahead of a
circus, and enthused about the splendor of the tent
and the intelligence of the performing goat. As
a preacher he was not gifted, but in carrying
round the hat he was almost without rival.
When at last such a fall of rain came over
'Sydney water supply catchment area as to ensure
a full reservoir, the supply canal promptly
developed a huge leak, and out of 135 million gal-
lons a day available 50 millions are running to
waste. Wor. e still, the absolute collapse of the
canal is threatened. Apparently it didn’t occur
to the Water Supply Board to put the supply
canal in order during the drought. But the Board
has at its head not an engineer, but a superan-
nuated politician and house-agent pitchforked
into the position by Reid on account of his politi-
cal services and in spite of his,notorious ignorance.
The Jenkins Iscariot Government of S.A.
last Saturday forenoon announced that the hours
of railway workers were to be increased from eight
to daily. The same afternoon it fled in great
disorder and put the hours back (temporarily) to
the old figure. But increased hours or increased
charges or reduced Wages, or probably all three,
are coming right enough. Through the constant
charging of repairs and renewals to loans, the
annual interest burden on each mile of line and on
each ton of goods carried always tends to increase,
and something (besides Jenkins) must happen to
counterbalance it*

“ Via ” : Re hat-pins and hair-pins (“ Pol-
stan,” B. 20/6/’03): At short range a hat-pin is as
dangerous as a bayonet. Witness the case up
north a while back on a crowded “moonlight
excursion down the river” boat, when an irate
female resented some jostling by prodding a
couple of inches of hat-pin into the arm of a by-
stander-result 1100. l poisoning and death. The
wielder of the weapon was never discovered.
Also, did you ever pick up an innocent hair-pin in
tram or ’bus and offer it to its owner ? I did, once ,
but the lady who dropped it scowled as if I had
offered her a corset or a venomous reptile.
“I hope there is distinction of ranks
below, and that the gentlemen will get the best
seats —next the fire.” —Crebillon.
Examination “ howlers ” quoted in Lon-
don Pilot
Who was the English commander in the late battles in
the Soudan?—Cardinal Wolsey.
Explain the phrase “The well of English undefiled”
Cawnpore.
Who wrote “ Oceania ” ?—A Fraud.
Who said “So much the worse for the cow ?—Philip
of Spain.
Probably Philip did say something like that when
“ great Eliza ” declined his offer.

Splendid Soil, Grand Climate, Abundant and Certain Rainfall, Good Harvests,
AND
Best Market in the World for Stock, Fruit, and Gerials.
Imports are still enormous, the Lorial Supply of Produce falling far short of the
demand, thus the Farmer finds a ready market for his produce, and good prices
are obtained. ; r
WEST AUSTRALIAN WHEAT secured the FIRST PRIZE at the
PARIS EXPOSITION, 1899.
Available Land is still plentiful in close proximity to the Railways, and in
Districts where the Annual Rainfall averages from 18 to 30 inches.
Mixed Farming may be carried on in almost any portion of the South-West
Division of the State. TIMBER is plentiful. Fruit Trees and Vines grow
luxuriantly. CLEARING on the whole is easy and cheap.
RAILWAY PASSES are granted to intending Selectors on application to Head
Office, Perth, or the Land Agents on the Goldfields. Full particulars on application.
Schools and Agricultural Halls in almost every centre.
160 acres is granted Free to the head of a family, or any male over 18 years
of age. Free Selection. SURVEYS PAID BY THE STATE. Improvements
required are reduced to a minimum. In addition 1000 acres may be selected under
Residence Conditions within 20 miles;, 1000 acres under Non-Residence, and 1000
acres by direct purchase. In most cases the Purchase Money is TEN SHILLINGS
per acre, extending over TWENTY YEARS.
The Agricultural Bank Loans Sums of from £5O to £lOOO at 5 per cent,
interest per annum. Repayable in from 25 to 30 years on security of fenced
holdings.
GRAZING LEASES may be selected adjoining the Holding of the Applicant.
Area, from 300 to 4000 acres. Purchase Money, 3s. 9d. to 10s. per acre, extending
over THIRTY YEARS.
PASTORAL LEASES—-Magnificent Pastoral Country still available. A
Parcel of Wbol from this State recently topped the Prices obtained at the London
Wool Sales.
FUTURE PROSPERITY is assured by the continued solid development of
the Goldfields and INCREASED OUTPUT.
JARRAH is unequalled as proof against WHITE ANTS and TEREDO.
Excellent for Fencing and Improvements. Railway Facilities through all Agricul-
tural Lands. Write for information to any of the Government LAND AGENTS
at Kalgooi’lie, Coolgardie, Menzies, Cue, and in all the Agricultural Centres, or to
the Head Office, Perth. PLANS AND PUBLICATIONS FREE on application.
LAND GUIDES available to drive Selectors out and show them the land.
THEIR SERVICES ARE PAID FOR BY THE STATE.
All information Gratis. No Droughts.

HERBT. FARMER,


During the 13 years between 1888 and
1901 the British seamen employed in British ships
decreased by 7583, while foreign seamen increased
by 12,184. The boy of the bulldog breed grows
more and more foreign—he is mostly called Hans
Bulldog nowadays.
“S.": It is time some action was taken
to centralise the Federal offices in Melbourne. At
present they are scattered all over the city in
little suites of rooms, and no one ever seems to
know where to go. The reporter “ doing the de-
partments” walks something short of 20,000 miles
a week when he can’t afford tram tickets. If the
offices were concentrated in one of the numerous
big buildings the result would be a considerable
saving in rents, boots and profanity.
Every chance he gets now, Tom Price, the
whole-hearted leader of the little Labor party in
S.A., preaches the good doctrine that the work-
men of that State could do a lot to make their
lives easier if they would club together to do some
of the work which, so far, the professional capi-
talist has always done. The idea makes most
headway among what S.A. knows as “the
blockers ’’ —the men who have made homes on the
working-men’s blocks. These people have already
shaken off one incubus —the man who calls for the
rent every week—and they like it so well that they
are willing to make a present sacrifice in order to
finally shake off another incubus or two. The
Richmond men have lately clubbed together to
put up a meeting-hall of their own ; and they are
using this place to hatch other schemes for pro-
viding one another with cows, and sewing-
machines, and other things.
The proposal that residence in Victoria
should count towards qualifying for a pension in
N.S.W., and vice versa, is again mooted in
Sydney ; but it gets no further advanced. The
representations made by the N.S.W. Government
on this subject over 12 months ago didn’t even
evoke a reply from Iceberg and co. All they
have done is to cut down the Victorian pensions
rate, with a further threatened reduction. So
that if ever the reciprocity is brought about,
residence in Victoria to a citizen domiciled in
Sydney will qualify towards a 10s. pension, while
a N.S.W. man living in Melbourne will get about
4s. or whatever the Irvine-Bent screw can
bring it down to. The Federal Old Age Pension
Law can’t arrive too soon.
VIEW OF HOMESTEAD, NEWCASTLE, WEST AUSTRALIA.
See advt. page 32.]
The Freetrade party in U.S.A., which,
true to the traditions of the cult, is essentially a
Jingo, nigger-oppressing, low-wage, capitalism-
favoring crowd, wishes to run Grover Cleveland
as its candidate next election, but that American
George Reid is not on. There’s not the ghost of
a chance of deposing the Republican high-wage,
high- tariff party in America these elections.
Dr. Creed recently complained to N.S.W.
Upper House that in Sydney
in one large building within the city were stored close
together, in very large quantities, naphtha, benzine,
kerosene, residual oil, carbide of calcium, and baled hay,
and in another naphtha, kapok, and carbide of calcium.
Whereupon he was informed that there was no
law
which in any way prevented such dangerous storage of
explosives and highly inflammable substances, but a Bill
would doubtless be introduced as soon as the course of
public business would admit.
This is interesting and combustible news, and the
remedy seems very far off. The municipal
authorities take no action, and Parliament is so
bogged by the eloquence of house-agents and
Dhoto. men that it can’t do much.
Max Hirsch, the copper joss of Victorian
Freetraders, has been chosen to battle for the
Wimmera seat in the House of Representatives.
Max, who has one of the most wonderful
collections of imaginary statistics on earth, is an
excitable little German who dances like a fury on
hot bricks when he gets going, and should be a
very interesting circus.
A letter from Emmaville (N.S.W.) :
Left here a few days ago ten men for somewhere in
West Africa, right on the Equator. Asked one of the men
why he went, and he said would go anywhere his passage
was paid to, so as to get out of this country. The men did
not know exactly where they were going, and didn’t seem
to care. They were to get £5 per week and an allowance
for tucker, and if they stayed a year their passage-money
would not be deducted. They had to go to Teneriffe and
tranship there. Six of the men are married and have
families. Some of them were over 50, nearer to 60.
Somebody said long ago that a country that lives by
exporting its raw material will, in the end, have to
export its men. And we have got there long ago. A
man said to me the other day that the reason he never
married was that he did not want to produce either
slaves or food for powder. Truly a great country ! lam
credibly informed that these men had no proper
agreement, and leave here on the responsibility of one
man—a storekeeper. There is another lot of young men
to go when the rainy season is over.

War E. : Be “H.M.” and misfit rai-
ment on R.C. shepherds (B., 16/7/’03): Several of
the teaching “ orders ” buy up half-a-dozen misfit
suits for the use of the brethren. When a
“brother” has occasion to go to town, he is
handed out a suit by the “storekeeper,” and it
isn’t always a perfect fit.
This country’s progressiveness and up-to-
date Democracy seems to be but a vain thing and
a bubble, after all. Here is Russia—Russia !—
legalising trade unionism ; more, practically mak-
ing unionism compulsory, and inciting to co-opera-
tion in labor. And the Australian Tory is still
fighting for freedom of contract.
The returned farm-burner still writes to
the papers occasionally. The latest wail (in
Melbourne Age) is from an individual “No. 526 ”
who, it seems, went to the disturbance and
received full and sufficient pay for his services—
the pay agreed upon. Coming back he was taken
ill, and was carefully nursed in Melbourne hos-
pital— not in the gaol hospital, where so many
others have to go. Now he is well except for
noises in the ear, and his complaint is that his
country won’t pension him off for noises in the
ear, but that he has to work for a living. He
states the facts in print so that 'Victoria can rise
up indignantly and see that justice is done him.
“ Boree ” : Be the problem of “D.H.”
(Bulletin, 16/7/’03), there are at least three dis-
tinct stages in the courtship of ’Arry and ’Arriet
and their kind. “ Walkin’-out ” comes first
—the initial nibbling of the fish at the bait.
It is non-committal for both parties, and the
nibbler is then known, I believe, as “me
bloke.” Next comes “ D.H.’s •’ “goin’-with,”
also referred to as “ carryin’ ’er umbreller.” The
bloke is now “me boy,” and he may not “go
with ” any other girl; likewise at dances or picnics
he must be in single-hearted attendance. I don’t
know for certain how “ walkin’-out ” merges into
“ goin’-with,” but they are quite distinct. The
donah has the best of this second stage as she is
by no means so restricted, but has full power to
make her “boy ” jealous. Last stage is, of course,
the “engaged” one, and very few engagements
after the two preliminary canters fail to result in
marriage. The first two stages may last for years;
in fact, a well-conducted girl in service will
strongly resent being asked if she is engaged to a
man unless she has been “ goin’-wich ” him for a
considerable time, nor will she accept a ring from
him until the “ going ” has developed into an en-
gagement, and he is transformed from “ me boy ”
into “me young man.” * r\
“MY LORD."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To a Moe swamp farmer who told him
that the only feasible drainage scheme for that
area was Professor Kernot’s untried one, Tom
Bent remarked, “My Lord, the Professor may be
clever, but he is no business man.”
“ Ho ! varlet undeservin’, go hence on flying heels
And bring me Bent and Irvine and Taverner and Shiels.
I fear me there is mischief ; the Country’s been ignored.*
The minion answered his chief, “It has indeed my
Lord.”
First came proud Irvine, wincing like fretted Arab steed,
But with a mien convincing the Farmer told his need :
“ Bring tillage where waste land is ; have swamps from
seas restored
And lakes where arid sand is." Quoth Irvine, “ Good,
my Lord,
“I’ll give to thee broad acres ; I'll give thee public
works—
This fair land owes its makers the handling of the perks.
A truce to silly scruple ! I’d wield a flaming sword
To treble and quadruple your treasury, my Lord.”
Then entered Shiels the Raven, a dismal sort of bird—
“ On this land thou hast ’graven thy melancholy word.
Now raise me loans and coffers from Moses far abroad ;
What though the City suffers?” Said Shiels, “What
odds, my Lord !
“ I’ll hie me now to Moses and get the wealth galore,
I’ll fly to where his nose is his coffers bending o’er.
The worker of the City, I’ll rob his little hoard,
Till not a blooming bit there’s left of all of it, my Lord."
“ Thou good and faithful Thomas,” the Farmer thus
hailed Bent;
“ What gifts have you to promise, and seal my soul’s
content ?”
“ I’ll give no gifts to you, sir,” fat Thomas fairly roared,
“Till Brighton has its due, sir, I’ll see yer damned, my
Lord.”
Then fell great avalanches, and there was no more sea;
“ You’ve blasted all our chances,” groaned Shiels. “ We’re
up a tree.”
Bent mocked him with a chuckle, “ Another point I’ve
scored,
You chaps may crawl and truckle, but I’ll own no over-
lord.”
T.D.

At Last.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sun rose like a Harper strong,
His gold harp in his hand,
The royal music of his song
Flowed over all the land.
In my brave youth on that great flood
In rapture I was borne;
The joy of life danced in my blood,
And merry was the morn.
Then I became aware of shapes,
Clear-eyed, and fair, and few,
That in a vineyard gathered grapes
Still gleaming with the dew.
That toil to me seemed poor and mean ;
Better, beneath blue skies,
To chase across the meadows green
The golden butterflies.
But while the earth was all a-flower,
And all the sky a-bloom,
A voice was calling hour by hour —
It was the Voice of Doom.
It called me to the Vineyard, too,
It called me all too soon ;
The earth was green, the sky was blue,
And fair the afternoon.
Below me where I sat, rose-crowned,
Beside the singing streams,
Grey Sloth lay like a toothless hound
Too old to hunt in dreams.
The Voice of the Eleventh Hour
Rang through the twilight grey.
I kicked the hound, and flung the flower—
The flower of Love away.
And unto the Great Vineyard then
The Shapes dishevelled streamed ;
Races, and clans, and tribes of men,
And crowns and mitres gleamed.
Beneath the falling night they sought
The Gate with its dim light;
Sinners, and saints, and poets fought—
It was a weird, wild fight.
The good God laughed, and said —“ ’Tis well!
The New Times now begin ;
This is my birthday, Gabriel—
• Let all these poor folk in.”
N.s.w,
VICTOR J. DALEY.
At a Tank in the West.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The long hot day’s tramp is over. The wind and
the sun are going down together, as I and my
companion, with swags swung crosswise over our
shoulders and billy cans in hand, sight the white
roof of a station homestead.
“Evening, boss,” says my mate; “any chance
of getting a bit of tucker to-night ? ”
Lately my mate# has quite given up going
through the formula of asking for work. The
boss eyes us both with a keen glance, and says to
my mate:
“If you can use an axe lean give you a jol
scrub-cutting for sheep.”
“Right, sir, what’s the wages?”
“One pound a week; so go into the kitchen
and get your supper, and I’ll drive you out in the
morning.”
My mate here gives me a meaning look, and
I feebly put in a word for myself. The boss,
however, after looking at my sun-blistered face
and hands, evidently sums me up for what I am,
and sharply tells me to come into the store for a
pint of flour. Just as I am turning ruefully away
he calls out:
“Here, young fellow, have you ever done any
bush work ? ”
“No, sir, except tramping about the bush with
my swag and begging for tucker,” I say, some-
what bitterly.
“Well, I’ll give you a job pumping at 15s. a
week.”
I haven’t the faintest idea what sort of a job
pumping means, but joyfully accept the offer.
Next morning I am taken down to a large exca-
vation called a tank, the water in which is rapidly
diminishing under the combined influences of the
blazing sun and the thirst of about 8000 sheep,
besides horses, cattle, birds, etc. As its sides
are getting very boggy a fence has been put
around it. A pump has been erected over a well
sunk at the side, while close by is about 100 yards
of iron troughing. My work is to keep this con-
stantly full of water. It takes me all I know to
do this at times, but the whole thing is very
interesting to my inexperience, and besides I now
get food without having to beg for it.
I arrive very early in the morning, just as the
faint dawn in the east begins to pale the sky.
I generally find a few emaciated rabbits, who, at
my- approach, struggle feebly away from the
brink of the water. Otherwise it is as still as
deatli, and the surface of the water unruffled by a
breath of wind. I take the handle of my pump
and begin, for a certain amount of water has
always been drunk by straggling stock during the
night.
As the dawn brightens red in the east the first
signs of life begin, generally by the screech of a
galah, and in a few minutes the air is alive with
them, uttering their discordant notes. There are
thousands of them. Also, there come whole
droves of blue and green parrots, crows, magpies,
and crested doves, besides many other birds.
Then the red sun shows up, streaming into a
cloudless brazen sky, and begins its day’s scorch.
Across the swamp may be seen some emus ap-
proaching, very shyly, for a drink, but the clank
of the water-lift keeps them for a time from
coming nearer. Then a couple of kangaroos hop
awkwardly, and with long pauses, up to the
water. Thirst makes all alike tame; but they
keep at the side opposite from me. The fence is
not very high, but the drought ’roos have not got
much spring in them, so they prefer to squeeze in
under the lower wire where the ground is a li:tle
unequal. They have a sort of suspicion of the
troughs. Now my real work begins as the sheep
:t re coming. They are generally led by some ancient
u ve. And what a row they make coming along. It
reminds me of the shouting voices of a large crowd
at a football match. I have to work my very
hardest to keep them going, and the sweat pours
off me like water. The air is thick with a hot,
pungent dust, and the sun so melting that I doubt
whether even a man from Hay or Boohgal could
stand it. This Australia seems to be one huge
By about 8 o’clock the sheep slacken, and as
they draw off they stroll towards the shade of
some box trees, and do not move all day until late
in the afternoon, except to now and then take a
lick at some lumps of the queerest-looking salt I
ever saw. Why th©y do this I don t know, for
they seem thirsty enough without it. I draw
under the shade of a box tree, light a small fire,
boil a quart pot, and have my breakfast sitting on
the ground until the ants make things too lively,
and then I go back to my pump and have a
smoke. .
I have now some leisure to watch life in the far
western desert. First the crack of a stockwhip
and a cloud of dust announce that something else
is coming to assuage that unquenchable thir.-t
which seems to lay hold of every living thing out
here. This turns out to be the working horsr s
driven by “ Billy-the-Boy.” These drink until
you would tbink that they must burst. I hen I
hear the rumble of wheels and the water-cart that
supplies the station is backed under my pump,
and the man sings out to me to fire away. As
soon as it is filled away it goes, old “ Bowler,” the
draught horse, digging his toes into the ground
with his heavy pull of about a ton of water. Next
come the miilcing cows—three in number—and
their calves, again driven by “Billy-the-Boy.”
These also seem to fill themselves right up with
water until it is a wonder that it does not run out
of their ears ; then they are taken to where the
mulga cutters are at work for the sheep, about
two miles off.
The water-cart comes down again for refilling,
this time its destination being the scrub-cutters’
camp, and they tell me that when the men see it
coming thoy rush it like sheep to fill their water-
bags. The heat now is so intense that even if
any animals want a drink they haven’t pluck enough
to go into the sun to get it, so until 5 o’clock I do
little but stand and endure. Then the sheep rush
me again and draw off towards the fallen scrub.
At last the sun disappears in the red, hazy, dusty
west; but I have yet another lot of visitors in the
shape of some half-wild station cattle and some
working bullocks stalking alone in nearly single
file to the water. Lastly comes an old shep-
herd who is looking after a mob of about
150 rams in an adjoining paddock. These have
their drink, and the shepherd puts them into a
little yard, close by, after loudly cursing his dog
for lying in the water instead of coming to help.
In the east, the red and solemn moon is now
rising, and, as I look in the opposite direction to
the south-west, I remember that I have been told
that there isn’t a drop of water for more than 80
miles that way—a somewhat uncanny thought.
The shepherd and I now go to the station. He
seems to be in a sullen, sour mood, and remarks
that, even if the ruddy grass ever did grow again
in the ruddy country, it would be little good now,
as only the oldest stock (and very ruddy few were
left) would know that it was grass. The rest
wouldn’t understand what it was, and would die
of hunger. As we approach the men’s quarters, I
hear the strains of a concertina coming from the
wood-heap outside. The cook is a white man,
and has kept something warm for the two late
comers. Later, I throw myself down on a
blanket, outside, on account of the heat within,
with a sheepskin for mattress, and, in spite of
mosquitos, am soon asleep.
N.s.w.
BILRY.
’Twixt Man and God.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Coffee was the most bigoted unbeliever I ever
met. No rabid religionist could “glorify God”
with more enthusiasm than Coffee displayed in
denying Him ; no evangelist could infuse greater
zeal into the work of preaching the Gospel than
Coffee threw into his gratuitous task of publishing
the mistakes of Moses, and betraying the errors
of the Apostles. The farmers and settlers for
miles around knew him as a rabid Atheist and an
untiring lay-preacher of the gospel of ungodliness.
Sinners are notorious believers, and several sinners
in the district had endeavored to convince Coffee
of the error of his ways with the biting logic of a
straight left, but Coffee was strong and fierce and
fought like the Devil whose cause he advocated,
and that line of argument was soon abandoned by
the champions of orthodoxy.
Coffee made several converts, but his most
active proselyte was Mrs. Coffee, a pale, timid,
hard-working little woman, who was never
betrayed into a display of feeling of any kind
until Tom got going on one of his favorite argu-
ments ; and then, flushed and excited, she backed
him up with half-hysterical ejaculations, such as
over-wrought women utter at revival meetings.
It was a Sunday afternoon ;■ two or three men
had chanced round, and Tom Coffee was tearing
the story of Noah’s Ark to pieces in the spirit of
this age of reason for their instruction and enter-
tainment, and Mrs. Coffee stood by, looking pale
and nervous, as she usually did while her husband
was smashing religions and abolishing gods, but
supporting him excitedly, as if anxious to please.
“Talk about puttin’ two of all the animals
inter the ark,” said Tom, “why, yer couldn’t do
it. You couldn’t put two of all the kinds of
insects an’ things into a ship the size what Noah
built, let alone the elephants an’ rhinoceroses, an’
whales, an’ sharks, an’ horses, an’ camels, an’ sea
cows, an’ what not.”
“Oh, it’s all rubbish—just rubbish ! ” said Mrs.
Coffee eagerly, with the air of a woman doing
desperate deeds.
“You couldn’t put’em in. I defy youse to do
it,” Tom continued, “Then what o’them there
mastodons, an’ mammoths, an’ magatheriums, an’
diprotodons, an’ dinosaurs, an’ dinocerases, an’
dinotheriums, an’ ichthyosauruses, an’ all the
other mighty reptiles an’ birds what’s now extinct
—what of them, I’d like to know ? ”
The men murmured among themselves. Coffee
was certainly very learned. The big words over-
whelmed them.
“Of course no one believes about Noah’s ark
now-a-days,” said Mrs. Coffee feverishly. “ It’s
all a lot o’ lies ! ” She looked anxiously at Tom
as if expecting a word of approval.
“Fact is,” said Coffee, “none o’ them Bible
yarns will bear the light o’ modern knowledge. It
shrivels ’em up. Religion is just ignorance writ
another way. The searchlight o’ science can’t find
a god anywhere.”
“ They’re dead ! ” cried Mrs. Coffee.
“They never lived,” said Tom.
“That’s true!” A red spot was burning in
Mrs. Coffee’s pale cheeks, her hands were
trembling painfully. “That’s true,” she re-
peated ; “ there never was any God ! ”
Tom continued his attack for over half-an-hour,
and his wife echoed his opinions with fiery zeal,
but at the end of that time Hine voted it dry
work and proposed a walk to the Travellers’ Best
and drinks round. The idea was popular, and the
men moved off. At the slip-panels Eastby re-
membered the whip he had left on the table in
Coffee’s kitchen and returned. As he approached
the house he was surprised to hear Mrs. Coffee’s
voice, intense with profound emotion, broken
with sobs, crying a pitiful appeal. At the door he
stopped, thunderstruck. The woman was on her
knees at the sofa, praying passionately, torn with
an agony of contrition.
Eastby took up his whip and stole quietly away.

“J.F.D.”: There was a religious cere-
mony at Manly a few Sundays back, and crowds
journeyed down, a special time-table being
arranged to carry them. A follower of Dill
Macky explained it to me: “The Cardinal,” he
explained, “gets the show up, and 5000 extra
travel down ; at 6d. each that is £125 extra to the
ferry company. Now, I ask you how much the
Cardinal gets out of that ? ” I was cornered, but
a little Irishman, who had craned his neck to
listen, promptly made a line with the toe of his
boot and struck out viciously. Dill Macky’s
disciple was game, and the astonished crowd was
knocked about like ninepins. Two water police
managed to lasso them at last, and carried them
off in triumph, the v. ill Mackyite roaring out his
charge about the Cardinal being in league with
the ferry company. “ Gets up a procession down
there once a month,” he said ; “ I know him.

Voices in the Mallee.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the world was full of tears,
Blackened love and coldest fears;
Where the earth lay hot and red,
There I flung me down and said :
“Blinding sand and mallee sere,
Must I live and die out here ?
Bitter Bush so bare and brown,
Oh, I weary for the town ! ”
Then a whisper faint and far
Swept the mournful grey belar,
Stirred the mottled leopard-wood
In the mallee solitude;
Breathed wind-voices strange and slow—
Came then answer sweet and low :
“ Sun and sand and glinting star,
Glimpse of moonlit pines afar,
“ Mallee roofs that stars peep through—
What could cities do for you ?
Cheeks of Bronze and Eyes of Brown ?
You would pine and die in town,
“Densest scrub and sky-bound plain,
Thundering hoof and flying mane,
Maddened horse and jay’s sweet call—
Are you weary of it all ?
“ Absent step and absent kiss,
Blame the grey Bush not for this ;
You have known her song and laughter,
And the woe that followed after.
“ Seen the shining skies a-spread,
Trees of gold and scrub bloom red,
Then—the gruesome eagle feast,
Withering bough and moaning beast.
“She has whispered secret things
Hidden from the hearts of kings,
Logic strange and learning fair ;
Would it profit you in there ?
“ You have drunk of bitter waters,
Framed to mourn are Earth’s frail daughters.
Cities could not ease this sorrow,
God will right all things to-morrow.
“Listen, Bush child, born and bred,
In the days when you are dead,
You will sleep where boughs droop down
Deeper, sounder than in town.
Leave the crowded street alone,
Let the grey Bush keep her own. ”
N.S.W.
MOULAMEIN.
Students of the population question should
be interested to learn of the action of a Melbourne
sassiety woman who, finding herself about to
become a patriot, burst into a wild rage ; and, in
the presence of a select assemblage of her female
acquaintances, cast into the drawing-room fire a
large quantity of useless articles “ warranted not
to fail.”
As Told by the Doctor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The annual heat-wave had arrived at Gannon’s.
The meteorological report for the current week,
as recorded by the steward of Gannon’s club,
from observation of the thermometer in the
smoking-room taken daily at 3 p.m., had been as
follows:
Monday, January 18th, 112°.
Tuesday, January 19th, 114°.
Wednesday, January 20th, 117°.
At midnight of Wednesday, Dr. Maurice
O’Carroll, resident medical officer, and hospital
surgeon of the field, having mustered sufficient
energy to rise from the long cane-chair which he
had inhabited since eleven o’clock, made a careful
inspection of the hard-worked instrument and
reported a reading of ninety-eight. “And what
kapes us from a drink ? Is ut poverty or what,
Steward ? ”
“ Worst night for years,” grunted the Warden,
gently swirling his half-finished whisky and soda.
“Don’t know, though,” he added thoughtfully,
we had a snorter or two about this time a few
years ago. Remember the night poor Haydon of’
the bank shot himself ? ”
“ Wasn’t here,’’ said Sayer; “in Perth then,
wallovving among the flesh-pots. Queer business,
that of Haydon’s. What were the rights of it,
•Dews-• You knew him better than anyone.”
The doctor pulled at his pipe in gloomy silence
for some moments. _ “If you mean how did
Haydon come by his death,” he said at length,
it was from mishandling a Smith and Wesson
thirty-eight-bore revolver, which was loaded.
Death by Misadventure’ was the verdict of
twelve good men ami true—some of them sober.
I swore them in myself—l was coroner. If you
wish to know the primary cause of the accident,
twas the d d drunken carelessness of one
Maurice O’Carroll, M.R.C.S. Boys, I could have
stopped it with a word that night, and I left it
over till the morning, because three wasters in
this room wanted a fourth at poker. If I’d only
known !
Twas a beast of a night. I mind it
well. As hot as this, with the addition of a
wind straight into these windows from the
camel camp. You know what that’s like when
Mutta Singh’s string is in town, as it was just
then. I’d been out at the * Straight Dinkum ’
mine, mendin a chap that had rolled down the
underlay a matter of fifty yards or so. Hurted,
he was. He’s right now, barrin’ his left leg’s
short. I had an accident coming home. I was
driving the bay horse and Norah Creina tandem,
and we met a donkey team carting chaff for Cobb.
Norali’s used to camels, but donkeys got on her
nerves. Moreover, the jackass in the shafts
lifted his voice in song. Norah detached herself
from the cart and made a record home to the
stable. Then the big horse knocked up crossing
the saltpan, and ’twas late when I got in. I
drifted over here about eleven, for a drink.
There were half-a-dozen fellows sprawled about
the room, taking it in turns to curse the weather.
“Haydon was lying on the lounge, there in the
shadow. .At first I thought he was asleep. He’d
been having a bad time for months, had poor
Jack. Been scrip-gambling and done no good at
it. I was the only one here who knew that (and
I didn’t know how bad it was), but it was talked
of in Kalgoorlie and maybe in Perth by that time.
He said nothing to ine about trouble, for all we
were pals, but by signs and tokens I knew the
heart was eaten out of him with worry. There
was a girl waiting for him somewhere in Victoria.
He did be boring the soul out of me about her at
one time, when we were alone in the bank, or in
my humpy, smoking of a night, but latterly he’d
dropped mention of her.
“Five minutes after I came in a buggy drove
round to the back, and presently in comes Roscoe
of the All’s Well mine. Just back from Perth,
with three inches of red dust on him and a thirst
that would shame a camel. Full of reasons why
Limber didn’t beat Australian for the Cup, and
nothing else in the way of news to be got out of him
till he’d demonstrated them. You know Roscoe ?
Presently he catches sight of Haydon. ‘That
Jack Haydon over there?’ says he. ‘l’ve tidings
for you, Jack,’ he says. ‘ That old Presbyterian
Moderator of an Inspector of yours is in Kanowna
and ’ll be here Sunday’s coach.’
“Jack half sat up, then dropped back again.
‘What, old Gillies?’ says he. ‘ That same holy
man,’ says Roscoe, ‘lsn’t due till March,’ says
Jack. ‘ Due or not due, the beast’s coming,’ says
Roscoe; ‘ told me so night before last.’ ‘ D n
him ! ’ says Jack, softly.
“ Then Roscoe makes' for the bath-room to
sluice the dust off himself, and in a minute or two
Jack gets up, stretches himself, and says he has
letters to write and must go, which he did at
once. I mind he turned at the door as if he were
coming back and hesitated for a second. ‘ Good
night, all you fellows,’ he says. ‘Doc., half a
minute. 1 went out with him and he turned
into the office. ‘Doc.,’ says he, ‘I can’t sleep
these three nights, and I’m bad company for my-
self. Fix me up a bolus.’ ‘Bolus be d d,
says I—‘have a drink and another before you
turn in. ’Tis only the weather.’ ‘’Tis more than
the weather,’ he says, with a laugh I didn't like.
‘ls anything wrong, Jack ? ’ says I. ‘Tell me,
boy, I could help, maybe.’ ‘Nothing you can
help, old man, thanks all the same,’ he says—
‘let’s have the drink.’ We had it standing at the
bar, and he took a second-mate’s nip, which I
noticed, because it wasn’t like him. He wa3
never a big-nip man. Then he shook hands with
me and strolled off towards the bank.
“‘The boy’s in trouble,’ thinks I. ‘l’ll call
round in the morning and have it out with him,’
and I came back in here, where Roscoe, in his
pyjamas, had the floor again.
“ ‘ Does anyone hold Last Laps ? ’ says he. ‘ I
know a man has 500,’ says I. ‘The devil you do !
Who’s the man ? ’ ‘ Jack Haydon,’ says I; ‘ and
’tis sick at heart with paying calls he is.’ * He’ll
pay no more,’ says Roscoe. ‘They were selling
at three quid night before last. Expect it’s four
now.’
• l j^r> vas J umping" with joy for Jack’s sake, and
said I d go up to the bank and tell him at once,
but three of them were sitting down for an hour’s
Poker, and they bothered me to make a fourth.
Id been winning for several nights before, and
couldn’t refuse, but I told myself I’d take the
good news round to Jack first thing in the
morning.
, “The hour’s poker lasted till 2 o’clock, and
twas past 8 next morning when I woke. I was
at the bank before 9, and found young Hughes,
the teller, at his breakfast. ‘Where’s Haydon?’
says I. ‘Not up yet,’ he says; ‘heard him
moving about his room till all hours. Suppose
he’s tired.’ ‘l’ve news will waken him,’ says I,
and knocked at his door. No answer. Knocked
louder. Then tried the handle, and it was un-
locked. No one in the room—bed not been slept
in.
“ ‘That’s queer,’ says Hughes, looking scared.
11 heard him moving about up till about one, when
I fell asleep.’ I took a mental inventory. He
had a writing-table in the room and a big
letter lay on the blotting-pad. It was addressed :
‘John Gillies, Esq.’ My heart went into my
throat. The bank revolver lay on the dressing-
table. His gun stood in one corner and a Win-
chester in another. ‘ These all the fire-arms ?’ I
asked. ‘ No, ’ said Hughes, trembling like a leaf;
‘he’d a Smith and Wesson of his own ; kept it
here,’ pulling open the table drawer. ‘And it’s
gone, ’he moaned. ‘My God, it’s gone ! ’
“ There was a door on to the back verandah,
and just then I noticed it was ajar. ‘He went
out this way,’ I says. We both stepped out on to
the verandah and looked up the hill at the back.
A couple of crows rose up and hung cawing above
the salmon-gums. We looked into each other’s
white faces and started to run, without a word.
He was there.
“ We spent most of that day in the pursuit of
Crime, young Hughes and I. I closed on old
Gillies’s letter to begin with. It was a business-
like statement of the shortage, and enclosed the
Last Lap scrip. ‘lt might be worth something
some day, ’ wrote poor Jack. How I cursed my-
self ! However, cursing isn’t business. He’d en-
closed a blank signed transfer with the scrip, by
great good luck, and I thought Maurice O’Carroil
a good name to fill in. Then I sent a wire to
Kalgoorlie, and before night had sold the lot at
three-ten. By Saturday night I had enough of
the money to enable Hughes to straighten poor
Jack’s books to a penny. Old Gillies couldn’t find
a nail to hang a theory on.
“ The balance of the money we sent to Haydon’s
mother. I wrote a full account of the accident to
her, and also to the girl—God forgive me ! I had
answers from them both that broke my heart.
The jury ? That was easy. Sure, I was the
coroner.”
H.F.
“L.W.”: Just now the Sydney daily
press is much fogged as to why the Dagoes
control the fruit and fish industries. Well, it’s
simple enough. I had occasion to enter a fruit
shop between 10.50 and 11 p.m. recently. Next
morning I passed that shop at 5.30 a.m., no matter
why; and the same unfortunate Dago lad who
had sold me the tomatoes the night before was
polishing up the apples in the morning; and he is
visible at any hour of the day, too. The weary
Greek youth who brings you a fish breakfast at
9 a.m. will be found scrubbing and sweeping at
1.30 a.m. the following morning, having worked
right through without a break—and all for his
keep, his clothes, and about <£s a year pocket
money. _ Cheap labor, or rather plain blank
slavery, is the reason why the Dago has been able
to drive the Britisher out of the pear and prawn
trades. Apparently it is impossible to enforce
any law about hours and wages in the case, for
the small slave is so terrorised, or so influenced in
some way, that he will swear, if necessary, that
he only works eight hours a day and gets £3 a
week. In the end it may be necessary to enact
that no person under the age of 21 shall be
employed in the shop of any Dago whatever.

off his nose to spite his face ” has a fine modern repre-

Tattersall’s sweeps “in the interests of moiality.

j in connection with Tatts. sweeps was 6 P
sentin*' a value of £62,184. Then came the postal ukase,
and next year the number dropped to 14,589, valued at

not on Tatt’s. at all, for agencies sprang up all over the

coin per medium of bank draft. The sweeps fill up as

securities and asking Cohen and Co.* London, how
much they will advance on that ? *
