Apropos the attitude of the M.L. Rugby Union
with respect to the English by-law which provides that
the referee alone has power to report a player who starts
out to slay an opponent. The Otago Union disqualified
a player for rough play on the report of some unauthor-
ised person. The player appealed, and the M.L. Union
ordered the Otago Union to remove the disqualification,
and intimated that if the Otago Union did not do it, and
do it quick, no Otago players would be included in the
team to visit Australia. The Otago Union removed the
disqualification accordingly. So far there is no mention
of the M.L. Union taking steps to get the English rule
amended. The anxiety of Otago to be represented as
much as possible in the M.L. team was shown by an extra-
ordinary incident One of the players selected to go
to Australia, struck another player on the last playing
day prior to his leaving to join the team. The referee
ordered him off the field, and reported him to the Otago
Union. The team in which the man was playing at once
left the field, and then Dunedin called on the Otago
Union in a body, and hinted that it would be lynched
if it disqualified him, and the agitated Union dis-
missed the charge, and looked severely at the re-
feree. He is an old representative player, and in his
various tours with Otago teams, has been thrice ordered
off the field for striking an opponent, and was suspended
for eighteen months on one of these occasions.
Association football is going ahead in Maori-
land. In Christchurch there has just been formed a
branch of the M.L. Association, so that all the cities are
equipped now. There is a feeling in Christchurch that
the time has come for something with less blood and
fractures.

“Ku-Klux”: Between South Yarra and Rich-
mond, close to the railway-line, is a small pocket-hand-
kerchief enclosure once known, I believe, as a pony race-
course. An old campaigner tells me this concerning it:
“ You talk about the crookedness of the ponies now, but
the game is nothing to what it was when the South Yarra
ponies ran on that course. I remember once the favorite
fell down, and the rest waited for him, and after the jock
had remounted he won. Another time the wrong horse
won, so the stewards declared it no race and it was run
over again, and again the favorite was beaten. So they
simply disqualified the first horse for interference—he
had Jed ah the way—and the favorite got the race. I
remember on one occasion a horse was finishing badly
and it looked as if the second one would overhaul him, so
his backers pulled up the winning-post bodily and ran to
meet him. He passed it first. You can call me a liar if
you like, but he got the race.”

Last year, when a rumpus was made by the
goody goody members of the M.L. House of Reps, about

House gently smoothed their ruffled hair by forbidding

there as usual, and when an agitated Rep. asked the why
and wherefore in a very angry tone of voice he was told
that the turfy people weren’t intruding oft their dom'd"
but on that of the Legislative Council, whose Speaker had
granted them permission ; whereupon the pious section
of the House sat back and sighed wearily.

Disqualification “during pleasure” may mean
very little or a big load in a waggon. Auckland (M.L )
R.O. committee decid d, before closing the season, to
state what it meant by the term when it passed
certain mokes and individuals out. Two owners, who
labored under the fond delusion that they were only
under a cloud temporarily, were passed out for life, and
in the remaining cases the penalty ranged frdm two years
to 12 months, to date from day of original disqualifica-
tion.

Such is Life, the new Australian book just published by The
Bulletin, is handsomely bound in cloth ; 300 pages, price
4s. 6 d. Originality is a characteristic of Such is Life ; and this
is attained, to begin with, in the simplicity of the scheme.
SUCH
Tom Collins can condense when he chooses—but that is not
always. However, as his antithesis to brevity is discursiveness,
rather than prolixity, he never drags. There is interest, if not
relevancy, in every sentence. The style is attractive—lucid,
IS
vigorous, half-scholarly, half-slangy, and occasionally graceful;
carrying the frank personality of the writer through every
paragraph.
Apart from the historical value of the book, as seizing and
LIFE!
fixing pictures of Riverina life necessarily transitory, the
portraiture deserves notice. Here the author is unconsciously
at home. Rarely is a character revealed by verbal description;
-each speaks for himself, and a clean-cut individuality, consistent
by
in its human inconsistency, is thrown on the page. Isocratic
rather than democratic in tone, these sketches present each
individual—squatter or sundowner —as a document of special
interest, yet bearing the common seal. Hence the silhouette
TOM
of any passer-by shows sharp and distinct as the finished por-
trait of a familiar.
Beyond all other Australian writers, Tom Collins is a roaster
of idiom. There is no confusion of patois, nor exaggeration of
COLLINS
grammatical solecism, in his dialogue. As in actual life, the
education of each speaker is denoted by his phraseology ; the
dialect of each European bespeaks his native locality ; and,
above all, the language of the most unbookish bushman never
degenerates into “coster.”
